=== Stripped Clozes Preview (No Changes in Anki) ===

  

    Theorem 1.1.1. There is no {{c5::{{c1::rational}}}} number whose {{c4::{{c3::square}}}} is {{c4::{{c2::2.}}}}

============================================================

  

    Theorem 1.1.1. There is no rational number whose square is 2.<br><br>Proof:<br><br>And so assume, for contradiction, that there exist integers \(p\) and \(q\) satisfying<br><br><ul><li>{{c1::\(\left(\frac{p}{q}\right)^2\)}} = 2<br></li></ul><br>We may also assume that \(p\) and \(q\) have {{c2::no common factor}}, because, {{c2::if they had one}}, we could simply {{c2::cancel it out and rewrite the fraction in lowest terms}}. Now, equation (1) implies<br><br><ul><li>{{c3::\(p^2\)}} =&nbsp;{{c4::\(2 q^2\)}}<br></li></ul><br>From this, we can see that the integer \(p^{2}\) is an {{c5::even number (it is divisible by 2 )}}, and hence \(p\) must be {{c5::even}} as well because the square of an {{c5::odd}} number is {{c5::odd}}. This allows us to write \(p\) = {{c5::\(2 r\)}}, where \(r\) is also an integer. If we substitute \(2 r\) for \(p\) in equation (2), then a little algebra yields the relationship<br><br><ul><li>{{c5::\(2 r^{2}\)}} = {{c5::\(q^{2}\)}}</li></ul><br>But now the absurdity is at hand. This last equation implies that \(q^{2}\) is {{c5::even}}, and hence \(q\) must also be {{c5::even}}. Thus, we have shown that \(p\) and \(q\) are both {{c5::even}}&nbsp; when they were originally assumed to have no common factor.&nbsp;<br>

============================================================

  

    Theorem 1.1.1. There is no rational number whose square is 2.<br><br>Proof:<br><br>And so assume, for contradiction, that there exist integers \(p\) and \(q\) satisfying<br><br>{{c1::\(\left(\frac{p}{q}\right)^2\)}} = 2<br><br>We may also assume that \(p\) and \(q\) have {{c2::no common factor}}, because, {{c2::if they had one}}, we could simply {{c2::cancel it out and rewrite the fraction in lowest terms}}. Now, equation (1) implies<br><br>{{c3::\(p^2\)}} =&nbsp;{{c4::\(2 q^2\)}}<br><br>From this, we can see that the integer \(p^{2}\) is an {{c5::even number (it is divisible by 2 )}}, and hence \(p\) must be {{c5::even}} as well because the square of an {{c5::odd}} number is {{c5::odd}}. This allows us to write \(p\) = {{c5::\(2 r\)}}, where \(r\) is also an integer. If we substitute \(2 r\) for \(p\) in equation (2), then a little algebra yields the relationship<br><br>{{c5::\(2 r^{2}\)}} = {{c5::\(q^{2}\)}}<br>But now the absurdity is at hand. This last equation implies that \(q^{2}\) is {{c5::even}}, and hence \(q\) must also be {{c5::even}}. Thus, we have shown that \(p\) and \(q\) are both {{c5::even}}&nbsp; when they were originally assumed to have no common factor.&nbsp;

============================================================

  

    Why did the proof of irrationaliyt of the quare root challenge the mathematical notions of the greeks?<br><ul><li>They assumed numbers were only the {{c1::rational}} numbers</li><li>However, the square root of two existed as a {{c2::geometric length}} for the {{c5::{{c3::diagonal of a square}}}} and was {{c1::irrational}}</li><li>Figure:</li><li>{{c5::{{c4::<img src="paste-d4d930fe7d60937f0aab2a36f1e3b92f76739711.jpg">}}}}<br></li></ul>

============================================================

  

    If we restrict our attention to the natural numbers \(\mathbf{N}\), then we can perform {{c3::addition}} perfectly well, but we must extend our system to the integers<br><br><ul><li>{{c2::\(\mathbf{Z}\)}} = {{c1::\(\{\ldots,-3,-2,-1,0,1,2,3, \ldots\}\)}}</li></ul>if we want to have an {{c4::additive identity (zero)}} and the {{c5::additive inverses}} necessary to define {{c5::subtraction}}.<br>

============================================================

  

    The next issue after addtion (what the integers get us) is {{c1::multiplication}} and {{c2::division}}. The number {{c3::1}} acts as the {{c1::multiplicative}} {{c3::identity}}, but in order to define {{c2::division}} we need to have {{c1::multiplicative}} {{c4::inverses}}. Thus, we extend our system again to the rational numbers<br><br><ul><li>\(\mathbf{Q}\) = {{c5::<ul><li>\(\left\{\text { all fractions } \frac{p}{q} \text { where } p \text { and } q \text { are integers with } q \neq 0\right\}\)</li></ul>}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Taken together, the properties of Q:<br><ul><li>Closed under {{c1::addtion}}</li><li>{{c1::Addivite}} {{c2::identiy}}</li><li>{{c1::Addivitve}} {{c3::inverse}}</li><li>Closed under {{c4::multiplication}}</li><li>{{c4::Multiplicative}} {{c2::identity}}</li><li>{{c4::Multiplicative}} {{c3::inverse}}</li></ul><div>make Q into a {{c5::field}}</div>

============================================================

  

    The set \(\mathbf{Q}\) also has a {{c5::{{c1::natural}}}} {{c4::{{c2::order}}}} defined on it. Given any two rational numbers \(r\) and \(s\), exactly one of the following is true:<br><br><ul><li>\(r\) {{c4::{{c3::&lt;}}}}&nbsp;\(s\)<br></li><li>\(r\) {{c4::{{c3::=}}}}&nbsp;\(s\)<br></li><li>\(r\)&nbsp;{{c4::{{c3::\(&gt;\)}}}}&nbsp;\(s\)<br></li></ul>

============================================================

  

    The set \(\mathbf{Q}\) also has a natural order defined on it. Given any two rational numbers \(r\) and \(s\), exactly one of the following is true:<br><br>\[<br>r&lt;s, \quad r=s, \quad \text { or } \quad r&gt;s \text {. }<br>\]<br><br><ul><li>This ordering is {{c1::transitive}} in the sense that if {{c2::\(r&lt;s\)}} and {{c2::\(s&lt;t\)}}, then {{c2::\(r&lt;t\)}}<br></li><li>So we are conveniently led to a mental picture of the rational numbers as being laid out {{c3::from left to right along a number line}}.&nbsp;</li><li>Unlike \(\mathbf{Z}\), there are no {{c4::intervals of empty space}}.&nbsp;</li><ul><li>Given any two rational numbers \(r&lt;s\), the rational number {{c5::\((r+s) / 2\)}} sits {{c5::halfway in between}}, implying that the rational numbers are {{c5::densely nestled together.}}</li></ul></ul><br>

============================================================

  

    Using only rational numbers, it is possible to approximate \(\sqrt{2}\) quite wel as can be seen in the figure:<br><ul><li>{{c1::<img src="paste-478bdc81f23e35bfd74dc9d56202ad66dd69458d.jpg">}}<br></li><li>For instance, \(1.414^{2}=1.999396\).&nbsp;</li><li>By adding more decimal places to our approximation, we can get even closer to a value for \(\sqrt{2}\),</li><li>Even so, we are now well aware that there is a "{{c2::hole}}" in the {{c2::rational number}} line where {{c3::\(\sqrt{2}\) ought to be.&nbsp;}}</li><li>Returning to the dilemma of the ancient Greek mathematicians, if we want {{c4::every length along the number line}} to correspond to {{c5::an actual number}}, then another extension to our number system is in order. Thus, to the chain \(\mathbf{N} \subseteq \mathbf{Z} \subseteq \mathbf{Q}\) we append the {{c5::real}} numbers {{c5::\(\mathbf{R}\).}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Intuitively speaking, a {{c1::set}} is {{c2::any collection of objects}}. These {{c2::objects}} are referred to as the {{c3::elements}} of the {{c1::set}}. For our purposes, the {{c1::sets}} in question will most often be {{c1::sets}} of {{c4::real numbers}}, although we will also encounter {{c1::sets}} of {{c5::functions}} and, on a few occasions, {{c1::sets}} whose {{c3::elements}} are {{c1::other sets.}}

============================================================

  

    Given a set \(A\), we write {{c1::\(x \in A\)}}&nbsp;if \(x\)(whatever it may be) is {{c2::an element of \(A\)}}. If \(x\) is {{c5::{{c3::not an element of \(A\)}}}}, then we write {{c5::{{c4::\(x \notin A\)}}}}

============================================================

  

    Given two sets \(A\) and \(B\), the {{c2::union}} is written {{c1::\(A \cup B\)}} and is defined by asserting that<br><br>{{c3::\[<br>x \in A \cup B \text { provided that } x \in A \text { or } x \in B \text { (or potentially both). }<br>\]}}<br><br>The {{c4::intersection}} {{c1::\(A \cap B\)}} is the set defined by the rule<br><br>{{c5::\[<br>x \in A \cap B \text { provided } x \in A \text { and } x \in B \text {. }<br>\]}}

============================================================

  

    (iii) Sometimes it is more efficient to provide a kind of rule or algorithm for determining the elements of a set. As an example, let<br><ul><li>\(S\) = {{c1::\(\left\{r \in \mathbf{Q}: r^{2}&lt;2\right\}\)}}<br></li></ul><br>Read aloud, the definition of \(S\) says, "Let \(S\) be the set of {{c2::all}} {{c3::rational numbers}} whose {{c4::squares}} are {{c5::less than 2}}."&nbsp;<br>

============================================================

  

    The set {{c1::\(\emptyset\)}} is called {{c2::the empty set}} and is understood to be the set that {{c3::contains no elements}}. An equivalent statement would be to say that two sets whose {{c5::intersection}} is&nbsp; {{c1::\(\emptyset\)}}&nbsp; are {{c4::disjoint}}.

============================================================

  

    <ul><li>The {{c1::inclusion}} relationship {{c2::\(A \subseteq B\)}} or {{c2::\(B \supseteq A\)}} is used to indicate that {{c3::every element of \(A\)}} is {{c4::also an element of \(B\)}}.&nbsp;<br></li><li>In this case, we say \(A\) is a {{c5::subset}} of \(B\), or \(B\) {{c5::contains}} \(A\). To assert that \(A=B\) means that {{c2::\(A \subseteq B\)}} {{c5::and}} {{c2::\(B \subseteq A\)}}.&nbsp;</li><li>Put another way, \(A\) and \(B\) have {{c5::exactly the same elements.}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Example 1.2.2. Let<br><br>\[<br>\begin{aligned}<br>&amp; A_{1}=\mathbf{N}=\{1,2,3, \ldots\}, \\<br>&amp; A_{2}=\{2,3,4, \ldots\}, \\<br>&amp; A_{3}=\{3,4,5, \ldots\},<br>\end{aligned}<br>\]<br><br>and, in general, for each \(n \in \mathbf{N}\), define the set<br><br>\[<br>A_{n}=\{n, n+1, n+2, \ldots\} .<br>\]<br><br>The result is a nested chain of sets<br><br>\[<br>A_{1} \supseteq A_{2} \supseteq A_{3} \supseteq A_{4} \supseteq \cdots,<br>\]<br><br>where each successive set is a subset of all the previous ones. Notationally,<br><br>{{c1::\[<br>\bigcup_{n=1}^{\infty} A_{n}, \quad \bigcup_{n \in \mathbf{N} } A_{n}, \quad \text { or } \quad A_{1} \cup A_{2} \cup A_{3} \cup \cdots<br>\]}}<br><br>are all equivalent ways to indicate the set whose elements consist of {{c2::any element}} that {{c3::appears in at least one particular \(A_{n}\)}}. <br><br>Because of the nested property of this particular collection of sets, it is not too hard to see that<br><br><ul><li>{{c3::\(\bigcup_{n=1}^{\infty} A_{n}\)}} = {{c4::\(A_{1}\)}}</li></ul><br>The notion of {{c5::intersection}} has the same kind of natural extension to infinite collections of sets. For this example, we have<br><br><ul><li>{{c5::\(\bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty} A_{n}\)}} = {{c4::\(\emptyset\)}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Given \(A \subseteq \mathbf{R}\), the {{c1::complement}} of \(A\), written {{c2::\(A^{c}\)}}, refers to the set of {{c5::{{c3::all elements of \(\mathbf{R}\) not in \(A\)}}}}. Thus, for \(A \subseteq \mathbf{R}\),<br><br><ul><li>{{c2::\(A^{c}\)}} = {{c5::{{c4::\(\{x \in \mathbf{R}: x \notin A\} .\)}}}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    A few times in our work to come, we will refer to {{c5::{{c3::De Morgan's Laws}}}}, which state that:<br><ul><li>{{c4::{{c1::\((A \cap B)^c\)}}}} = {{c4::{{c2::\(A^c \cup B^c\)}}}}<br></li></ul>

============================================================

  

    <b>Definition 1.2.3:</b><br><ul><li>Given two sets \(A\) and \(B\), a {{c1::function}} from \(A\) to \(B\) is a {{c2::rule}} or {{c2::mapping}} that {{c3::takes each element \(x \in A\)}} and {{c4::associates with it}} {{c5::a single element of \(B\).&nbsp;}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    <b>Definition 1.2.3:</b><br><ul><li>Given two sets \(A\) and \(B\), a function from \(A\) to \(B\) is a rule or mapping that takes each element \(x \in A\) and associates with it a single element of \(B\).&nbsp;</li><li>In this case, we write {{c1::\(f: A \rightarrow B\).}}</li><li>Given {{c3::an element \(x \in A\)}}, the expression {{c2::\(f(x)\)}} is used to represent {{c4::the element of \(B\)}} {{c5::associated with \(x\) by \(f\).&nbsp;}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    <b>Definition 1.2.3:</b><br><ul><li>The set \(A\) is called the {{c1::domain}} of \(f\).&nbsp;<br></li><li>The {{c2::range of \(f\)}} is not necessarily {{c3::equal to \(B\)}} {{c5::but refers to the subset of \(B\)}} given by {{c4::\(\{y \in B: y=f(x)\)}} for {{c5::some \(x \in A\}\).}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Example 1.2.4. In 1829, Dirichlet proposed the unruly function<br><br><ul><li>\(g(x) \) =&nbsp; {{c5::{{c3::\(\begin{cases}1 &amp; \text { if } x \in \mathbf{Q} \\ 0 &amp; \text { if } x \notin \mathbf{Q}\end{cases}\)}}}}</li></ul><br>The domain of \(g\) is all of \(\mathbf{R}\), and the range is the set \(\{0,1\}\). There is {{c4::{{c1::no single formula for \(g\)}}}} in the usual sense, and it is quite difficult to {{c4::{{c2::graph}}}} this function (see Section 4.1 for a rough attempt), but it certainly qualifies as a function<br>

============================================================

  

    &nbsp;The {{c2::absolute value}} function is so important that it merits the special notation {{c2::\(|x|\)}} in place of the usual \(f(x)\) or \(g(x)\). It is defined for every real number via the piecewise definition<br><br><ul><li>{{c2::\(|x|\)}} =&nbsp; {{c1::\(\begin{cases}x &amp; \text { if } x \geq 0 \\ -x &amp; \text { if } x&lt;0\end{cases}\)}}</li></ul><br>With respect to multiplication and division, the {{c2::absolute value}} function satisfies<br><br><ul><li>(i) {{c4::\(|a b|\)}} {{c5::=}} {{c3::\(|a||b|\)}} and</li><li>(ii) {{c3::\(|a+b|\)&nbsp;}} {{c5::\(\leq\)}} {{c4::\(|a|+|b|\)}}</li></ul><br>for all choices of \(a\) and \(b\).&nbsp;<br>

============================================================

  

    Given three real numbers \(a, b\), and \(c\), we certainly have<br><br><ul><li>{{c1::\(|a-b|\)}}&nbsp;= {{c2::\(|(a-c)\)}} + {{c2::\((c-b)| \text {. }\)}}</li></ul><br>By the triangle inequality,<br><br><ul><li>{{c2::\(|(a-c)+(c-b)|\)}}&nbsp; \(\leq\) {{c3::\(|a-c|+|c-b|,\)}}</li></ul><br>so we get<br><ul><li>{{c1::\(|a-b| \)}} \(\leq\) {{c4::\(|a-c|\)}} + {{c4::\(|c-b| . \tag{1}\)}}</li></ul><br>Now, the expression \(|a-b|\) is equal to \(|b-a|\) and is best understood as the distance between the points \(a\) and \(b\) on the number line. With this interpretation, equation (1) makes the plausible statement that the distance from \(a\) to \(b\) is less than or equal to the {{c5::distance from \(a\) to \(c\)}} plus {{c5::the distance from \(c\) to \(b\).}} Pretending for a moment that these are points in the plane (instead of on the real line), it should be evident why this is referred to as the "triangle inequality."<br>

============================================================

  

    A {{c1::direct}} proof begins from {{c2::some valid statement}}, most often taken from the {{c3::theorem's hypothesis}}, and then proceeds through {{c4::rigorously logical deductions}} to a {{c5::demonstration of the theorem's conclusion}}.&nbsp;

============================================================

  

    An {{c5::{{c1::indirect}}}} proof always begins by {{c4::{{c2::negating}}}} {{c4::{{c3::what it is we would like to prove.}}}}&nbsp;

============================================================

  

    Theorem 1.2.6. Two real numbers \(a\) and \(b\) are equal if and only if for every {{c5::real number \(\epsilon\)}} {{c4::&gt;}} {{c3::\(0\)}} it follows that {{c2::\(|a-b|\)}} {{c4::\(&lt;\)}} {{c1::\(\epsilon\).}}

============================================================

  

    Theorem 1.2.6. Two real numbers \(a\) and \(b\) are equal if and only if for every real number \(\epsilon&gt;0\) it follows that \(|a-b|&lt;\epsilon\).<br><br><ul><li>For the proof of the first statement, there is really not much to say. If \(a=b\), then \(|a-b|\) = {{c5::{{c1::\(0\),}}}} and so certainly \(|a-b|\) {{c4::{{c2::\(&lt;\) \(\epsilon\)}}}} no matter what {{c4::{{c3::\(\epsilon&gt;0\) is chosen.}}}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    <b>Theorem 1.2.6. </b>Two real numbers \(a\) and \(b\) are equal if and only if for every real number \(\epsilon&gt;0\) it follows that \(|a-b|&lt;\epsilon\).<br><br><ul><li>For the second statement, we give a proof by contradiction. The conclusion of the proposition in this direction states that \(a=b\), so we assume that {{c1::\(a \neq b\)}}.&nbsp;</li><li>Heading off in search of a contradiction brings us to a consideration of the phrase "for every \(\epsilon&gt;0\)." Some equivalent ways to state the hypothesis would be to say that "for all possible choices of \(\epsilon&gt;0\) " or "no matter how \(\epsilon&gt;0\) is selected, it is always the case that \(|a-b|&lt;\epsilon\)."&nbsp;</li><li>But assuming {{c1::\(a \neq b\)}} (as we are doing at the moment), the choice of</li><ul><li>\(\epsilon_{0}\) = {{c2::\(|a-b|&gt;0\)}}</li><li>poses a serious problem.&nbsp;</li></ul><li>We are assuming that {{c3::\(|a-b|&lt;\epsilon\)}} is true for {{c3::every \(\epsilon&gt;0\)}}, so this must certainly be true of the particular \(\epsilon_{0}\) just defined.&nbsp;</li><li>However, the statements:</li><ul><li>\(|a-b|\) \(&lt;\) {{c4::\(\epsilon_{0}\)&nbsp;}}</li><li>\(|a-b|\) {{c5::= \(\epsilon_{0}\)}}</li></ul><li>cannot {{c5::both be true}}.&nbsp;</li><li>This contradiction means that our initial assumption that \(a \neq b\) is unacceptable.&nbsp;</li><li>Therefore, \(a=b\), and the indirect proof is complete.<br></li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Exercise 1.2.2. Show that there is no {{c5::{{c1::rational number \(r\)}}}} satisfying {{c4::{{c2::\(2^{r}\)}}}} = {{c4::{{c3::\(3\).}}}}

============================================================

  

    Exercise 1.2.5 (De Morgan's Laws). Let \(A\) and \(B\) be subsets of \(\mathbf{R}\).<br><br>(a) If \(x\)&nbsp; \(\in\) {{c5::{{c1::\((A \cap B)^{c}\)}}}}, explain why \(x\) \(\in\) {{c4::{{c2::\(A^{c} \cup B^{c}\).}}}} This shows that {{c1::\((A \cap B)^{c}\)}} {{c4::{{c3::\(\subseteq\)}}}} {{c2::\(A^{c} \cup B^{c}\).}}

============================================================

  

    <br>(b) Prove the reverse inclusion {{c1::\((A \cap B)^{c}\)}} {{c5::\(\supseteq\)}} {{c2::\(A^{c} \cup B^{c}\)}}, and conclude that {{c3::\((A \cap B)^{c}\)}} {{c5::=}} {{c4::\(A^{c} \cup B^{c}\).}}

============================================================

  

    (c) Show {{c5::{{c1::\((A \cup B)^{c}\)}}}} = {{c4::{{c2::\(A^{c} \cap B^{c}\)}}}} by demonstrating {{c4::{{c3::inclusion both ways.}}}}

============================================================

  

    Exercise 1.2.6. (a) Verify the triangle inequality in the special case where \(a\) and \(b\) have the same sign.<br><br><ul><li>(b) Find an efficient proof for all the cases at once by first demonstrating {{c1::\((a+b)^{2}\)}} {{c5::\( \leq\)}} {{c2::\((|a|+|b|)^{2}\).}}</li><li>(c) Prove {{c3::\(|a-b|\)}}&nbsp; {{c5::\(\leq\)}} {{c4::\(|a-c|\)}}+{{c4::\(|c-d|\)}} + {{c4::\(|d-b|\)}} for all \(a, b\), \(c\), and \(d\).</li><li>(d) Prove {{c4::||\(a|-| b||\)}} {{c5::\(\leq\)}} {{c3::\(|a-b|\).}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    (c) Show that, for an arbitrary function \(g: \mathbf{R} \rightarrow \mathbf{R}\), it is always true that:<br><ul><li>{{c1::&nbsp;\(g(A \cap B)\)&nbsp;}} {{c3::\(\subseteq\)}} {{c5::{{c2::\(g(A) \cap g(B)\)}}}} for {{c5::{{c4::all sets \(A, B \subseteq \mathbf{R}\).}}}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Exercise 1.2.8. Here are two important definitions related to a function \(f\) : \(A \rightarrow B\):<br><ul><li>The function \(f\) is {{c1::one-to-one}} ({{c1::1-1}}) if {{c2::\(a_{1} \neq a_{2}\)}} in \(A\) implies that {{c3::\(f\left(a_{1}\right)\)}} &nbsp;{{c4::\(\neq\)\(f\left(a_{2}\right)\)}} in \(B\).&nbsp;</li><li>The function \(f\) is {{c1::onto}} if, given {{c5::any \(b \in B\)}}, it is possible to {{c5::find an element \(a \in A\)}} for which {{c4::\(f(a)\)}} = {{c5::\(b\).}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    The function \(f\) is {{c5::onto}} if, given {{c1::any \(b \in B\)}}, it is {{c4::possible to find}} an {{c3::element \(a \in A\)}} for which {{c2::\(f(a)\)}} = {{c1::\(b\).}}

============================================================

  

    Exercise 1.2.9. Given a function \(f: D \rightarrow \mathbf{R}\) and a subset \(B \subseteq \mathbf{R}\):<br><ul><li>Let {{c3::\(f^{-1}(B)\)}} be the {{c4::set of all points from the domain \(D\)}} that {{c5::get mapped into \(B\)}}</li><li>That is, {{c3::\(f^{-1}(B)\)}} = {{c2::\(\{x \in D: f(x) \in B\}\)}}. This set is called the {{c1::preimage of \(B\)}}.</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    The good behavior of preimages demonstrated in (a) is completely general.Show that for an arbitrary function \(g: \mathbf{R} \rightarrow \mathbf{R}\), it is always true that:<br><ul><li>&nbsp;\(g^{-1}\){{c1::\((A \cap B)\)}} = {{c3::\(g^{-1}\)\((A) \cap g^{-1}(B)\)}} and&nbsp;</li><li>\(g^{-1}\){{c5::{{c2::\((A \cup B)\)}}}} = {{c5::{{c4::\(g^{-1}\) \((A) \cup g^{-1}(B)\)}}}} for all sets \(A, B \subseteq \mathbf{R}\).</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    (a) Show how induction can be used to conclude that<br><br><ul><li>{{c4::\((\)}} {{c1::\(A_{1} \cup A_{2} \cup \cdots \cup A_{n}\)}} {{c4::\()^{c}\)}} = {{c2::\(A_{1}^{c}\)}} {{c5::\(\cap\)}} {{c2::\(A_{2}^{c}\)}} {{c5::\(\cap\)}} \(\cdots\) {{c3::\(\cap A_{n}^{c}\)}}</li></ul><br>for any finite \(n \in \mathbf{N}\).<br>

============================================================

  

    <ul><li>{{c4::\(\)}} {{c3::\((\bigcup_{i=1}^{\infty}\)}} {{c5::{{c1::\(A_{i}\)}}}} {{c4::\()^{c}\)}} = {{c3::\(\bigcap_{i=1}^{\infty}\)}} {{c5::{{c2::\( A_{i}^{c}\)}}}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    (b) It is tempting to appeal to {{c3::induction}} to conclude<br><br>\[<br>\left(\bigcup_{i=1}^{\infty} A_{i}\right)^{c}=\bigcap_{i=1}^{\infty} A_{i}^{c}<br>\]<br><br>but induction does not apply here. {{c4::Induction}} is used to prove that a particular statement holds for {{c1::every value of \(n \in \mathbf{N}\)}}, but this {{c5::does not imply}} the validity of {{c2::the infinite}} case.&nbsp;

============================================================

  

    <b>An Initial Definition for \(\mathrm{R}\)<br></b><br><ul><li>First, \(\mathbf{R}\) is a set containing \(\mathbf{Q}\).&nbsp;</li><li>The operations of {{c2::addition}} and {{c2::multiplication}} on \(\mathbf{Q}\) extend to all of \(\mathbf{R}\) in such a way that every element of \(\mathbf{R}\) has an {{c2::additive}} inverse and every {{c3::nonzero element of \(\mathbf{R}\)}} has a {{c2::multiplicative}} inverse. Echoing the discussion in Section 1.1, we assume \(\mathbf{R}\) is a {{c5::field}}, meaning that {{c2::addition}} and {{c2::multiplication}} of real numbers are {{c5::commutative}}, {{c5::associative}}, and the {{c5::distributive property}} holds.&nbsp;</li><li>We also assume that the familiar properties of the {{c4::ordering}} on \(\mathbf{Q}\) extend to all of \(\mathbf{R}\).&nbsp;</li><li>To summarize the situation in the official terminology&nbsp;of the subject, we assume that \(\mathbf{R}\) is an {{c4::ordered}} {{c5::field}}, which contains {{c5::\(\mathbf{Q}\) as a subfield}}</li><li>This brings us to the final, and most distinctive, assumption about the real number system. We must find some way to clearly articulate what we mean by insisting that \(\mathbf{R}\) does {{c1::not contain the gaps that permeate \(\mathbf{Q}\)}}. Because this is the defining difference between the rational numbers and the real numbers, we will be excessively precise about how we phrase this assumption, hereafter referred to as the <b>Axiom of&nbsp;</b>{{c1::Completeness}}.</li><ul><li>{{c1::Every nonempty set of real numbers that is bounded above has a least upper bound.}}</li></ul></ul>

============================================================

  

    <b>Axiom of&nbsp;</b>{{c1::Completeness}}:<br><ul><li>{{c5::Every nonempty}} {{c4::set of real numbers}} that is {{c3::bounded above}} has {{c2::a least upper bound}}.</li><li>Figure:</li><ul><li>{{c3::<img src="paste-1c592b265f649a91ce499333de142664fe51261a.jpg">}}<br></li></ul></ul>

============================================================

  

    <b>Definition 1.3.1.</b>&nbsp;<br><ul><li>A set \(A \subseteq \mathbf{R}\) is {{c1::bounded}} {{c2::above}} if there exists {{c5::a number \(b \in \mathbf{R}\)}} such that \(a\) {{c3::\(\leq\)}} {{c5::\(b\)}} for all \(a \in A\). The {{c5::number \(b\)}} is called an {{c4::upper bound for \(A\).}}</li><li>Similarly, the set \(A\) is {{c1::bounded}} {{c2::below}} if there exists {{c4::a lower bound \(l \in \mathbf{R}\)}} satisfying {{c4::\(l\)}} {{c3::\(\leq\)}} \(a\) for every \(a \in A\).</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Definition 1.3.1. A set \(A \subseteq \mathbf{R}\) is {{c5::bounded above}} if there exists a {{c2::number \(b\)}} \(\in\) {{c1::\(\mathbf{R}\)}} such that {{c4::\(a\)}}&nbsp;&nbsp;{{c3::\(\leq\)}} {{c2::\(b\)}} for all \(a \in A\). The {{c2::number \(b\)}} is called an {{c5::upper bound for \(A\).}}

============================================================

  

    Similarly, the set \(A\) is {{c5::bounded below}} if there exists a {{c2::lower bound \(l\)}} \(\in\) {{c1::\(\mathbf{R}\)}} satisfying {{c2::\(l \)}} {{c3::\(\leq\)}} {{c4::\(a\)}} for {{c4::every \(a \in A\).}}

============================================================

  

    <b>Definition 1.3.2.<br></b><br><ul><li>A real number \(s\) is the {{c2::least}} {{c1::upper bound}} for a set \(A \subseteq \mathbf{R}\) if it meets the following two criteria:</li><ul><li>(i) \(s\) is an {{c1::upper bound}} for \(A\);</li><li>(ii) if {{c3::\(b\)}} is {{c3::any}} {{c1::upper bound}} for \(A\), then \(s\) {{c4::\(\leq\)}} {{c3::\(b\)}}.</li></ul><li>The {{c2::least}} {{c1::upper bound}} is also frequently called the {{c5::supremum}} of the set \(A\).&nbsp;</li><ul><li>Although the notation \(s\) = {{c2::\(\operatorname{lub} A\)}} is sometimes used, we will always write \(s=\) {{c5::\(\sup A\)}} for the {{c2::least}} {{c1::upper bound.}}</li></ul></ul>

============================================================

  

    {{c5::{{c1::<img src="paste-00343c6e664ca70dfe78601679c77bfba025c44f.jpg">}}}}<br>Figure 1.3: Definition of {{c4::{{c2::\(\sup\)}}}} \(A\) and {{c4::{{c3::\(\inf\)}}}} \(A\).

============================================================

  

    <ul><li>Definition 1.3.2. A real number \(s\) is the least upper bound for a set \(A \subseteq \mathbf{R}\) if it meets the following two criteria:</li><li>(i) \(s\) is an upper bound for \(A\);</li><li>(ii) if \(b\) is any upper bound for \(A\), then \(s \leq b\).</li></ul><br>Although a set can have a host of upper bounds, it can have {{c1::only one}} least upper bound. If \(s_{1}\) and \(s_{2}\) are both least upper bounds for a set \(A\), then by property {{c2::(ii)}} in Definition 1.3 .2 we can assert {{c3::\(s_{1}\)}} {{c5::\(\leq\)}} {{c4::\(s_{2}\)}} and {{c4::\(s_{2}\)}} {{c5::\(\leq\)}} {{c3::\(s_{1}\)}}. The conclusion is that \(s_{1}=s_{2}\) and least upper bounds are unique.<br>

============================================================

  

    Example 1.3.3. Let<br><br>\[<br>A=\left\{\frac{1}{n}: n \in N\right\}=\left\{1, \frac{1}{2}, \frac{1}{3}, \ldots\right\}<br>\]<br><br><ul><li>{{c2::sup}} A = {{c1::1&nbsp;&nbsp;}}</li><ul><li>To verify (ii), we begin by assuming we {{c3::are in possession of some other upper bound \(b\)}}. Because {{c4::\(1 \in A\)}} and {{c3::\(b\) is an upper bound for \(A\)}}, we must have {{c5::\(1 \leq b\).}}<br></li></ul><li>{{c2::inf}} A = {{c1::0}}</li></ul><div><br></div><div><img src="paste-b314723cb98d49641c58f14e961afb5b448999c6.jpg"><br></div>

============================================================

  

    An important lesson to take from Example 1.3.3 is that {{c1::\(\sup A\)}} and {{c2::\(\inf A\)}} {{c3::may}} or {{c4::may not}} be {{c5::elements of the set \(A\)}}. This issue is tied to understanding the crucial difference between the {{c5::maximum}} and the {{c1::supremum}}&nbsp;&nbsp;of a given set.

============================================================

  

    <b>Definition 1.3.4.</b><br><ul><li>&nbsp;A real number \(a_{0}\) is a {{c1::maximum}} of the set \(A\) if \(a_{0}\) is an {{c3::element of \(A\)}} and \(a_{0}\) {{c2::\(\geq\)}} {{c4::\(a\)}} for {{c5::all \(a \in A\)}}.&nbsp;</li><li>Similarly, a number \(a_{1}\) is a {{c1::minimum}} of \(A\) if \(a_{1}\) {{c3::\(\in\) \(A\)}} and \(a_{1}\) {{c2::\(\leq\)}} {{c4::\(a\)}} for {{c5::every \(a \in A\).}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Example 1.3.5. To belabor the point, consider the open interval<br><br>\[<br>(0,2)=\{x \in \mathbf{R}: 0&lt;x&lt;2\},<br>\]<br><br>and the closed interval<br><br>\[<br>[0,2]=\{x \in \mathbf{R}: 0 \leq x \leq 2\} .<br>\]<br><br>Both sets are bounded above (and below), and both have the same least upper bound, namely 2 . It is not the case, however, that both sets have a {{c4::maximum}}. A {{c4::maximum}} is a specific type of upper bound that is required to be {{c1::an element of the set in question}}, and the open interval \((0,2)\) does {{c5::{{c2::not possess such an element}}}}. Thus, the {{c5::{{c3::supremum}}}} can exist and not be a {{c4::maximum}}, but when a {{c4::maximum}} exists, then it is also the {{c3::supremum}}.

============================================================

  

    The axiom of {{c1::completeness}} if {{c2::not}} {{c4::a valid statement}} on {{c3::Q}}, unlike {{c5::R}}

============================================================

  

    Example 1.3.6. Consider again the set<br><br>\[<br>S=\left\{r \in \mathbf{Q}: r^{2}&lt;2\right\},<br>\]<br><br><ul><li>and pretend for the moment that our world consists only of {{c1::rational}} numbers.&nbsp;</li><li>The set \(S\) is certainly bounded above. Taking \(b=2\) works, as does \(b=3 / 2\). But notice what happens as we go in search of the least upper bound. (It may be useful here to know that the decimal expansion for \(\sqrt{2}\) begins \(1.4142 \ldots\). .)&nbsp;</li><li>We might try \(b=142 / 100\), which is indeed an upper bound, but then we discover that \(b=1415 / 1000\) is an upper bound that is {{c4::smaller still}}. Is there a {{c4::smallest}} one?</li><li>In the {{c1::rational}} numbers, there {{c2::is not.}} In the {{c3::real}} numbers, there is.&nbsp;</li><li>Back in \(\mathbf{R}\), the Axiom of {{c5::Completeness}} states that we may set \(\alpha=\sup S\) and be confident that such a number exists.&nbsp;</li><li>In the next section, we will prove that \(\alpha^{2}=2\). But according to Theorem 1.1.1, this implies \(\alpha\) is not a {{c1::rational}} number.&nbsp;</li><li>If we are restricting our attention to only {{c1::rational}} numbers, then \(\alpha\) is not an allowable option for \(\sup S\), and the search for a least upper bound {{c5::goes on indefinitely.}}&nbsp;</li><li>Whatever {{c1::rational}} upper bound is discovered, it is always possible to {{c4::find one smaller.}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Example 1.3.7. Let \(A \subseteq \mathbf{R}\) be {{c5::nonempty}} and {{c5::bounded above}}, and let \(c \in \mathbf{R}\). Define the set {{c2::\(c+A\)}} by<br><br><ul><li>{{c2::\(c+A\)}} = {{c4::\(\{c+a: a \in A\} .\)}}</li><li>Then {{c3::\(\sup\)}} {{c2::\((c+A)\)}} = {{c1::\(c+\sup A\).}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    <b>Lemma</b> 1.3.8:<br><ul><li>Assume \(s \in \mathbf{R}\) is an upper bound for a set \(A \subseteq \mathbf{R}\).&nbsp;</li><li>Then, \(s\) = {{c5::\(\sup A\)}} if and only if, for {{c3::every choice of \(\epsilon\)}} {{c4::\(&gt;\) \(0\)}}, there exists an {{c3::element \(a\) \(\in\) \(A\)}} satisfying {{c2::\(s-\epsilon\)}} {{c1::\(&lt;a\).}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Lemma 1.3.8. Assume \(s \in \mathbf{R}\) is an upper bound for a set \(A \subseteq \mathbf{R}\). Then, \(s=\sup A\) if and only if, for every choice of \(\epsilon&gt;0\), there exists an element \(a \in A\) satisfying \(s-\epsilon&lt;a\).<br><br>Proof. Here is a short rephrasing of the lemma: Given that \(s\) is an upper bound, \(s\) is the {{c4::least upper bound}} if and only if {{c3::any}} number {{c5::{{c2::smaller than \(s\)}}}} is {{c5::{{c1::not an upper bound.&nbsp;}}}}

============================================================

  

    It is certainly the case that all of our conclusions to this point about least upper bounds have analogous versions for {{c1::greatest lower bounds}}. The Axiom of Completeness does not explicitly assert that a {{c2::nonempty}} set {{c5::{{c3::bounded below}}}} has an {{c5::{{c4::infimum}}}}, but this is because we do not need to assume this fact as part of the axiom. Using the Axiom of Completeness, there are several ways to prove that {{c1::greatest lower bounds}} exist for {{c2::nonempty}} {{c5::{{c3::bounded}}}} sets.&nbsp;

============================================================

  

    <b>Definition 1.3.2</b>. A real number \(i\) is the {{c3::greatest lower bound}} or {{c4::infimum}} for a set \(A\) \(\subseteq\) {{c5::\( \mathbf{R}\)}} if it meets the following two criteria:<ul><li>(i) \(i\) is a {{c1::lower bound}} for \(A\);</li><li>(ii) if \(b\) is any {{c1::lower bound}} for \(A\), then \(i\) {{c2::\(\geq\) \(b\).}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Exercise 1.3.3.<br><br><ul><li>&nbsp;(a) Let \(A\) be nonempty and {{c3::bounded below}}, and define \(B=\) \(\{b \in \mathbf{R}: b\) is a&nbsp; {{c3::lower bound}} for \(A\}\). Show that {{c1::\(\sup\)}} {{c2::\(B\)}} = {{c2::\(\inf\)}}&nbsp; {{c1::\(A\).}}</li><li>(b) Use (a) to explain why there is no need to assert that {{c3::greatest lower bounds}} {{c5::exist}} as part of the Axiom of {{c4::Completeness}}.</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Exercise 1.3.5. As in Example 1.3.7, let \(A \subseteq \mathbf{R}\) be nonempty and bounded above, and let \(c \in \mathbf{R}\). This time define the set \(c A=\{c a: a \in A\}\).<br><ul><li>(a) If \(c\) {{c4::\(\geq\) \(0\)}}, show that {{c3::\(\sup\)}} {{c5::{{c2::\((c A)\)}}}} = {{c5::{{c1::\(c \sup A\).}}}}<br></li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Exercise 1.3.6. Given sets \(A\) and \(B\), define \(A+B=\{a+b: a \in A\) and \(b \in B\}\). Follow these steps to prove that if \(A\) and \(B\) are {{c4::nonempty}} and {{c5::bounded above}} then {{c3::\(\sup\)}} {{c2::\((A+B)\)}} = {{c1::\(\sup A+\sup B\).}}

============================================================

  

    Exercise 1.3.6. Given sets \(A\) and \(B\), define \(A+B=\{a+b: a \in A\) and \(b \in B\}\). Follow these steps to prove that if \(A\) and \(B\) are nonempty and bounded above then \(\sup (A+B)=\sup A+\sup B\).<br><br><ul><li>(a) Let \(s=\sup A\) and \(t=\sup B\). Show {{c1::\(s+t\)}} is an {{c2::upper bound}} for {{c2::\(A+B\).}}</li><li>(b) Now let \(u\) be an {{c3::arbitrary upper bound}} for {{c2::\(A+B\),}} and temporarily fix \(a \in A\). Show \(t \) \(\leq\) {{c4::\(u-a\)}}.</li><li>(c) Finally, show \(\sup (A+B)\) = {{c5::\(s+t\).}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Exercise 1.3.7. Prove that if \(a\) is an {{c1::upper bound}} for \(A\), and if \(a\) is also an {{c2::element of \(A\)}}, then it must be that {{c5::{{c4::\(a\)}}}} = {{c5::{{c3::\(\sup A\).}}}}

============================================================

  

    Exercise 1.3.9. <br><ul><li>(a) If {{c1::\(\sup A\)}} {{c5::&lt;}} {{c2::\(\sup B\)}}, show that there exists an element \(b \in B\) that is an {{c3::upper bound}} for {{c4::\(A\).}}<br></li><li>(b) Give an example to show that this is not always the case if we only assume {{c1::\(\sup A\)}} {{c5::\(\leq\)}} {{c2::\(\sup B\).}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Exercise 1.3.10<br><br>The {{c1::Cut Property}} of the real numbers is the following:<ul><li>If \(A\) and \(B\) are nonempty, disjoint sets with {{c2::\(A \cup B\)}} = {{c2::\(\mathbf{R}\)}} and {{c3::\(a\)}} {{c5::&lt;}} {{c4::\(b\)}} for all {{c3::\(a \in A\)}} and {{c4::\(b \in B\)}}, then there {{c5::exists \(c\)}}&nbsp; \(\in\) {{c2::\(\mathbf{R}\)}} such that \(x\) {{c1::\(\leq\)}} {{c5::\(c\)}} whenever \(x\) \(\in\) {{c2::\(A\)}} and \(x\) {{c1::\(\geq\)}} {{c5::\(c\)}} whenever \(x\) \(\in\) {{c3::\(B\).}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Exercise 1.3.10 (Cut Property). The Cut Property of the real numbers is the following:<br><br>If \(A\) and \(B\) are nonempty, disjoint sets with \(A \cup B=\mathbf{R}\) and \(a&lt;b\) for all \(a \in A\) and \(b \in B\), then there exists \(c \in \mathbf{R}\) such that \(x \leq c\) whenever \(x \in A\) and \(x \geq c\) whenever \(x \in B\).<br><br><ul><li>(a) Use the {{c1::Axiom of Completeness}} to prove the Cut Property.</li><li>(b) Show that the implication goes the other way; that is, assume \(\mathbf{R}\) possesses the Cut Property and let \(E\) be a nonempty set that is {{c2::bounded above}}. Prove {{c3::\(\sup E\)}} exists.</li><li>(c) The punchline of parts (a) and (b) is that the Cut Property could be used in place of the {{c1::Axiom of Completeness}} as the fundamental axiom that {{c4::distinguishes the real numbers}} from the {{c4::rational numbers}}. To drive this point home, give a concrete example showing that the Cut Property is not a valid statement when {{c5::\(\mathbf{R}\)}} is {{c5::replaced by \(\mathbf{Q}\).}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Theorem 1.4.1 ({{c5::Nested Interval}}<b>&nbsp;Property</b>). <br><br><ul><li>For each \(n \in \mathbf{N}\), assume we are given a closed interval {{c1::\(I_{n}\)}} = {{c5::\(\left[a_{n}, b_{n}\right] \)}} = {{c5::\(\left\{x \in \mathbf{R}: a_{n} \leq x \leq b_{n}\right\}\).&nbsp;}}</li><li>Assume also that each {{c1::\(I_{n}\)}} {{c5::contains \(I_{n+1}\)}}.&nbsp;</li><li>Then, the resulting nested sequence of closed intervals</li><ul><li>{{c1::\(I_{1}\)}} {{c2::\(\supseteq\)}} {{c1::\(I_{2}\)}} {{c2::\(\supseteq\)}} {{c1::\(I_{3}\)}} {{c2::\(\supseteq\)}} {{c1::\(I_{4}\)}} {{c2::\(\supseteq\)}} \(\cdots\)</li></ul><li>has a {{c3::nonempty intersection}}; that is, {{c4::\(\bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty}\)}} {{c1::\(I_{n}\)}}&nbsp; {{c3::\(\neq\) \(\emptyset\).}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    <img src="paste-82b591d2788564fcb184d3545fa186cb89d3a466.jpg"><br>Proof. In order to show that \(\bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty} I_{n}\) is not empty, we are going to use the {{c1::Axiom of Completeness (AoC)}} to produce a single real number \(x\) satisfying \(x \in I_{n}\) for every \(n \in \mathbf{N}\). Now, {{c1::AoC}} is a statement about bounded sets, and the one we want to consider is the set<br><br><ul><li>\(A\) = {{c2::\(\left\{a_{n}: n \in \mathbf{N}\right\}\)}}</li></ul><br>of {{c2::left-hand endpoints of the intervals.}} as seen in the figure:<br>{{c3::<img src="paste-8125e3d4b152b61d72dd0cc79b9e550fcab212ce.jpg">}}<br><br>Because the intervals are nested, we see that every \(b_{n}\) serves as an {{c4::uper bound}} for \(A\). Thus, we are justified in setting<br><ul><li>\(x\) = {{c5::\(\sup A \text {. }\)}}<br></li></ul><br>Now, consider a particular \(I_{n}=\left[a_{n}, b_{n}\right]\). Because \(x\) is an {{c4::upper bound}} for \(A\), we have {{c5::\(a_{n}\) \(\leq\)}} \(x\). The fact that each \(b_{n}\) is an {{c4::upper bound}} for \(A\) and that \(x\) is the {{c5::least upper bound}} implies \(x\) {{c5::\(\leq\) \(b_{n}\).}}<br><br>Altogether then, we have {{c5::\(a_{n} \leq x \leq b_{n}\),}} which means \(x \in I_{n}\) for every choice of \(n \in \mathbf{N}\). Hence, \(x \in \bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty} I_{n}\), and the intersection is not empty.<br>

============================================================

  

    Theorem 1.4.2 ({{c5::Archimedean}}<b>&nbsp;Property</b>). <br><br><ul><li>(i) Given any number \(x\)&nbsp; \(\in\) {{c4::\(\mathbf{R}\)}}, there exists an \(n\) \(\in\) {{c5::\(\mathbf{N}\)}} satisfying {{c2::\(n\)}} {{c1::\(&gt;\) \(x\).}}</li><li>(ii) Given any real number \(y\) {{c4::\(&gt;0\)}}, there exists an \(n\) \(\in\) {{c5::\(\mathbf{N}\)}} satisfying {{c2::\(1 / n\)}} {{c3::\(&lt;\)}} \(y\).</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    The {{c5::{{c1::Axiom of Completeness}}}}, which we adopted to patch up the holes in \(\mathbf{Q}\), carries with it the implication that \(\mathbf{N}\) is an {{c4::{{c2::unbounded}}}} {{c4::{{c3::subset}}}} of \(\mathbf{R}\).

============================================================

  

    Theorem 1.4.2 (Archimedean Property). (i) Given any number \(x \in \mathbf{R}\), there exists an \(n \in \mathbf{N}\) satisfying \(n&gt;x\).<br><br>(ii) Given any real number \(y&gt;0\), there exists an \(n \in \mathbf{N}\) satisfying \(1 / n&lt;y\).<br><br><b>Proof:</b><br><ul><li>Part I: And so to the proof. Assume, for contradiction, that \(\mathbf{N}\) is {{c3::bounded above}}. By the {{c4::Axiom of Completeness (AoC)}}, \(\mathbf{N}\) should then have a {{c5::least upper bound}}, and we can set \(\alpha\) = {{c5::\(\sup \mathbf{N}\)}}. If we consider {{c1::\(\alpha-1\)}}, then we no longer have an {{c5::upper bound}}, and therefore there exists an \(n \in \mathbf{N}\) satisfying {{c3::\(\alpha-1&lt;n\)}}. But this is equivalent to \(\alpha\) &lt; {{c2::\(n+1\)}}. Because {{c2::\(n+1\)}}&nbsp; \(\in \mathbf{N}\), we have a contradiction to the fact that \(\alpha\) is supposed to be an {{c5::upper bound}} for \(\mathbf{N}\). (Notice that the contradiction here depends only on AoC and the fact that \(\mathbf{N}\) is closed under addition.)</li><li>Part (ii) follows from (i) by letting \(x\) = {{c5::\(1 / y\).}}<br></li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Theorem 1.4.3 ({{c5::Density}}&nbsp;of {{c5::\(\mathbf{Q}\)}} in {{c4::\(\mathbf{R}\)}} ). <br><br>For every two {{c4::real}} numbers \(a\) and \(b\) with {{c2::\(a\)}} {{c3::&lt;}} {{c2::\(b\)}}, there exists a {{c5::rational}}&nbsp;number \(r\) satisfying {{c2::\(a\)}} {{c1::&lt;}} r {{c1::&lt;}} {{c2::\(b\).}}

============================================================

  

    <b>Corollary</b> 1.4.4. Given any two {{c4::real}} numbers {{c1::\(a\)}} {{c5::{{c2::\(&lt;\)}}}} {{c1::\(b\)}}, there exists an {{c5::{{c3::irrational}}}} number \(t\) satisfying {{c1::\(a\)}} {{c5::{{c2::&lt;}}}} \(t\) {{c2::&lt;}} {{c1::\(b\).}}

============================================================

  

    <b>Theorem</b> 1.4.5. There exists a {{c5::{{c3::real}}}} number \(\alpha \in \mathbf{R}\) satisfying {{c4::{{c2::\(\alpha^{2}\)}}}} = {{c4::{{c1::\(2\).}}}}

============================================================

  

    Theorem 1.4.5. There exists a real number \(\alpha \in \mathbf{R}\) satisfying \(\alpha^{2}=2\).<br><br>Proof. After reviewing Example 1.3.6, consider the set<br><br>\[<br>T=\left\{t \in \mathbf{R}: t^{2}&lt;2\right\}<br>\]<br>and set \(\alpha=\sup T\).&nbsp;<br><br><br>Let's first see what happens if we assume \(\alpha^{2}&lt;2\). In search of an element of \(T\) that is larger than \(\alpha\), write<br><br><ul><li>{{c1::\(\left(\alpha+\frac{1}{n}\right)^2\)}} =&nbsp;\(\alpha^2+\frac{2 \alpha}{n}+\frac{1}{n^2}\)</li><li>{{c5::&lt;}}&nbsp;\(\alpha^2+\frac{2 \alpha}{n}+\frac{1}{n}\)</li><li>=&nbsp;\(\alpha^2+\frac{2 \alpha+1}{n}\).</li></ul><div><br></div><div>But now assuming \(\alpha^{2}&lt;2\) gives us a little space in which to fit the \((2 \alpha+1) / n\) term and keep the total less than 2 . Specifically, choose \(n_{0} \in \mathbf{N}\) large enough so that<br><br><ul><li>\(\frac{1}{n_{0}}\) \(&lt;\) {{c2::\(\frac{2-\alpha^{2} }{2 \alpha+1}\)}}</li></ul><br>This implies \((2 \alpha+1) / n_{0}\) &lt; {{c3::\(2-\alpha^{2}\)}}, and consequently that</div><div><br><ul><li>\(\left(\alpha+\frac{1}{n_{0}}\right)^{2}\) &lt; {{c3::\(\alpha^{2}+\left(2-\alpha^{2}\right)\)}} = {{c4::\(2 .\)}}</li></ul><div><br></div><div>Now, what about the case \(\alpha^{2}&gt;2\) ? This time, write<br></div></div><div><ul><li>{{c1::\(\left(\alpha+\frac{1}{n}\right)^2\)}} =&nbsp;\(\alpha^2+\frac{2 \alpha}{n}+\frac{1}{n^2}\)</li><li>{{c5::&gt;&nbsp;}}\(\alpha^2-\frac{2 \alpha}{n}\).</li></ul><div><br></div></div>

============================================================

  

    <img src="paste-94e9318bcfe8af421262bb3276245a0d40243a52.jpg"><br><br>A small modification of this proof can be made to show that {{c1::\(\sqrt{x}\)}} exists for any \(x \geq 0\). A formula for expanding {{c2::\((\alpha+1 / n)^{m}\)}} called the {{c4::binomial}} formula can be used to show that {{c3::\(\sqrt[m]{x}\)}} exists for {{c5::arbitrary values of \(m \in \mathbf{N}\).}}

============================================================

  

    Exercise 1.4.1. Recall that I stands for the set of {{c5::irrational}} numbers.<br><ul><li>(a) Show that if \(a, b \in \mathbf{Q}\), then {{c1::\(a b\)}} and {{c2::\(a+b\)}} are {{c3::elements of \(\mathbf{Q}\) as well}}<br></li><li>(b) Show that if \(a \in \mathbf{Q}\) and \(t \in \mathbf{I}\), then {{c2::\(a+t\)}} \(\in\) \(\mathbf{I}\) and {{c1::\(a t\)}} \(\in\) {{c4::\(\mathbf{I}\)}} as long as \(a\) \(\neq\) {{c5::\(0\).}}</li><li>(c) Part (a) can be summarized by saying that \(\mathbf{Q}\) is closed under {{c2::addition}} and {{c1::multiplication}}. Is I closed under {{c2::addition}} and {{c1::multiplication}}?&nbsp;</li><ul><li>Answer: {{c5::no}}</li></ul></ul>

============================================================

  

    Exercise 1.4.2.:<br><ul><li>Let \(A \subseteq \mathbf{R}\) be {{c5::nonempty}} and {{c5::bounded above}}</li><li>Let \(s \in \mathbf{R}\) have the property that for all \(n \in \mathbf{N}\), {{c2::\(s+\frac{1}{n}\)}} is an {{c4::upper bound for \(A\)}} and {{c3::\(s-\frac{1}{n}\)}} is {{c4::not an upper bound for \(A\).&nbsp;}}</li><li>Show \(s\) = {{c1::\(\sup A\).}}</li></ul>

    Exercise 1.4.6. Recall that a set \(B\) is {{c1::dense}} in \(\mathbf{R}\) if {{c4::an element of \(B\)}} {{c5::can be found between}} {{c3::any two real numbers}} {{c2::\(a&lt;b\).&nbsp;}}

============================================================

  

    <b>Definition 1.5.1</b>. <br><br><ul><li>A function \(f: A \rightarrow B\) is {{c1::one-to-one}} ({{c1::1-1}}) if {{c3::\(a_{1}\)}} {{c2::\(\neq\)}} {{c3::\(a_{2}\)}} in {{c2::\(A\)}} implies that {{c4::\(f\left(a_{1}\right)\)}} {{c2::\(\neq\)}} {{c4::\(f\left(a_{2}\right)\)}} in {{c2::\(B\).&nbsp;}}</li><li>The function \(f\) is {{c1::onto}} if, given {{c5::any \(b \in B\)}}, it is possible to find {{c1::an element \(a \in A\)}} for which {{c1::\(f(a)\)}}= {{c5::\(b\).}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    A function \(f: A \rightarrow B\) that is both {{c1::1-1}} and {{c2::onto}} provides us with exactly what we mean by a {{c3::\(1-1\) correspondence}} between two sets. The property of being {{c1::1-1}} means that {{c4::no two elements of \(A\) correspond to the same element of \(B\)}} , and the property of being {{c2::onto}} ensures that {{c5::every element of \(B\) corresponds to something in \(A\)}}&nbsp;

============================================================

  

    <b>Definition</b> 1.5.2. The set \(A\) has {{c5::{{c3::the same cardinality}}}} as \(B\) if there exists \(f: A \rightarrow B\) that is {{c4::{{c1::\(1-1\)}}}} and {{c4::{{c2::onto}}}}. In this case, we write \(A\) {{c5::{{c3::\(\sim\)}}}} \(B\).

============================================================

  

    Example 1.5.3. (i) If we let \(E=\{2,4,6, \ldots\}\) be the set of even natural numbers, then we can show {{c4::\(\mathbf{N}\)}} {{c3::\(\sim\) \(E\)}}. To see why, let \(f: \mathbf{N} \rightarrow E\) be given by \(f(n)\) = {{c5::{{c2::\(2 n\).}}}}<br><br>{{c5::{{c1::\[<br>\begin{array}{llllllll}<br>\mathbf{N}: &amp; 1 &amp; 2 &amp; 3 &amp; 4 &amp; \cdots &amp; n &amp; \cdots \\<br>&amp; \downarrow &amp; \downarrow &amp; \downarrow &amp; \downarrow &amp; \cdots &amp; \uparrow &amp; \\<br>&amp; &amp; &amp; \\<br>E: &amp; 2 &amp; 4 &amp; 6 &amp; 8 &amp; \cdots &amp; 2 n &amp; \cdots<br>\end{array}<br>\]}}}}

============================================================

  

    (ii) To make this point again, note that although \(\mathbf{N}\) is contained in \(\mathbf{Z}\) as a proper subset, we can show \(\mathbf{N}\)&nbsp; {{c2::\(\sim\)}} \(\mathbf{Z}\). This time le<br><br><ul><li>f(n) = {{c1::\(\begin{cases}(n-1) / 2 &amp; \text { if } n \text { is odd } \\ -n / 2 &amp; \text { if } n \text { is even. }\end{cases}\)}}</li></ul><br>The important details to verify are that \(f\) does {{c4::not map any two natural numbers to the same element of \(\mathbf{Z}\)}} ( \(f\) is {{c4::1-1}}) and that {{c5::every element of \(\mathbf{Z}\) gets "hit" by something in \(\mathbf{N}\)}} ( \(f\) is {{c5::onto}}).<br><br>{{c3::\[<br>\begin{array}{lcccccccc}<br>\mathbf{N}: &amp; 1 &amp; 2 &amp; 3 &amp; 4 &amp; 5 &amp; 6 &amp; 7 &amp; \cdots \\<br>&amp; \uparrow &amp; \downarrow &amp; \downarrow &amp; \downarrow &amp; \uparrow &amp; \downarrow &amp; \uparrow &amp; \\<br>\mathbf{Z}: &amp; 0 &amp; -1 &amp; 1 &amp; -2 &amp; 2 &amp; -3 &amp; 3 &amp; \cdots<br>\end{array}<br>\]}}<br>

============================================================

  

    Example 1.5.4. A little calculus (which we will not supply) shows that the function \(f(x)\) = {{c2::\(x\)}} {{c3::\(/\)}} {{c1::\(\left(x^{2}-1\right)\)}} takes the interval {{c4::\((-1,1)\)}} onto \(\mathbf{R}\) in a \(1-1\) fashion (Fig. 1.4). In fact, \((a, b)\)&nbsp; {{c5::\(\sim\)}} \(\mathbf{R}\) for {{c5::any interval \((a, b)\).}}

============================================================

  

    <b>Definition 1.5.5</b>. A set \(A\) is {{c2::countable}} if {{c1::\(\mathbf{N} \sim A\)}}. An infinite set that is {{c5::{{c4::not}}}} {{c2::countable}} is called an {{c5::{{c3::uncountable}}}} set.

============================================================

  

    {{c1::<img src="paste-ce9d53d0c32a6841fbed6adc5766b5ec78c98203.jpg">}}<br><br>Figure 1.4: {{c4::\((-1,1)\)}} {{c3::\(\sim\)}} {{c5::\(\mathbf{R}\)}} using \(f(x)\) = {{c2::\(x /\left(x^{2}-1\right)\).}}<br><br>

============================================================

  

    <b>Theorem</b> 1.5.6. <br><br><ul><li>(i) The set {{c3::\(\mathbf{Q}\)}} is {{c1::countable}}.&nbsp;</li><li>(ii) The set {{c5::{{c4::\(\mathbf{R}\)}}}} is {{c5::{{c2::uncountable}}}}.</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    <b>Theorem</b> 1.5.6. (i) The set \(\mathbf{Q}\) is countable. (ii) The set \(\mathbf{R}\) is uncountable.<br><br>Proof. (i) Set \(A_{1}=\{0\}\) and for each \(n \geq 2\), let \(A_{n}\) be the set given by<br><br><ul><li>\(A_{n}\) = {{c1::\(\left\{ \pm \frac{p}{q}: \text { where } p, q \in \mathbf{N} \text { are in lowest terms with } p+q=n\right\} \text {. }\)}}</li></ul><div><br></div><div>The first few of these sets look like<br><br>{{c2::\[<br>\begin{gathered}<br>A_{1}=\{0\}, \quad A_{2}=\left\{\frac{1}{1}, \frac{-1}{1}\right\}, \quad A_{3}=\left\{\frac{1}{2}, \frac{-1}{2}, \frac{2}{1}, \frac{-2}{1}\right\}, \\<br>A_{4}=\left\{\frac{1}{3}, \frac{-1}{3}, \frac{3}{1}, \frac{-3}{1}\right\}, \quad \text { and } A_{5}=\left\{\frac{1}{4}, \frac{-1}{4}, \frac{2}{3}, \frac{-2}{3}, \frac{3}{2}, \frac{-3}{2}, \frac{4}{1}, \frac{-4}{1}\right\} .<br>\end{gathered}<br>\]}}<br></div><div><br></div><div>The crucial observation is that each \(A_{n}\) is finite and {{c3::every rational number appears in exactly one of these sets}}. Our 1-1 correspondence with \(\mathbf{N}\) is then achieved by consecutively listing the elements in each \(A_{n}\) as in the following image:<br></div><div>{{c4::<img src="SanwQUH5PbeLukkBw79IKQiylptHjVcb91w-ZPUMM1Y.original.fullsize.png">}}<br></div><div><br></div><div>To verify that it is \(1-1\), we observe that the sets \(A_{n}\) were constructed to be {{c5::disjoint}} so that {{c5::no rational number appears twice}}. This completes the proof of (i).<br></div>

============================================================

  

    Theorem 1.5.6. (i) The set \(\mathbf{Q}\) is countable. (ii) The set \(\mathbf{R}\) is uncountable.<br><br>(ii) The second statement of Theorem 1.5.6 is the truly unexpected part, and its proof is done by contradiction. Assume that there does exist a 1-1, onto function \(f: \mathbf{N} \rightarrow \mathbf{R}\). Again, what this suggests is that it is possible to enumerate the elements of \(\mathbf{R}\). If we let \(x_{1}=f(1), x_{2}=f(2)\), and so on, then our assumption that \(f\) is onto means that we can write<br><br>\[<br>\begin{equation*}<br>\mathbf{R}=\left\{x_{1}, x_{2}, x_{3}, x_{4}, \ldots\right\} \tag{1}<br>\end{equation*}<br>\]<br><br>and be confident that every real number appears somewhere on the list. We will now use the Nested Interval Property (Theorem 1.4.1) to produce a real number that is not there.<br><br>Let \(I_{1}\) be a closed interval that does not contain \(x_{1}\). Next, let \(I_{2}\) be a closed interval, contained in \(I_{1}\), which does not contain \(x_{2}\). The existence of such an \(I_{2}\) is easy to verify. Certainly \(I_{1}\) contains {{c4::two smaller disjoint closed intervals}}, and {{c4::\(x_{2}\) can only be in one of these}}. In general, given an interval \(I_{n}\), construct \(I_{n+1}\) to satisfy<br><br><ol><li>(i) {{c1::\(I_{n+1} \subseteq I_{n}\)}} and</li><li>(ii) {{c2::\(x_{n+1} \notin I_{n+1}\)}}.</li></ol><div>As shown in the following picture:</div><div>{{c3::<img src="paste-7971b6d7f1cfa76a9bdb8016b0840da1dac32a12.jpg">}}<br></div><div><br></div><div>We now consider the intersection \(\bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty} I_{n}\). If \(x_{n_{0}}\) is some real number from the list in (1), then we have \(x_{n_{0}} \notin I_{n_{0}}\), and it follows that<br><br><ul><li>\(x_{n_{0}}\) \(\notin\) {{c5::\(\bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty} I_{n} .\)}}</li></ul><div>Now, we are assuming that the list in (1) contains every real number, and this leads to the conclusion that<br><br>\[<br>\bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty} I_{n}=\emptyset<br>\]<br><br>However, the Nested Interval Property (NIP) asserts that \(\bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty} I_{n}\) {{c5::\(\neq\)}} \(\emptyset\). By NIP, there is {{c5::at least one \(x \in \bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty} I_{n}\)}} that, consequently, cannot be on the list in (1). This contradiction means that such an enumeration of \(\mathbf{R}\) is impossible, and we conclude that \(\mathbf{R}\) is an {{c5::uncountable}} set.<br></div></div>

============================================================

  

    &nbsp;It is an important exercise to show that any {{c4::subset}} of a {{c1::countable}} set must be either {{c5::{{c2::countable}}}} or {{c5::{{c3::finite}}}}.

============================================================

  

    This means that {{c5::countable}} sets are the {{c1::smallest type}} of {{c2::infinite}} set. Anything smaller is either still {{c3::countable}} or {{c4::finite}}.

============================================================

  

    <ul><li>&nbsp;The set \(\mathbf{Q}\), on the other hand, is countable.&nbsp;</li><li>As far as infinite sets are concerned, this is as small as it gets.&nbsp;</li><li>What does this imply about the set I of irrational numbers?&nbsp;</li><li>By imitating the demonstration that \(\mathbf{N} \sim \mathbf{Z}\), we can prove that the union of two {{c1::countable}} sets must be {{c1::countable}}.&nbsp;</li><li>Because \(\mathbf{R}\) = {{c2::\(\mathbf{Q} \cup \mathbf{I}\)}}, it follows that \(\mathbf{I}\) {{c3::cannot be countable}} because {{c4::otherwise \(\mathbf{R}\) would be.&nbsp;}}</li><li>The inescapable conclusion is that, the irrational numbers form {{c5::a far greater subset of \(\mathbf{R}\)}} than {{c5::\(\mathbf{Q}\).}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    <b>Theorem</b> 1.5.7. If \(A\) {{c5::{{c1::\(\subseteq\)}}}} \(B\) and \(B\) is {{c4::{{c2::countable}}}}, then \(A\) is either {{c2::countable}} or {{c4::{{c3::finite}}}}.

============================================================

  

    Theorem 1.5.8. <br><ul><li><br></li><li>(i) If \(A_{1}, A_{2}, \ldots A_{m}\) are each countable sets, then the {{c2::union}} {{c3::\(A_{1} \cup A_{2} \cup \cdots \cup A_{m}\)}} is {{c1::countable}}.</li><li>(ii) If \(A_{n}\) is a {{c1::countable}} set for each \(n \in \mathbf{N}\), then {{c5::\(\bigcup_{n=1}^{\infty}\)}} {{c4::\(A_{n}\)}} is {{c1::countable}}.</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Assume \(B\) is a {{c1::countable}} set. Thus, there exists \(f: \mathbf{N} \rightarrow B\), which is {{c4::\(1-1\)}} and {{c5::onto}}. Let \(A \subseteq B\) be an {{c2::infinite subset}} of \(B\). We must show that \(A\) is {{c3::countable}}.

Follow the steps to show that there exists a 1-1, onto function \(h: X \rightarrow Y\) and hence \(X \sim Y\).<br><br>The strategy is to partition \(X\) and \(Y\) into components<br><br>\[<br>X=A \cup A^{\prime} \quad \text { and } \quad Y=B \cup B^{\prime}<br>\]<br><br>with \(A \cap A^{\prime}=\emptyset\) and \(B \cap B^{\prime}=\emptyset\), in such a way that \(f\) maps \(A\) onto \(B\), and \(g\) maps \(B^{\prime}\) onto \(A^{\prime}\).<br><br><br>(b) Set \(A_{1}\) = {{c1::\(X \backslash g(Y)\)}} = {{c2::\(\{x \in X: x \notin g(Y)\}\)}} and inductively define a sequence of sets by letting \(A_{n+1}\)= {{c3::\(g\left(f\left(A_{n}\right)\right)\).}} Show that \(\left\{A_{n}: n \in \mathbf{N}\right\}\) is a {{c4::pairwise}} {{c5::disjoint}} collection of subsets of \(X\), while {{c5::\(\left\{f\left(A_{n}\right): n \in \mathbf{N}\right\}\)}} is a similar collection in \(Y\).

    Exercise 1.5.11 (Schrder-Bernstein Theorem). Assume there exists a 1-1 function \(f: X \rightarrow Y\) and another 1-1 function \(g: Y \rightarrow X\).     Exercise 1.5.11 (Schrder-Bernstein Theorem). Assume there exists a 1-1 function \(f: X \rightarrow Y\) and another 1-1 function \(g: Y \rightarrow X\). Follow the steps to show that there exists a 1-1, onto function \(h: X \rightarrow Y\) and hence \(X \sim Y\).<br><br>The strategy is to partition \(X\) and \(Y\) into components<br><br>\[<br>X=A \cup A^{\prime} \quad \text { and } \quad Y=B \cup B^{\prime}<br>\]<br><br>with \(A \cap A^{\prime}=\emptyset\) and \(B \cap B^{\prime}=\emptyset\), in such a way that \(f\) maps \(A\) onto \(B\), and \(g\) maps \(B^{\prime}\) onto \(A^{\prime}\).<br><br>(b) Set \(A_{1}=X \backslash g(Y)=\{x \in X: x \notin g(Y)\}\) (what happens if \(A_{1}=\emptyset\) ?) and inductively define a sequence of sets by letting \(A_{n+1}=g\left(f\left(A_{n}\right)\right)\). Show that \(f\) maps \(A\) onto \(B\).<br><br>(d) Let \(A^{\prime}\) = {{c1::\(X \backslash A\)}} and \(B^{\prime}\) = {{c2::\(Y \backslash B\)}}. Show \(g\) maps {{c3::\(B^{\prime}\)}} {{c5::onto}} {{c4::\(A^{\prime}\).}}
