    Exercise 3.2.15. A set \(A\) is called an \(F_{\sigma}\) set if it can be written as the countable union of closed sets. A set \(B\) is called a \(G_{\delta}\) set if it can be written as the countable intersection of open sets.<br><br><ul><li>(c) Show that {{c3::\(\mathbf{Q}\)}} is an {{c1::\(F_{\sigma}\)}} set, and the set of {{c5::{{c4::irrationals \(\mathbf{I}\)}}}} forms a {{c5::{{c2::\(G_{\delta}\)}}}} set.<br></li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Definition 3.3.1 ({{c2::Compactness}}). A set \(K \subseteq \mathbf{R}\) is {{c2::compact}} if every sequence in \(K\) has {{c4::a subsequence}} {{c5::{{c3::that converges to a limit}}}} that is {{c5::{{c1::also in \(K\).}}}}

============================================================

  

    Example 3.3.2. The most basic example of a compact set is a {{c1::closed}} interval. To see this, notice that if \(\left(a_{n}\right)\) is contained in an interval \([c, d]\), then the Bolzano Weierstrass Theorem guarantees that we can find a {{c3::convergent subsequence \(\left(a_{n_{k} }\right)\)}}. Because a {{c1::closed}} interval is a {{c1::closed}} set (Example 3.2.9, (ii)), we know that {{c5::{{c4::the limit of this subsequence}}}} is {{c5::{{c2::also in \([c, d]\).}}}}

============================================================

  

    What are the properties of closed intervals that we used in the preceding argument? The Bolzano-Weierstrass Theorem requires {{c1::boundedness}}, and we used the fact that {{c2::closed}} sets contain {{c5::{{c3::their limit points}}}}. As we are about to see, these two properties completely characterize {{c5::{{c4::compact}}}} sets in \(\mathbf{R}\).&nbsp;

============================================================

  

    Definition 3.3.3. A set \(A \subseteq \mathbf{R}\) is {{c1::bounded}} if {{c2::there exists \(M&gt;0\)}} such that {{c5::{{c3::\(|a|\)}}}}&nbsp; {{c5::{{c4::\(\leq\)}}}}&nbsp; {{c2::\(M\)}} for {{c5::{{c4::all \(a \in A\).}}}}

============================================================

  

    Theorem 3.3.4 (Characterization of {{c5::{{c1::Compactness}}}} in \(\mathbf{R}\) ). \(A\) set \(K\) \(\subseteq \mathbf{R}\) is {{c5::{{c1::compact}}}} if and only if it is {{c4::{{c2::closed}}}} and {{c4::{{c3::bounded}}}}.

============================================================

  

    Theorem 3.3.4 (Characterization of Compactness in \(\mathbf{R}\) ). \(A\) set \(K \subseteq \mathbf{R}\) is compact if and only if it is closed and bounded.<br><br>Proof. Let \(K\) be compact. We will first prove that \(K\) must be bounded, so assume, for contradiction, that \(K\) is not a bounded set. The idea is to produce a sequence in \(K\) that {{c1::marches off to infinity}} in such a way that it cannot have a {{c2::convergent subsequence}} as the definition of compact requires. To do this, notice that because \(K\) is not bounded there must exist an element \(x_{1} \in K\) satisfying \(\left|x_{1}\right|\) {{c5::{{c3::\(&gt;1\)}}}}. Likewise, there must exist \(x_{2} \in K\) with \(\left|x_{2}\right|\) {{c5::{{c3::\(&gt;2\)}}}}, and in general, given any \(n \in \mathbf{N}\), we can produce \(x_{n} \in K\) such that \(\left|x_{n}\right|\) &gt; {{c5::{{c3::\(n\)}}}}.<br><br>Now, because \(K\) is assumed to be compact, \(\left(x_{n}\right)\) should have a {{c2::convergent subsequence \(\left(x_{n_{k} }\right)\)}}. But the elements of the subsequence must satisfy {{c2::\(\left|x_{n_{k} }\right|\)}} {{c5::{{c4::\(&gt;\) \(n_{k}\)}}}}, and consequently {{c2::\(\left(x_{n_{k} }\right)\)}} is {{c5::{{c4::unbounded}}}}. Because convergent sequences are bounded (Theorem 2.3.2), we have a contradiction. Thus, \(K\) must at least be a bounded set.<br>

============================================================

  

    Theorem 3.3.4 (Characterization of Compactness in \(\mathbf{R}\) ). \(A\) set \(K \subseteq \mathbf{R}\) is compact if and only if it is closed and bounded.<br><br>Proof:<br>We will show that \(K\) is closed. To see that \(K\) contains its limit points, we let \(x\) = {{c1::\(\lim x_{n}\)}}, where \(\left(x_{n}\right)\) is contained in \(K\) and argue that \(x\) {{c2::must be in \(K\) as well}}. By Definition 3.3.1, the sequence \(\left(x_{n}\right)\) has a {{c3::convergent&nbsp;subsequence \(\left(x_{n_{k} }\right)\)}}, and by Theorem 2.5.2, we know {{c3::\(\left(x_{n_{k} }\right)\)}} {{c4::converges to the same limit \(x\)}}. Finally, Definition 3.3.1 requires that \(x\) {{c5::\(\in K\)}}. This proves that \(K\) is closed.

============================================================

  

    There may be a temptation to consider {{c5::{{c2::closed intervals}}}} as being a kind of standard archetype for {{c4::{{c1::compact}}}} sets, but this is misleading. The structure of {{c1::compact}} sets can be much more intricate and interesting. For instance, one implication of Theorem 3.3.4 is that the Cantor set is {{c1::compact}}. It is more useful to think of {{c1::compact}} sets as {{c4::{{c3::generalizations}}}} of {{c2::closed intervals}}. Whenever a fact involving {{c2::closed intervals}}&nbsp; is true, it is often the case that the same result holds when we replace "{{c5::{{c2::closed interval}}}}" with "{{c4::{{c1::compact set}}}}."

============================================================

  

    Theorem 3.3.5 ({{c1::Nested}}&nbsp;{{c2::Compact Set}} Property). If<br><br><ul><li>\(K_{1}\)&nbsp; {{c5::\(\supseteq\)}} \(K_{2}\)&nbsp; {{c5::\(\supseteq\)}}&nbsp; \(K_{3}\)&nbsp; {{c5::\(\supseteq\)&nbsp;}} \(K_{4}\)&nbsp; {{c5::\(\supseteq\)}}&nbsp; \(\cdots\)</li></ul><br>is a {{c1::nested sequence}} of {{c4::nonempty}} {{c2::compact sets}}, then {{c3::\(\bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty}\)}} \(K_{n}\) is {{c4::not empty.}}<br>

============================================================

  

    \section*{Theorem 3.3.5 (Nested Compact Set Property). If}<br><br>\[<br>K_{1} \supseteq K_{2} \supseteq K_{3} \supseteq K_{4} \supseteq \cdots<br>\]<br><br>is a nested sequence of nonempty compact sets, then the intersection \(\bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty} K_{n}\) is not empty.<br><br><br>Proof. In order to take advantage of the compactness of each \(K_{n}\), we are going to produce a sequence that is {{c1::eventually}} {{c2::in each of these sets}}. Thus, for each \(n \in \mathbf{N}\), pick a point \(x_{n} \in K_{n}\). Because the compact sets are nested, it follows that the sequence \(\left(x_{n}\right)\) is {{c3::contained in \(K_{1}\)}}. By Definition of compactness, \(\left(x_{n}\right)\) has a {{c5::convergent subsequence \(\left(x_{n_{k} }\right)\) }} for which \(x\) = {{c5::\(\lim x_{n_{k} }\)}} is {{c4::an element of \(K_{1}\)}}.

============================================================

  

    \section*{Theorem 3.3.5 (Nested Compact Set Property). If}<br><br>\[<br>K_{1} \supseteq K_{2} \supseteq K_{3} \supseteq K_{4} \supseteq \cdots<br>\]<br><br>is a nested sequence of nonempty compact sets, then the intersection \(\bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty} K_{n}\) is not empty.<br><br>Proof. In order to take advantage of the compactness of each \(K_{n}\), we are going to produce a sequence that is eventually in each of these sets. Thus, for each \(n \in \mathbf{N}\), pick a point \(x_{n} \in K_{n}\). Because the compact sets are nested, it follows that the sequence \(\left(x_{n}\right)\) is contained in \(K_{1}\). By Definition 3.3.1, \(\left(x_{n}\right)\) has a convergent subsequence \(\left(x_{n_{k}}\right)\) whose limit \(x=\lim x_{n_{k}}\) is an element of \(K_{1}\).<br><br>In fact, \(x\) is an element of {{c1::every \(K_{n}\)}} for essentially the same reason. Given a particular \(n_{0} \in \mathbf{N}\), the terms in the sequence \(\left(x_{n}\right)\) are contained in {{c2::\(K_{n_{0} }\)}} as long as \(n\) {{c3::\(\geq n_{0}\)}}. Ignoring the finite number of terms for which \(n_{k}\) \(&lt;n_{0}\), the same subsequence \(\left(x_{n_{k}}\right)\) is then {{c4::also contained in \(K_{n_{0} }\)}}. The conclusion is that \(x\) = {{c5::\(\lim x_{n_{k} }\) }} is an element of {{c4::\(K_{n_{0} }\).}} Because \(n_{0}\) was {{c1::arbitrary}}, \(x\) \(\in\) {{c5::\(\bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty} K_{n}\)}} and the proof is complete.

============================================================

  

    <b>Theorem 3.3.4 (Characterization of Compactness in \(\mathbf{R}\) ). \(A\) set \(K \subseteq \mathbf{R}\) is compact if and only if it is closed and bounded.</b><br><br>What this theorem implies is that we could have decided to define compact sets to be sets that are {{c1::closed}} and {{c2::bounded}}, and then proved that {{c3::sequences contained in compact sets}} have {{c4::convergent subsequences}} with {{c5::limits in the set}}.&nbsp;

============================================================

  

    <b>Definition</b> 3.3.6. Let \(A \subseteq \mathbf{R}\). An {{c1::open cover}} for \(A\) is a (possibly infinite) collection of {{c2::open}} sets \(\left\{O_{\lambda}: \lambda \in \Lambda\right\}\) {{c3::whose union contains the set \(A\);}} that is, \(A\) {{c3::\(\subseteq\)\( \bigcup_{\lambda \in \Lambda} O_{\lambda}\)}}. Given an {{c1::open cover}} for \(A\), a {{c4::finite subcover}} is a {{c4::finite subcollection}} of {{c2::open}} sets from the original {{c2::open cover}} whose {{c3::union}} manages to {{c5::completely contain \(A\).}}

============================================================

  

    <b>Definition</b> 3.3.6. Let \(A \subseteq \mathbf{R}\). An open cover for \(A\) is a (possibly infinite) collection of open sets \(\left\{O_{\lambda}: \lambda \in \Lambda\right.\) whose union contains the set \(A\); that is, \(A \subseteq \bigcup_{\lambda \in \Lambda} O_{\lambda}\). Given an open cover for \(A\), a {{c1::finite subcover}} is a {{c1::finite}} {{c4::subcollection of open sets}} from {{c5::the original open cover}} whose {{c3::union}} manages to {{c2::completely contain \(A\).}}

============================================================

  

    Example 3.3.7. Consider the open interval \((0,1)\). For each point \(x \in(0,1)\), let \(O_{x}\) be the open interval {{c2::\((x / 2,1)\)}}. Taken together, the {{c4::infinite::size?}} collection {{c4::\(\left\{O_{x}: x \in(0,1)\right\}\)}} forms an {{c3::open cover}} for the open interval \((0,1)\). Notice, however, that it is impossible to find a {{c5::finite subcover}}. Given any proposed {{c5::finite subcollection}}<br><br>\[<br>\left\{O_{x_{1}}, O_{x_{2}}, \ldots, O_{x_{n}}\right\},<br>\]<br><br>set \(x^{\prime}\) = {{c5::\(\min \left\{x_{1}, x_{2}, \ldots, x_{n}\right\}\)}} and observe that any real number \(y\) satisfying \(0 &lt;y\) {{c5::\(\leq x^{\prime} / 2\)}} is {{c5::not contained}} in {{c5::\(\bigcup_{i=1}^{n} O_{x_{i} }\)}}. As shown in the figure:<br>{{c1::<img src="paste-6ef031466db3e70f8f1ffd7c77f6f991ede74419.jpg">}}

============================================================

  

    &nbsp;For \(x \in(0,1)\), the sets \(O_{x}=(x / 2,1)\) do a fine job covering \((0,1)\), but in order to have an {{c1::open cover}} of the closed interval \([0,1]\), we must also {{c2::cover the endpoints.}} To remedy this, we could fix \(\epsilon&gt;0\), and let \(O_{0}\) = {{c3::\((-\epsilon, \epsilon)\)}} and \(O_{1}\) = {{c4::\((1-\epsilon, 1+\epsilon)\)}}. Then, the collection<br><br>{{c5::\[<br>\left\{O_{0}, O_{1}, O_{x}: x \in(0,1)\right\}<br>\]}}<br><br>is an {{c1::open cover}} for \([0,1]\).&nbsp;

============================================================

  

    For \(x \in(0,1)\), the sets \(O_{x}=(x / 2,1)\) do a fine job covering \((0,1)\), but in order to have an open cover of the closed interval \([0,1]\), we must also cover the endpoints. To remedy this, we could fix \(\epsilon&gt;0\), and let \(O_{0}=(-\epsilon, \epsilon)\) and \(O_{1}=(1-\epsilon, 1+\epsilon)\). Then, the collection<br><br>\[<br>\left\{O_{0}, O_{1}, O_{x}: x \in(0,1)\right\}<br>\]<br><br>is an open cover for \([0,1]\). But this time, notice there is a {{c1::finite subcover}}. Because of the addition of the set {{c2::\(O_{0}\)}}, we can choose \(x^{\prime}\) so that {{c5::{{c3::\(x^{\prime} /&nbsp;2\)}}}}&nbsp;&lt; \(\epsilon\). It follows that {{c5::{{c4::\(\left\{O_{0}, O_{x^{\prime} }, O_{1}\right\}\)}}}} is a {{c1::finite subcover}} for the closed interval \([0,1]\).

============================================================

  

    Theorem 3.3.8 ({{c5::<b>Heine-Borel</b>}} Theorem). Let \(K\) be a subset of \(\mathbf{R}\). All of the following statements are equivalent in the sense that any one of them implies the two others:<ul><li>(i) \(K\) is {{c1::compact}}.</li><li>(ii) \(K\) is {{c2::closed}} and {{c1::bounded}}.</li><li>(iii) Every {{c3::open cover for \(K\)}} has {{c4::a finite subcover}}.</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Theorem 3.3.8 (Heine-Borel Theorem). Let \(K\) be a subset of \(\mathbf{R}\). All of the following statements are equivalent in the sense that any one of them implies the two others:<br><ul><li>(i) \(K\) is compact.<br></li><li>(ii) \(K\) is closed and bounded.</li><li>(iii) Every open cover for \(K\) has a finite subcover.</li></ul><div>Proof: Let's first assume (iii), and prove that it implies (ii) (and thus (i) as well).</div><div><br></div><div><ul><li>To show that \(K\) is bounded, we construct an {{c1::open cover}} for \(K\) by defining \(O_{x}\) to be an {{c2::open}} interval of {{c3::radius 1}} around each point \(x \in K\). In the language of neighborhoods, \(O_{x}\) = {{c3::\(V_{1}(x)\)}}. The {{c1::open cover}} {{c1::\(\left\{O_{x}: x \in K\right\}\)::math def}} then must have a {{c4::finite subcover}} {{c4::\(\left\{O_{x_{1} }, O_{x_{2} }, \ldots, O_{x_{n} }\right\}\)::math def}}. Because \(K\) is {{c5::contained}} in a {{c5::finite union}} of {{c5::bounded sets}}, \(K\) must be bounded.</li></ul></div>

============================================================

  

    Theorem 3.3.8 (Heine-Borel Theorem). Let \(K\) be a subset of \(\mathbf{R}\). All of the following statements are equivalent in the sense that any one of them implies the two others:<br><ul><li>(i) \(K\) is compact.<br></li><li>(ii) \(K\) is closed and bounded.</li><li>(iii) Every open cover for \(K\) has a finite subcover.</li></ul><div>Proof: Let's first assume (iii), and prove that it implies (ii) (and thus (i) as well).</div><div><br></div><div><br></div><div>The proof that \(K\) is closed is more delicate, and we argue it by contradiction. Let \(\left(y_{n}\right)\) be a Cauchy sequence contained in \(K\) with \(\lim y_{n}=y\). To show that \(K\) is closed, we must demonstrate that \(y \in K\), so assume for contradiction that this is not the case. If \(y \notin K\), then every \(x \in K\) is some positive distance away from \(y\). We now construct an open cover by taking \(O_{x}\) to be an interval of radius {{c4::\(|x-y| / 2\)}} around each point \(x\) in \(K\). Because we are assuming (iii), the resulting open cover \(\left\{O_{x}: x \in K\right\}\) must have a {{c5::finite subcover}} {{c5::\(\left\{O_{x_{1} }, O_{x_{2} }, \ldots, O_{x_{n} }\right\}\)::notation}}. The contradiction arises when we realize that, in the spirit of Example 3.3.7, this finite subcover cannot {{c5::contain all of the elements of the sequence \(\left(y_{n}\right)\)}}. To make this explicit, set<br><br><ul><li>\(\epsilon_{0}\) = {{c3::\(\min \left\{\frac{\left|x_{i}-y\right|}{2}: 1 \leq i \leq n\right\}\)}}</li></ul><br>Because \(\left(y_{n}\right) \rightarrow y\), we can certainly find a term \(y_{N}\) satisfying {{c2::\(\left|y_{N}-y\right|\)}} \(&lt;\epsilon_{0}\). But such a \(y_{N}\) must necessarily be {{c1::excluded from each \(O_{x_{i} }\)}}, meaning that<br><br><ul><li>\(y_{N}\) {{c1::\(\notin\)::operator}} {{c1::\(\bigcup_{i=1}^{n} O_{x_{i} } \)::set}}</li></ul><br>Thus our supposed subcover does not {{c5::actually cover all of \(K\)}}. This contradiction implies that \(y \in K\), and hence \(K\) is closed and bounded.<br></div>

============================================================

    Theorem 3.3.8 (Heine-Borel Theorem). Let \(K\) be a subset of \(\mathbf{R}\).     Theorem 3.3.8 (Heine-Borel Theorem). Let \(K\) be a subset of \(\mathbf{R}\). Let \(\left\{O_{\lambda}: \lambda \in \Lambda\right\}\) be an open cover for \([a, b]\) and define \(S\) to be the set of {{c2::all \(x \in[a, b]\)}} such that {{c2::\([a, x]\)}} has {{c3::a finite subcover}} from \(\left\{O_{\lambda}: \lambda \in \Lambda\right\}\).<br><br><ul><li>(a) Argue that \(S\) is {{c4::nonempty}} and {{c4::bounded}}, and thus \(s\) = {{c4::\(\sup S\)}} exists.</li><li>(b) Now show \(s\) = {{c5::\(b\)}}, which implies \([a, b]\) has a {{c5::finite subcover.}}</li><li>(c) Finally, prove the theorem for an arbitrary closed and bounded set \(K\).</li></ul></div>

============================================================

  

    Exercise 3.3.12. Using the concept of {{c1::open covers}}, prove that every {{c2::bounded}} {{c5::{{c3::infinite}}}} set has a {{c5::{{c4::limit point.}}}}

============================================================

  

    One of the underlying goals of topology is to strip away all of the extraneous information that comes with our intuitive picture of the real numbers and isolate just those properties that are responsible for the phenomenon we are studying. For example, we were quick to observe that any closed interval is a compact set. The content of Theorem 3.3.4, however, is that the compactness of a closed interval has nothing to do with the fact that the set is an {{c1::interval}} but is a consequence of the set being {{c2::bounded}} and {{c3::closed}}. In Chapter 1, we argued that the set of real numbers between 0 and 1 is an uncountable set. This turns out to be the case for any {{c4::nonempty}} {{c3::closed}} set that {{c5::does not}} contain {{c5::isolated}} points.

============================================================

  

    Definition 3.4.1. A set \(P \subseteq \mathbf{R}\) is {{c1::perfect}} if it is {{c2::closed}} and contains {{c5::{{c4::no}}}} {{c5::{{c3::isolated}}}} points.

============================================================

  

    Example 3.4.2 (Cantor Set). It is not too hard to see that the Cantor set is {{c1::perfect}}. In Section 3.1, we defined the Cantor set as the intersection<br><br><ul><li>\(C=\bigcap_{n=0}^{\infty} C_{n}\)</li></ul><br>where each \(C_{n}\) is a {{c3::finite}} union of {{c2::closed intervals}}. By Theorem 3.2.14, each \(C_{n}\) is {{c2::closed}}, and by the same theorem, \(C\) is {{c2::closed}} as well. It remains to show that no point in \(C\) is {{c4::isolated}}.<br><br>Let \(x \in C\) be arbitrary. To convince ourselves that \(x\) is not {{c4::isolated}}, we must construct a sequence \(\left(x_{n}\right.\) ) of points in \(C\), different from \(x\), that converges to \(x\). From our earlier discussion, we know that \(C\) at least contains the {{c5::endpoints of the intervals}} that {{c5::make up each \(C_{n}\)}}. In Exercise 3.4.3, we sketch the argument that these are all that is needed to construct \(\left(x_{n}\right)\).<br>

============================================================

  

    Theorem 3.4.3. A {{c5::{{c1::nonempty}}}} {{c4::{{c2::perfect}}}} set is {{c4::{{c3::uncountable}}}}.

============================================================

  

    Theorem 3.4.3. A nonempty perfect set is uncountable.<br><br>Proof. If \(P\) is perfect and nonempty, then it must be {{c5::{{c1::infinite}}}} because otherwise it would consist {{c4::{{c2::only of}}}} {{c4::{{c3::isolated}}}} points.

============================================================

  

    Theorem 3.4.3. A nonempty perfect set is uncountable.<br><br>Proof. If \(P\) is perfect and nonempty, then it must be infinite because otherwise it would consist only of isolated points. Let's assume, for contradiction, that \(P\) is countable. Thus, we can write<br><ul><li>\(P\)= \(\left\{x_{1}, x_{2}, x_{3}, \ldots\right\},\)</li></ul><br>where every element of \(P\) appears on this list. The idea is to construct a sequence of {{c1::nested compact sets}} \(K_{n}\), all contained in \(P\), with the property that :<br><ul><li>{{c2::\(x_{1}\)}} {{c5::{{c4::\(\notin\)}}}} {{c5::{{c3::\(K_{2}\)}}}}, {{c2::\(x_{2}\)}} {{c4::\(\notin\)}} {{c5::{{c3::\(K_{3}\)}}}}, {{c2::\(x_{3}\)}} {{c4::\(\notin\)}} {{c5::{{c3::\(K_{4}\)}}}}, \(\ldots\).&nbsp;</li></ul><br>

    Theorem 3.4.3. A nonempty perfect set is uncountable.<br><br>Proof. If \(P\) is perfect and nonempty, then it must be infinite because otherwise it would consist only of isolated points. Let's assume, for contradiction, that \(P\) is countable. Thus, we can write<br><ul><li>\(P\)= \(\left\{x_{1}, x_{2}, x_{3}, \ldots\right\},\)</li></ul><br>where every element of \(P\) appears on this list. The idea is to construct a sequence of nested compact sets \(K_{n}\), all contained in \(P\), with the property that&nbsp;<br>\(x_{1} \notin K_{2}, x_{2} \notin K_{3}, x_{3} \notin K_{4}, \ldots\). <br><br>Some care must be taken to ensure that each \(K_{n}\) is {{c3::nonempty}}, for then we can use Theorem 3.3.5 to produce an<br><ul><li>\(x \in\){{c1::\( \bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty}\)}} {{c5::{{c2::\(K_{n}\)}}}} \(\subseteq P\)</li></ul><br>that {{c5::{{c4::cannot be on the list \(\left\{x_{1}, x_{2}, x_{3}, \ldots\right\}\)}}}}.<br>

============================================================

  

    Theorem 3.4.3. A nonempty perfect set is uncountable.<br><br>Proof. If \(P\) is perfect and nonempty, then it must be infinite because otherwise it would consist only of isolated points. Let's assume, for contradiction, that \(P\) is countable. Thus, we can write<br><ul><li>\(P\)= \(\left\{x_{1}, x_{2}, x_{3}, \ldots\right\},\)</li></ul><br>where every element of \(P\) appears on this list. The idea is to construct a sequence of nested compact sets \(K_{n}\), all contained in \(P\), with the property that&nbsp;<br>\(x_{1} \notin K_{2}, x_{2} \notin K_{3}, x_{3} \notin K_{4}, \ldots\). Some care must be taken to ensure that each \(K_{n}\) is nonempty, for then we can use Theorem 3.3.5 to produce an<br><ul><li>\(x \in \bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty} K_{n} \subseteq P\)</li></ul><br>that cannot be on the list \(\left\{x_{1}, x_{2}, x_{3}, \ldots\right\}\).<br><br>Let \(I_{1}\) be a closed interval that contains \(x_{1}\) in {{c1::its interior}} . Now, \(x_{1}\) is {{c1::not isolated}}, so there exists some other point \(y_{2} \in P\) that is {{c2::also in the interior of \(I_{1}\)}}. Construct a {{c3::closed}} interval \(I_{2}\), centered on {{c3::\(y_{2}\)}}, so that \(I_{2}\) {{c4::\(\subseteq\) \(I_{1}\)}} but \(x_{1}\) {{c4::\(\notin I_{2}\)}}. More explicitly, if \(I_{1}=[a, b]\), let<br><br><ul><li>\(\epsilon\) = {{c5::\(\min \left\{y_{2}-a, b-y_{2},\left|x_{1}-y_{2}\right|\right\} .\)}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Theorem 3.4.3. A nonempty perfect set is uncountable.<br><br>Proof. If \(P\) is perfect and nonempty, then it must be infinite because otherwise it would consist only of isolated points. Let's assume, for contradiction, that \(P\) is countable. Thus, we can write<br><ul><li>\(P\)= \(\left\{x_{1}, x_{2}, x_{3}, \ldots\right\},\)</li></ul><br>where every element of \(P\) appears on this list. The idea is to construct a sequence of nested compact sets \(K_{n}\), all contained in \(P\), with the property that&nbsp;<br>\(x_{1} \notin K_{2}, x_{2} \notin K_{3}, x_{3} \notin K_{4}, \ldots\). Some care must be taken to ensure that each \(K_{n}\) is nonempty, for then we can use Theorem 3.3.5 to produce an<br><ul><li>\(x \in \bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty} K_{n} \subseteq P\)</li></ul><br>that cannot be on the list \(\left\{x_{1}, x_{2}, x_{3}, \ldots\right\}\).<br><br>Let \(I_{1}\) be a closed interval that contains \(x_{1}\) in its interior (i.e., \(x_{1}\) is not an endpoint of \(\left.I_{1}\right)\). Now, \(x_{1}\) is not isolated, so there exists some other point \(y_{2} \in P\) that is also in the interior of \(I_{1}\). Construct a closed interval \(I_{2}\), centered on \(y_{2}\), so that \(I_{2} \subseteq I_{1}\) but \(x_{1} \notin I_{2}\). More explicitly, if \(I_{1}=[a, b]\), let<br><br><ul><li>\(\epsilon\) = \(\min \left\{y_{2}-a, b-y_{2},\left|x_{1}-y_{2}\right|\right\} .\)</li></ul><br>Then, the interval:<br><ul><li>&nbsp;\(I_{2}\) = {{c1::\(\left[y_{2}-\epsilon / 2, y_{2}+\epsilon / 2\right]\)&nbsp;}}</li></ul>has the desired properties as shown in the figure<br><br>{{c2::<img src="paste-106414706d1a272ec1b5324859bc0a9cfaef91ac.jpg">}}<br><br>This process can be continued. Because \(y_{2} \in P\) is not isolated, there must exist another point \(y_{3} \in P\) in {{c5::{{c3::the interior of \(I_{2}\)}}}}, and we may insist that \(y_{3}\) {{c5::{{c4::\(\neq x_{2}\)}}}}. Now, construct \(I_{3}\) centered on \(y_{3}\) and small enough so that \(x_{2} \notin I_{3}\) and \(I_{3} \subseteq I_{2}\). Observe that \(I_{3} \cap P \neq \emptyset\) because this intersection contains at least \(y_{3}\).<br><br>If we carry out this construction inductively, the result is a sequence of closed intervals \(I_{n}\) satisfying<br><br><ul><li>(i) \(I_{n+1}\) \(\subseteq\) \(I_{n}\),</li><li>(ii) \(x_{n}\) \(\notin\) \(I_{n+1}\)</li><li>(iii) \(I_{n}\) \(\cap P\) \(\neq\) \(\emptyset\).</li></ul><br>To finish the proof, we let \(K_{n}\) = \(I_{n} \cap P\). For each \(n \in \mathbf{N}\), we have that \(K_{n}\) is closed because it is the intersection of closed sets, and bounded because it is contained in the bounded set \(I_{n}\). Hence, \(K_{n}\) is compact. By construction, \(K_{n}\) is not empty and \(K_{n+1} \subseteq K_{n}\). Thus, we can employ the Nested Compact Set Property (Theorem 3.3.5) to conclude that the intersection<br><br><ul><li>\(\bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty} K_{n} \) \(\neq\) \(\emptyset\)</li></ul><br>But each \(K_{n}\) is a subset of \(P\), and the fact that \(x_{n} \notin I_{n+1}\) leads to the conclusion that \(\bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty} K_{n}=\emptyset\), which is the sought-after contradiction.<br>

    Theorem 3.4.3. A nonempty perfect set is uncountable.<br><br>Proof. If \(P\) is perfect and nonempty, then it must be infinite because otherwise it would consist only of isolated points. Let's assume, for contradiction, that \(P\) is countable. Thus, we can write<br><ul><li>\(P\)= \(\left\{x_{1}, x_{2}, x_{3}, \ldots\right\},\)</li></ul><br>where every element of \(P\) appears on this list. The idea is to construct a sequence of nested compact sets \(K_{n}\), all contained in \(P\), with the property that&nbsp;<br>\(x_{1} \notin K_{2}, x_{2} \notin K_{3}, x_{3} \notin K_{4}, \ldots\). Some care must be taken to ensure that each \(K_{n}\) is nonempty, for then we can use Theorem 3.3.5 to produce an<br><ul><li>\(x \in \bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty} K_{n} \subseteq P\)</li></ul><br>that cannot be on the list \(\left\{x_{1}, x_{2}, x_{3}, \ldots\right\}\).<br><br>Let \(I_{1}\) be a closed interval that contains \(x_{1}\) in its interior (i.e., \(x_{1}\) is not an endpoint of \(\left.I_{1}\right)\). Now, \(x_{1}\) is not isolated, so there exists some other point \(y_{2} \in P\) that is also in the interior of \(I_{1}\). Construct a closed interval \(I_{2}\), centered on \(y_{2}\), so that \(I_{2} \subseteq I_{1}\) but \(x_{1} \notin I_{2}\). More explicitly, if \(I_{1}=[a, b]\), let<br><br><ul><li>\(\epsilon\) = \(\min \left\{y_{2}-a, b-y_{2},\left|x_{1}-y_{2}\right|\right\} .\)</li></ul><br>Then, the interval \(I_{2}=\left[y_{2}-\epsilon / 2, y_{2}+\epsilon / 2\right]\) has the desired properties as shown in the figure<br><br><img src="paste-106414706d1a272ec1b5324859bc0a9cfaef91ac.jpg"><br><br>This process can be continued. Because \(y_{2} \in P\) is not isolated, there must exist another point \(y_{3} \in P\) in the interior of \(I_{2}\), and we may insist that \(y_{3} \neq x_{2}\). Now, construct \(I_{3}\) centered on \(y_{3}\) and small enough so that \(x_{2} \notin I_{3}\) and \(I_{3} \subseteq I_{2}\). Observe that \(I_{3} \cap P \neq \emptyset\) because this intersection contains at least \(y_{3}\).<br><br>If we carry out this construction inductively, the result is a sequence of closed intervals \(I_{n}\) satisfying<br><br><ul><li>(i) {{c2::\(I_{n+1}\)}} {{c1::\(\subseteq\)}} {{c3::\(I_{n}\)}},</li><li>(ii) {{c4::\(x_{n}\)}} {{c1::\(\notin\)}} {{c2::\(I_{n+1}\)}}</li><li>(iii) {{c3::\(I_{n}\)}} {{c5::\(\cap P\)}} {{c1::\(\neq\)}} {{c5::\(\emptyset\).}}</li></ul><br>To finish the proof, we let \(K_{n}\) = \(I_{n} \cap P\). For each \(n \in \mathbf{N}\), we have that \(K_{n}\) is closed because it is the intersection of closed sets, and bounded because it is contained in the bounded set \(I_{n}\). Hence, \(K_{n}\) is compact. By construction, \(K_{n}\) is not empty and \(K_{n+1} \subseteq K_{n}\). Thus, we can employ the Nested Compact Set Property (Theorem 3.3.5) to conclude that the intersection<br><br><ul><li>\(\bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty} K_{n} \) \(\neq\) \(\emptyset\)</li></ul><br>But each \(K_{n}\) is a subset of \(P\), and the fact that \(x_{n} \notin I_{n+1}\) leads to the conclusion that \(\bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty} K_{n}=\emptyset\), which is the sought-after contradiction.<br>

    Theorem 3.4.3. A nonempty perfect set is uncountable.<br><br>Proof. If \(P\) is perfect and nonempty, then it must be infinite because otherwise it would consist only of isolated points. Let's assume, for contradiction, that \(P\) is countable. Thus, we can write<br><ul><li>\(P\)= \(\left\{x_{1}, x_{2}, x_{3}, \ldots\right\},\)</li></ul><br>where every element of \(P\) appears on this list. The idea is to construct a sequence of nested compact sets \(K_{n}\), all contained in \(P\), with the property that&nbsp;<br>\(x_{1} \notin K_{2}, x_{2} \notin K_{3}, x_{3} \notin K_{4}, \ldots\). Some care must be taken to ensure that each \(K_{n}\) is nonempty, for then we can use Theorem 3.3.5 to produce an<br><ul><li>\(x \in \bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty} K_{n} \subseteq P\)</li></ul><br>that cannot be on the list \(\left\{x_{1}, x_{2}, x_{3}, \ldots\right\}\).<br><br>Let \(I_{1}\) be a closed interval that contains \(x_{1}\) in its interior (i.e., \(x_{1}\) is not an endpoint of \(\left.I_{1}\right)\). Now, \(x_{1}\) is not isolated, so there exists some other point \(y_{2} \in P\) that is also in the interior of \(I_{1}\). Construct a closed interval \(I_{2}\), centered on \(y_{2}\), so that \(I_{2} \subseteq I_{1}\) but \(x_{1} \notin I_{2}\). More explicitly, if \(I_{1}=[a, b]\), let<br><br><ul><li>\(\epsilon\) = \(\min \left\{y_{2}-a, b-y_{2},\left|x_{1}-y_{2}\right|\right\} .\)</li></ul><br>Then, the interval \(I_{2}=\left[y_{2}-\epsilon / 2, y_{2}+\epsilon / 2\right]\) has the desired properties as shown in the figure<br><br><img src="paste-106414706d1a272ec1b5324859bc0a9cfaef91ac.jpg"><br><br>This process can be continued. Because \(y_{2} \in P\) is not isolated, there must exist another point \(y_{3} \in P\) in the interior of \(I_{2}\), and we may insist that \(y_{3} \neq x_{2}\). Now, construct \(I_{3}\) centered on \(y_{3}\) and small enough so that \(x_{2} \notin I_{3}\) and \(I_{3} \subseteq I_{2}\). Observe that \(I_{3} \cap P \neq \emptyset\) because this intersection contains at least \(y_{3}\).<br><br>If we carry out this construction inductively, the result is a sequence of closed intervals \(I_{n}\) satisfying<br><br><ul><li>(i) \(I_{n+1}\) \(\subseteq\) \(I_{n}\),</li><li>(ii) \(x_{n}\) \(\notin\) \(I_{n+1}\)</li><li>(iii) \(I_{n}\) \(\cap P\) \(\neq\) \(\emptyset\).</li></ul><br>To finish the proof, we let \(K_{n}\) = {{c1::\(I_{n} \cap P\)}}. For each \(n \in \mathbf{N}\), we have that \(K_{n}\) is {{c2::closed}} because it is the {{c3::intersection}} of {{c2::closed}} sets, and {{c4::bounded}} because it is {{c4::contained}} in the {{c4::bounded}} set \(I_{n}\). Hence, \(K_{n}\) is {{c5::compact}}. By construction, \(K_{n}\) is not empty and \(K_{n+1} \subseteq K_{n}\). Thus, we can employ the Nested Compact Set Property (Theorem 3.3.5) to conclude that the intersection<br><br><ul><li>\(\bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty} K_{n} \) \(\neq\) \(\emptyset\)</li></ul><br>But each \(K_{n}\) is a subset of \(P\), and the fact that \(x_{n} \notin I_{n+1}\) leads to the conclusion that \(\bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty} K_{n}=\emptyset\), which is the sought-after contradiction.<br>

============================================================

  

    Theorem 3.4.3. A nonempty perfect set is uncountable.<br><br>Proof. If \(P\) is perfect and nonempty, then it must be infinite because otherwise it would consist only of isolated points. Let's assume, for contradiction, that \(P\) is countable. Thus, we can write<br><ul><li>\(P\)= \(\left\{x_{1}, x_{2}, x_{3}, \ldots\right\},\)</li></ul><br>where every element of \(P\) appears on this list. The idea is to construct a sequence of nested compact sets \(K_{n}\), all contained in \(P\), with the property that&nbsp;<br>\(x_{1} \notin K_{2}, x_{2} \notin K_{3}, x_{3} \notin K_{4}, \ldots\). Some care must be taken to ensure that each \(K_{n}\) is nonempty, for then we can use Theorem 3.3.5 to produce an<br><ul><li>\(x \in \bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty} K_{n} \subseteq P\)</li></ul><br>that cannot be on the list \(\left\{x_{1}, x_{2}, x_{3}, \ldots\right\}\).<br><br>Let \(I_{1}\) be a closed interval that contains \(x_{1}\) in its interior (i.e., \(x_{1}\) is not an endpoint of \(\left.I_{1}\right)\). Now, \(x_{1}\) is not isolated, so there exists some other point \(y_{2} \in P\) that is also in the interior of \(I_{1}\). Observe that \(I_{3} \cap P \neq \emptyset\) because this intersection contains at least \(y_{3}\).<br><br>If we carry out this construction inductively, the result is a sequence of closed intervals \(I_{n}\) satisfying<br><br><ul><li>(i) \(I_{n+1}\) \(\subseteq\) \(I_{n}\),</li><li>(ii) \(x_{n}\) \(\notin\) \(I_{n+1}\)</li><li>(iii) \(I_{n}\) \(\cap P\) \(\neq\) \(\emptyset\).</li></ul><br>To finish the proof, we let \(K_{n}\) = \(I_{n} \cap P\). Hence, \(K_{n}\) is compact. <br><br>By construction, \(K_{n}\) is not empty and \(K_{n+1}\) \(\subseteq K_{n}\). Thus, we can employ the Nested Compact Set Property (Theorem 3.3.5) to conclude that<br><br><ul><li>{{c1::\(\bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty}\)}} \(K_{n} \) {{c2::\(\neq\) \(\emptyset\)}}</li></ul><br>But each \(K_{n}\) is a {{c3::subset of \(P\)}}, and the fact that \(x_{n}\) \(\notin I_{n+1}\) leads to the conclusion that {{c4::\(\bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty} K_{n}\)}} ={{c5::\(\emptyset\)}}, which is the sought-after contradiction.<br>

============================================================

  

    Definition 3.4.4. Two nonempty sets \(A, B \subseteq \mathbf{R}\) are {{c1::separated}} if {{c3::\(\bar{A}\)}} {{c2::\(\cap\)}} {{c4::\(B\)}} and {{c4::\(A\)}} {{c2::\(\cap\)}} {{c3::\(\bar{B}\)}} are both {{c5::empty}}.&nbsp;

============================================================

  

    Definition 3.4.4. Two {{c5::nonempty}} sets \(A, B \subseteq \mathbf{R}\) are {{c4::separated}} if {{c1::\(\bar{A} \cap B\)}} and {{c1::\(A \cap \bar{B}\)}} are both {{c5::empty}}. A set \(E \subseteq \mathbf{R}\) is {{c3::disconnected}} if it can be written as \(E\)= {{c2::\(A \cup B\),}} where \(A\) and \(B\) are {{c5::nonempty}} {{c4::separated}} sets.<br><br>A set that is not {{c3::disconnected}} is called a {{c3::connected}} set.

============================================================

  

    Definition 3.4.4. Two nonempty sets \(A, B \subseteq \mathbf{R}\) are separated if \(\bar{A} \cap B\) and \(A \cap \bar{B}\) are both empty. A set \(E \subseteq \mathbf{R}\) is disconnected if it can be written as \(E=A \cup B\), where \(A\) and \(B\) are nonempty separated sets.<br><br>A set that is not disconnected is called a connected set.<br><br>Example 3.4.5. (i) If we let \(A=(1,2)\) and \(B=(2,5)\), then it is not difficult to verify that \(E\) = \((1,2) \cup(2,5)\) is {{c1::disconnected}}. Notice that the sets \(C\) = {{c2::\((1,2]\)}} and \(D\) = {{c2::\((2,5)\)}} are not {{c3::separated}} because {{c4::\(C \cap \bar{D}\)}} = {{c4::\(\{2\}\)}} is {{c4::not empty}}. This should be comforting. The {{c4::union \(C \cup D\)}} is equal to the interval {{c5::\((1,5)\)}}, which better not qualify as a {{c1::disconnected}} set.&nbsp;

============================================================

  

    Definition 3.4.4. Two nonempty sets \(A, B \subseteq \mathbf{R}\) are separated if \(\bar{A} \cap B\) and \(A \cap \bar{B}\) are both empty. A set \(E \subseteq \mathbf{R}\) is disconnected if it can be written as \(E=A \cup B\), where \(A\) and \(B\) are nonempty separated sets.<br><br>A set that is not disconnected is called a connected set.<br><br>Example 3.4.5. (i) If we let \(A=(1,2)\) and \(B=(2,5)\), then it is not difficult to verify that \(E=(1,2) \cup(2,5)\) is disconnected. Notice that the sets \(C=(1,2]\) and \(D=(2,5)\) are not separated because \(C \cap \bar{D}=\{2\}\) is not empty. This should be comforting. The union \(C \cup D\) is equal to the {{c1::interval}} \((1,5)\), which better not qualify as a disconnected set. We will prove in a moment that every {{c1::interval}} is a {{c2::connected}} subset of {{c5::{{c3::\(\mathbf{R}\)}}}} and {{c5::{{c4::vice versa.}}}}

============================================================

  

    (ii) Let's show that the set of rational numbers is disconnected. If we let<br><ul><li>\(A\) = \(\mathbf{Q}\) \(\cap\) {{c1::\( (-\infty, \sqrt{2})\)&nbsp;}}</li><li>\(B\) = \(\mathbf{Q}\) \(\cap\) {{c2::\((\sqrt{2}, \infty)\)}}</li></ul><br>then we certainly have \(\mathbf{Q}\) = {{c3::\(A \cup B\)}}. The fact that \(A\) \(\subseteq\) {{c1::\((-\infty, \sqrt{2})\)}} implies (by the Order Limit Theorem) that any limit point of \(A\) will necessarily fall in {{c4::\((-\infty, \sqrt{2}]\)}}. Because this is {{c5::disjoint}} from \(B\), we get {{c5::\(\bar{A} \cap B\)}} = {{c4::\(\emptyset\).}} We can similarly show that {{c5::\(A \cap \bar{B}\)}} = {{c4::\(\emptyset\)}}, which implies that \(A\) and \(B\) are {{c5::separated}}.<br>

============================================================

  

    &nbsp;Essentially, a set \(E\) is {{c1::connected}} if, no matter how it is partitioned into two {{c5::nonempty}} {{c5::disjoint}} sets, it is always possible to show that {{c4::at least one of the sets}} contains {{c2::a limit point}} of {{c3::the other.}}

============================================================

  

    <b>Theorem</b> 3.4.6. \(A\) set \(E\)&nbsp; \(\subseteq \mathbf{R}\) is {{c4::connected}} if and only if, for all {{c5::nonempty}} {{c5::disjoint}} sets \(A\) and \(B\) satisfying \(E\) = {{c5::\(A \cup B\)}}, there always exists a {{c1::convergent sequence}} {{c1::\(\left(x_{n}\right) \rightarrow x\)::notation}} with {{c1::\(\left(x_{n}\right)\)}} contained in {{c2::one of \(A\) or \(B\)}}, and {{c1::\(x\)}} {{c3::an element of the other.}}

============================================================

  

    The concept of {{c5::connectedness}} is more relevant when working with {{c2::subsets of the plane}} and other {{c1::higher-dimensional}} spaces. This is because, in \(\mathbf{R}\), the {{c5::connected}} sets coincide precisely with the collection of {{c3::intervals}} (with the understanding that {{c4::unbounded}}&nbsp; are included).

============================================================

  

    <b>Theorem</b> 3.4.7. A set \(E \subseteq \mathbf{R}\) is {{c4::connected}} if and only if whenever \(a\) {{c3::&lt;}} \(c\) {{c3::&lt;}} \(b\) with \(a, b\) {{c5::{{c2::\(\in E\)}}}}, it follows that \(c\) {{c5::{{c1::\(\in E\)}}}}.

============================================================

  

    Theorem 3.4.7. A set \(E \subseteq \mathbf{R}\) is connected if and only if whenever \(a&lt;c&lt;b\) with \(a, b \in E\), it follows that \(c \in E\) as well.<br><br>Proof. Assume \(E\) is {{c3::connected}}, and let \(a, b \in E\) and \(a\) {{c1::&lt;}} \(c\) {{c1::&lt;}} \(b\). Set<br><br><ul><li>\(A\) = {{c1::\((-\infty, c)\)}} {{c2::\(\cap E \)&nbsp;}}</li><li>\(B\) = {{c2::\((c, \infty)\)}} {{c1::\(\cap E\)&nbsp;}}</li></ul><br>Because \(a \in A\) and \(b \in B\), neither set is {{c4::empty}} and, just as in Example 3.4.5 (ii), neither set {{c4::contains a limit point of the other}}. If \(E\) = {{c5::\(A \cup B\)}}, then we would have that \(E\) is {{c5::disconnected}}, which it is not. It must then be that {{c5::\(A \cup B\)}} is missing some element of \(E\), and {{c5::\(c\)}} is the only possibility. Thus, {{c5::\(c \in E\).<br>}}<br>

============================================================

  

    Theorem 3.4.7. A set \(E \subseteq \mathbf{R}\) is connected if and only if whenever \(a&lt;c&lt;b\) with \(a, b \in E\), it follows that \(c \in E\) as well.<br><br>Proof. Assume \(E\) is connected, and let \(a, b \in E\) and \(a&lt;c&lt;b\). Set<br><br>\[<br>A=(-\infty, c) \cap E \quad \text { and } \quad B=(c, \infty) \cap E \text {. }<br>\]<br><br><ul><li>Conversely, assume that \(E\) is an interval in the sense that whenever \(a, b \in E\) satisfy \(a&lt;c&lt;b\) for some \(c\), then \(c \in E\).&nbsp;</li><li>Our intent is to use the characterization of connected sets in Theorem 3.4.6, so let \(E\) = \(A \cup B\), where \(A\) and \(B\) are nonempty and disjoint.&nbsp;</li><li>We need to show that one of these sets {{c1::contains a limit point of the other.&nbsp;}}</li><li>Pick \(a_{0} \in A\) and \(b_{0} \in B\), and, for the sake of the argument, assume \(a_{0}\) {{c1::&lt;}} \(b_{0}\).&nbsp;</li><li>Because \(E\) is itself an interval, the interval \(I_{0}\) = {{c2::\(\left[a_{0}, b_{0}\right]\)}} is contained in \(E\). Now, {{c3::bisect}} \(I_{0}\) {{c3::into two equal halves}}.&nbsp;</li><li>The {{c3::midpoint}} of \(I_{0}\) must {{c3::either be in \(A\) or \(B\)}}, and so choose \(I_{1}\) = {{c2::\(\left[a_{1}, b_{1}\right]\)}} to be the {{c3::half}} that allows us to have {{c3::\(a_{1} \in A\)}} and {{c3::\(b_{1} \in B\).&nbsp;}}</li><li>Continuing this process yields a sequence of {{c2::nested}} intervals \(I_{n}\) = {{c2::\(\left[a_{n}, b_{n}\right]\)}}, where {{c3::\(a_{n} \in A, b_{n} \in B\)}}, and {{c4::\(\left(b_{n}-a_{n}\right)\)}}&nbsp; \(\rightarrow\) {{c4::\(0\)}}. The remainder of this argument should feel familiar. By the Nested Interval Property, there exists an</li><ul><li>\(x\) \(\in\) {{c5::\(\bigcap_{n=0}^{\infty}\)&nbsp; \(I_{n}\)}}</li></ul><li>and it is straightforward to show that the sequences of endpoints each satisfy {{c5::\(\lim a_{n}\)}} = \(x\) and {{c5::\(\lim b_{n}\)}} = \(x\). But now \(x \in E\) must {{c5::belong to either \(A\) or \(B\)}}, thus making it a {{c5::limit point of the other}}. This completes the argument.</li></ul>

============================================================

    The structure of {{c5::{{c3::open}}}} sets is fairly straightforward. Every {{c3::open}} set is either a {{c4::{{c1::finite}}}} or {{c4::{{c1::countable}}}} {{c4::{{c2::union of open intervals}}}}. Standing in opposition&nbsp; to this tidy description of all {{c3::open}} sets is the Cantor set. The Cantor set is a {{c5::{{c3::closed}}}}, {{c4::{{c1::uncountable}}}} set that contains {{c4::{{c2::no intervals of any kind}}}}.&nbsp;

============================================================

  

    Definition 3.5.1:<br><ul><li>&nbsp;A set \(A \subseteq \mathbf{R}\) is called an {{c1::\(F_{\sigma}\)}} set if it can be written as the {{c2::countable}} {{c3::union}} of {{c5::closed}} sets.&nbsp;</li><li>A set \(B \subseteq \mathbf{R}\) is called a {{c1::\(G_{\delta}\)}} set if it can be written as the {{c2::countable}} {{c4::intersection}} of {{c5::open}} sets.</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Definition 3.5.1:<br><ul><li>&nbsp;A set \(A \subseteq \mathbf{R}\) is called an {{c1::\(F_{\sigma}\)}} set if it can be written as the {{c2::countable}} {{c5::{{c3::union}}}} of {{c5::{{c4::closed}}}} sets.&nbsp;</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Definition 3.5.1:<br><ul><li>A set \(B \subseteq \mathbf{R}\) is called a {{c1::\(G_{\delta}\)}} set if it can be written as the {{c2::countable}} {{c5::{{c3::intersection}}}} of {{c5::{{c4::open}}}} sets.<br></li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Definition 3.5.1. A set \(A \subseteq \mathbf{R}\) is called an \(F_{\sigma}\) set if it can be written as the countable union of closed sets. A set \(B \subseteq \mathbf{R}\) is called a \(G_{\delta}\) set if it can be written as the countable intersection of open sets.<br><br>Exercise 3.5.1. Argue that a set \(A\) is a {{c5::{{c1::\(G_{\delta}\)}}}} set if and only if its {{c4::{{c3::complement}}}} is an {{c4::{{c2::\(F_{\sigma}\)}}}} set.

    Exercise 3.5.3.     Definition 3.5.1. A set \(A \subseteq \mathbf{R}\) is called an \(F_{\sigma}\) set if it can be written as the countable union of closed sets. A set \(B \subseteq \mathbf{R}\) is called a \(G_{\delta}\) set if it can be written as the countable intersection of open sets.<br><br>It is not readily obvious that the class {{c1::\(F_{\sigma}\)}} does not include every subset of \(\mathbf{R}\), but we are now ready to argue that {{c4::\(\mathbf{I}\)}} is not an {{c1::\(F_{\sigma}\)}} set (and consequently {{c5::{{c3::\(\mathbf{Q}\)}}}} is not a {{c5::{{c2::\(G_{\delta}\)}}}} set). This will follow from a theorem due to Ren Louis Baire (1874-1932).

============================================================

  

    <b>Theorem</b> 3.5.2. If \(\left\{G_{1}, G_{2}, G_{3}, \ldots\right\}\) is a {{c3::countable}} collection of {{c4::dense}}, {{c4::open}} sets, then the {{c5::{{c2::intersection \(\bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty}\)}}}} \(G_{n}\) is {{c5::{{c1::not empty.}}}}

============================================================

  

    Theorem 3.5.2. If \(\left\{G_{1}, G_{2}, G_{3}, \ldots\right\}\) is a countable collection of dense, open sets, then the intersection \(\bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty} G_{n}\) is not empty.<br><br>Proof. Before embarking on the proof, notice that we have seen a conclusion like this before. Theorem 3.3.5 asserts that a {{c1::nested}} {{c2::sequence}} of {{c3::compact}} sets has a {{c4::nontrivial}} {{c5::intersection}}. In this theorem, we are dealing with {{c3::dense}}, {{c3::open}} sets, but as it turns out, we are going to use Theorem 3.3.5-and actually, just the {{c5::Nested Interval}} Property - as the crucial step in the argument.

============================================================

  

    Theorem 3.5.2. If \(\left\{G_{1}, G_{2}, G_{3}, \ldots\right\}\) is a countable collection of dense, open sets, then the intersection \(\bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty} G_{n}\) is not empty.<br><br>Exercise 3.5.4.     Exercise 3.5.5. ============================================================

  

    We have encountered several equivalent ways to assert that a particular set \(G\) is {{c1::dense}} in \(\mathbf{R}\). In Section 3.2, we observed that \(G\) is {{c1::dense}} in \(\mathbf{R}\) if and only if every point of \(\mathbf{R}\) is a {{c2::limit}} point of \(G\). Because the {{c1::closure}} of any set is obtained by {{c5::taking the union}} of {{c5::the set}} and {{c5::its limit points}}, we have that<br><br><ul><li>\(G\) is {{c1::dense}} in&nbsp; \(\mathbf{R}\) iff {{c4::\(\bar{G}\)}}= {{c3::\(\mathbf{R} \text {. }\)}}</li></ul><br>The set \(\mathbf{Q}\) is {{c1::dense}} in \(\mathbf{R}\); the set \(\mathbf{Z}\) is clearly not. In fact, in the jargon of analysis, \(\mathbf{Z}\) is {{c5::nowhere}}-{{c1::dense}} in \(\mathbf{R}\).<br>

============================================================

  

    Definition 3.5.3. A set \(E\) is {{c1::nowhere}}-{{c2::dense}} if {{c3::\(\bar{E}\)}} contains {{c1::no}} {{c5::nonempty}} {{c4::open}} intervals.

============================================================

  

    Exercise 3.5.8. Show that a set \(E\) is {{c4::nowhere}}-{{c3::dense}} in \(\mathbf{R}\) if and only if {{c5::{{c1::the complement}}}} of {{c5::{{c2::\(\bar{E}\)}}}} is {{c3::dense}} in \(\mathbf{R}\).

============================================================

  

    Theorem 3.5.4 ({{c5::Baire's}} Theorem). The set of {{c5::real}} numbers {{c5::\(\mathbf{R}\)}} {{c4::cannot be written}} as the {{c2::countable}} {{c3::union}} of {{c1::nowhere-dense}} sets.

============================================================

  

    {{c1::Baire's}} Theorem is yet another statement about the size of \(\mathbf{R}\). We have already encountered several ways to describe the sizes of infinite sets. In terms of {{c2::cardinality}}, {{c5::{{c3::countable}}}} sets are {{c5::{{c4::relatively small}}}} whereas {{c5::{{c3::uncountable}}}} sets are {{c5::{{c4::large}}}}.&nbsp;

============================================================

  

    We also briefly discussed the concept of "length," or "measure," in Section 3.1. Baire's Theorem offers a third perspective. From this point of view, {{c2::nowhere-dense}} sets are considered to be "{{c1::thin}}" sets. Any set that is the {{c3::countable}} {{c4::union}} - i.e., a {{c3::not very large}} {{c4::union}} - of these small sets is called a "{{c5::meager}}" set or a set of "{{c5::first}} category." A set that is not of {{c5::first}} category is of "{{c5::second}} category." Intuitively, sets of the second category are the "{{c3::fat}}" subsets. The Baire Category Theorem, as it is often called, states that \(\mathbf{R}\) is of second category.

============================================================

  

    <ul><li>There is a significance to the Baire Category Theorem that is difficult to appreciate at the moment because we are only seeing a special case of this result.&nbsp;</li><li>The real numbers are an example of a {{c1::complete}} {{c2::metric}} space.&nbsp;</li><li>{{c2::Metric}} spaces are discussed in some detail in Section 8.2, but here is the basic idea.&nbsp;</li><li>Given a set of mathematical objects such as real numbers, points in the plane or continuous functions defined on \([0,1]\), a "{{c2::metric}}" is a rule that assigns a "{{c3::distance}}" between two elements in the set. In \(\mathbf{R}\), we have been using {{c4::\(|x-y|\)}} as the {{c3::distance}} between the real numbers \(x\) and \(y\).&nbsp;</li><li>The point is that if we can create a satisfactory notion of "{{c3::distance}}" on these other spaces (we will need the {{c5::triangle inequality}} to hold, for instance), then the concepts of convergence, Cauchy sequences, and open sets, for example, can be naturally transferred over.&nbsp;</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    A {{c3::complete}} {{c4::metric}} space is any set with a suitably defined metric in which {{c1::Cauchy sequences}} have {{c2::limits}}. We have spent a good deal of time discussing the fact that {{c5::\(\mathbf{R}\)}} is a {{c3::complete}} {{c4::metric}} space whereas {{c5::\(\mathbf{Q}\)}} is not.

============================================================

  

    The Baire Category Theorem in its more general form states that any {{c1::complete}} {{c2::metric}} space must be {{c3::too large}} to be the {{c4::countable}} {{c5::union}} of {{c4::nowhere-dense}} subsets.&nbsp;

============================================================

  

    <ul><li>One particularly interesting example of a {{c2::complete}} {{c2::metric}} space is the set of continuous functions defined on the interval \([0,1]\).&nbsp;</li><li>(The distance between two functions \(f\) and \(g\) in this space is defined to be {{c1::\(\sup |f(x)-g(x)|\)}}, where \(x \in[0,1]\).)&nbsp;</li><li>Now, in this space we will see that the collection of continuous functions that are differentiable {{c3::at even one point}} can be written as the {{c3::countable}} {{c3::union}} of {{c4::nowhere-dense}} sets.&nbsp;</li><li>Thus, a fascinating consequence of Baire's Theorem in this setting is that most continuous functions {{c5::do not have}} derivatives {{c5::at any point}}.&nbsp;</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    This odd situation mirrors the roles of \(\mathbf{Q}\) and \(\mathbf{I}\) as subsets of \(\mathbf{R}\). Just as the familiar {{c1::rational}} numbers constitute a {{c2::minute}} proportion of the real line, the {{c3::differentiable}} functions of calculus are {{c4::exceedingly atypical}} of {{c5::continuous}} functions in general.

============================================================

  

    {{c1::Constructable}} numbers (in a {{c2::geometric}} sense) are always {{c5::{{c3::algebraic}}}} and not {{c5::{{c4::transcendental}}}}

============================================================

  

    If {{c3::A is in B}} and {{c4::B is not in A}} then A is a {{c5::{{c1::proper}}}} {{c5::{{c2::subset}}}} of B

============================================================

  

    For sets A and B, if {{c5::{{c1::A is in B}}}} and {{c4::{{c2::B is in A}}}} then {{c4::{{c3::A = B}}}}

============================================================

  

    What is an equivalence relation \(R\) for a set \(S\)?<br><ul><li>\(R\) is a subset of {{c5::\(S \times S\)}}, formed of {{c5::pairs \((a,b)\)}}, such that it is:</li></ul><ol><ol><li>{{c1::Reflexive}}: {{c2::\(aRa\)}}</li><li>{{c2::Symetric}}: {{c1::\(aRb\)}} =&nbsp; {{c1::\(bRa\)}}</li><li>{{c3::Transitive}}:&nbsp;{{c1::\(aRb\)}}&nbsp;{{c4::\( \land \)}} {{c3::\(bRc\)}}&nbsp;{{c4::\(\implies\)}}&nbsp;{{c3::\(aRc\)}}</li></ol></ol>

============================================================

  

    1. (Abbott 1.2.1(b)) Where does the proof in Theorem 1.1.1 (of the book) breakdown if we try to use it to prove \(\sqrt{4}\) is irrational.<br><br>Solution. We could start by assuming that \((p / q)^{2}=4\) where \(p, q \in \mathbb{Z}\) have no common factor. This implies that \(p^{2}=4 q^{2}\). If we proceed in the same manner of the book we would want to argue that if {{c1::4 divides \(p^{2}\)}} then {{c2::4 divides \(p\)}}, but this is not true. So the same proof does not work.<br><br>We could try to deviate from the proof just slightly. Notice that \(p^{2}=4 q^{2}=2 \cdot 2 q^{2}\) implies that \(p\) = \(2 r\) is even. Thus, \((2 r)^{2}\) = {{c5::{{c4::\(4 q^{2} \Longrightarrow r^{2}=q^{2} \Longrightarrow r= \pm q\)}}}}. We have shown that \(r\) divides \(p\) and \(r\) divides \(q\). We conclude \(r\) = {{c5::{{c3::\(1\)}}}} and therefore \(p\) = {{c5::{{c3::\(2\)}}}} and \(q\) = {{c3::\(1\)}}. Of course this is true: \((2 / 1)^{2}=4\).

============================================================

  

    2. (Abbott 1.2.4) Produce an infinite collection of sets \(\left\{A_{n}\right\}_{n \in \mathbb{N}}\) with the properties that<br><br>(a) Every \(A_{n}\) has an infinite number of elements<br><br>(b) \(A_{m} \cap A_{n}=\emptyset\) for all \(i \neq j\)<br><br>(c) \(\cup_{n=1}^{\infty} A_{n}=\mathbb{N}\).<br><br><ul><li>Example 1:</li><ul><li>\(A_i\) = {{c1::all numbers with i prime factors}}<br></li></ul><li>Example 2: semi-ordered:</li><ul><li>\(A_i\) ={{c2::&nbsp;\(\{ (\prod_{k=1}^{i-1} p_k^{x_k}) p_{i}^{x_i +1}, \forall \vec{x} \in N^{i} \}\)}} which means all numbers formed of {{c3::the first i prime factors}} where {{c4::the most recent factor}} has to {{c5::have power 1 at least}}<br></li></ul></ul>

============================================================

  

    3. (Abbott 1.2.6 (a)(d))<br><br>(a) Verify the triangle inequality: \(|a+b| \leq|a|+|b|\), in the special case where both \(a\) and \(b\) have the same sign.<br><br>(d) Prove that ||\(a|-| b|| \leq|a-b|\).<br><br>(d) Notice that \(a\) = {{c1::\((a-b)+b\)}}. It follows that<br><br>{{c2::\[<br>\begin{aligned}<br>|a| &amp; =|(a-b)+b| \\<br>&amp; \leq|a-b|+|b| \\<br>\Longrightarrow \quad|a|-|b| &amp; \leq|a-b| .<br>\end{aligned}<br>\]}}<br><br>Likewise \(b\) = {{c5::{{c3::\((b-a)+a\)}}}} and similarly<br><br>{{c5::{{c4::\[<br>\begin{aligned}<br>|b| &amp; =|(b-a)+a| \\<br>&amp; \leq|b-a|+|a| \\<br>\Longrightarrow \quad|b|-|a| &amp; \leq|a-b| .<br>\end{aligned}<br>\]}}}}<br><br>We conclude that ||\(a|-| b|| \leq|a-b|\).

    4. (Abbott 1.2.8) Give an example of a function in each of the following cases.<br><br>(a) \(f: \mathbb{N} \rightarrow \mathbb{N}\) that is \(1-1\) but not onto.<br><br>(b) \(f: \mathbb{N} \rightarrow \mathbb{N}\) that is onto but not \(1-1\).<br><br>(c) \(f: \mathbb{N} \rightarrow \mathbb{Z}\) that is \(1-1\) and onto.<br><br><ul><li>For a: f(n) = {{c1::2n}}</li><li>For b: f(n)= {{c2::\[<br>\left\{\begin{array}{ll}<br>j &amp; \text { if } n=2 j \\<br>j &amp; \text { if } n=2 j-1<br>\end{array} .\right.<br>\]}}</li><ul><li>If \(j \in \mathbb{N}\) then \(f(2 j)\) = {{c5::{{c4::\(j\)}}}} thus \(f\) is onto. However, {{c5::{{c4::\(f(1)=f(2)=1\)}}}} thus \(f\) is not one to one.<br></li></ul><li>For c: f(n)= {{c5::{{c3::\[<br>\left\{\begin{array}{ll}<br>-n / 2 &amp; \text { if } n \text { is even } \\<br>(n-1) / 2 &amp; \text { if } n \text { is odd }<br>\end{array} .\right.<br>\]}}}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    <br>4. (Abbott 1.2.8) Give an example of a function in each of the following cases.<br><br>(a) \(f: \mathbb{N} \rightarrow \mathbb{N}\) that is \(1-1\) but not onto.<br><br>(b) \(f: \mathbb{N} \rightarrow \mathbb{N}\) that is onto but not \(1-1\).<br><br>(c) \(f: \mathbb{N} \rightarrow \mathbb{Z}\) that is \(1-1\) and onto.<br><br><ul><li>For c:</li><ul><li>\[<br>f(n)=\left\{\begin{array}{ll}<br>-n / 2 &amp; \text { if } n \text { is even } \\<br>(n-1) / 2 &amp; \text { if } n \text { is odd }<br>\end{array} .\right.<br>\]</li><li>Notice that \(f(1)=-1, f(2)=0, f(3)=-2, f(4)=1\) and so forth. The idea is that \(f\) is mapping the even natural numbers to negative integers and the odd natural numbers to the positive integers including zero.</li><li>Let \(k \in \mathbb{Z}\). If \(k \geq 0\) then \(f(2 k+1)\) = {{c1::\(((2 k+1)-1) / 2\)}} = {{c2::\(k\)}}.&nbsp;</li><li>If \(k&lt;0\) then \(-2 k \in \mathbb{N}\) and \(f(-2 k)\) = {{c3::\(-(-2 k) / 2\)}} = {{c2::\(k\)}}. Thus, \(f\) is onto.</li><li>Suppose \(f(n)\) = \(f(m)\). Then, \(m\) and \(n\) must be {{c4::of the same sign}}, otherwise {{c5::their images wouldn't match parity}}. So either \(-m / 2=-n / 2\) or \((n-1) / 2=(m-1) / 2\). In both cases we find with a little algebra that \(m\) = \(n\). Thus \(f\) is one to one.<br></li></ul></ul>

    6. Given a function \(f: A \rightarrow B\) and a subset \(C \subset B\). The preimage of \(C\) is defined as \(f^{-1}(C)=\{a \in A\) : \(f(a) \in C\}\). Notice that \(f^{-1}(C) \subset A\).<br><br>(a) Let \(X \subset A\). Show that \(X \subset f^{-1}(f(X))\).<br><br>(b) Let \(Y \subset B\). How are \(f\left(f^{-1}(Y)\right)\) and \(Y\) generally related?<br><br><ul><li>b){{c5::{{c3::\(f\left(f^{-1}(Y)\right)\)}}}}{{c4::{{c2::&nbsp; \(\subset\)}}}} {{c4::{{c1::\(Y\).}}}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    \section*{7. (Abbott 1.3.3)}<br><br>(a) Let \(A\) be non-empty and bounded below and define \(B=\{b \in \mathbb{R}: b\) is a lower bound for \(A\}\). Show that \(\sup B=\inf A\).<br><br>(a) Notice that by construction \(B\) is non-empty and bounded above by any element of the set \(A\). By completeness, we can let \(s=\sup B\), that is, \(s\) is an upper bound for \(B\) and if \(b\) is an upper bound then \(s \leq b\). (We want to show \(s=\inf A\) ).<br><br>First, we show \(s\) is a lower bound for \(A\). Let \(a \in A\). By definition of \(B\), we have that \(b\) {{c1::\(\leq a\)}} for all \(b \in B\). Therefore, {{c1::\(a\)}} is an {{c2::upper bound}} for \(B\). Since \(s\) is the least upper bound for \(B\) we have that \(s\) {{c3::\(\leq a\)}}. This shows that \(s\) is a {{c4::lower bound}} for \(A\).<br><br>Next, we show that \(s\) is the greatest lower bound for \(A\). Let \(l\) be a lower bound for \(A\). It follows that \(l \in B\). Since \(s\) is an upper bound for \(B\) we have that \(l\) {{c5::\(\leq s\)}}. Therefore, \(s\) is the {{c5::greatest}} {{c4::lower bound}} and \(s=\inf A\).

============================================================

  

    8. (Abbott 1.3.4) Let \(\left\{A_{n}\right\}\) be a collection of non-empty and bounded above sets.<br><br>(a) Find a formula for \(\sup \left(A_{1} \cup A_{2}\right)\) and extend this to \(\sup \left(\bigcup_{n=1}^{N} A_{n}\right)\).<br><ul><li>Fromula is \(\sup \left(\bigcup_{n=1}^{N} A_{n}\right)\) =&nbsp;{{c1::\(\max(\sup A_1,\ldots, \sup A_n)\)}}</li><li>Prove base case:</li><ul><li>Let \(a \in A_{1} \cup A_{2}\). Then, \(a \in A_{1}\) or \(a \in A_{2}\) which implies that \(a\) {{c2::\(\leq \sup A_{1}\)}} or \(a\) {{c2::\(\leq \sup A_{2}\)}}. Thus, \(a\) {{c1::\(\leq \max \left(\sup A_{1}, \sup A_{2}\right)\)}} and {{c1::\(\max \left(\sup A_{1}, \sup A_{2}\right)\)}} is an upper bound for \(A_{1} \cup A_{2}\).</li><li>Let \(b\) be an upper bound for \(A_{1} \cup A_{2}\). Then, \(b\) is an upper bound for \(A_{1}\) and \(A_{2}\). It follows that \(\sup A_{1} \) {{c5::{{c3::\(\leq b\)}}}} and \(\sup A_{2}\)&nbsp; {{c3::\(\leq b\)}}. Therefore, {{c1::\(\max \left(\sup A_{1}, \sup A_{2}\right)\)}}&nbsp; {{c3::\(\leq b\)}} and {{c1::\(\max \left(\sup A_{1}, \sup A_{2}\right)\)}} is the {{c5::{{c4::least upper bound.}}}}<br></li></ul></ul>

============================================================

(Abbott 1.3.4) Let \(\left\{A_{n}\right\}\) be a collection of non-empty and bounded above sets.<br><br>(a) Find a formula for \(\sup \left(A_{1} \cup A_{2}\right)\) and extend this to \(\sup \left(\bigcup_{n=1}^{N} A_{n}\right)\).<br><br>(b) Does your formula above extend to the infinite case.<br><br><ul><li>Answer: No</li><li>(b) Consider a sequence of non-empty and bounded above sets \(A_{n}\) = {{c1::\([n, n+1]\).}} In this case, the formula does not hold since {{c2::\(\bigcup_{n=1}^{\infty} A_{n}=[1, \infty)\)}} is {{c5::{{c3::not bounded above.<br>}}}}<br></li><li>Another example where the formula fails is for the sequence \(A_{n}\) = {{c5::{{c4::\(\left[0,1-\frac{1}{n}\right]\).}}}}<br></li></ul>

    \section*{9. (Abbott 1.3.6)}<br><br>(a) Let \(s=\sup A\) and \(t=\sup B\). Show that \(s+t\) is an upper bound for \(A+B\).<br><br>(a) Let \(a+b \in A+B\) where \(a \in A\) and \(b \in B\). Then, {{c3::\(a\)}} {{c1::\(\leq s\)}} and {{c3::\(b\)}} {{c2::\( \leq t\)}}. It follows that {{c5::\(a+b\)}} {{c4::\(\leq s+t\)}}.

    \section*{9. (Abbott 1.3.6)}<br>(a) Let \(s=\sup A\) and \(t=\sup B\). Show that \(s+t\) is an upper bound for \(A+B\).<br><br>(b) Let \(u\) be an upper bound for \(A+B\) and fix \(a \in A\). Show that \(t \leq u-a\).<br><br>(b) Let \(b \in B\). Since \(u\) is an upper for \(A+B\) then \(a+b\) {{c1::\(\leq u\).}} Thus, \(b\)&nbsp; {{c2::\(\leq u-a\)}}. Since \(b\) was {{c3::arbitrary}}, this shows that {{c2::\(u-a\)}} is an {{c4::upper bound for \(B\)}}. By the {{c4::least upper bound}} definition for \(B\), \(t\) {{c5::\(\leq u-a\).}}

============================================================

  

    \section*{9. (Abbott 1.3.6)}<br><br>(a) Let \(s=\sup A\) and \(t=\sup B\). Show that \(s+t\) is an upper bound for \(A+B\).<br><br>(b) Let \(u\) be an upper bound for \(A+B\) and fix \(a \in A\). Show that \(t \leq u-a\).<br><br>(c) Show that \(\sup A+B=s+t\).<br><br>(c) From (b) we have that \(t\)&nbsp; {{c1::\(\leq u-a\)}} \(\Longrightarrow\) \(a\) {{c2::\(\leq u-t\)}}. Since \(a\) was {{c3::arbitrary}} we have, by the {{c4::least upper bound definition for \(A\)}}, that \(s\) {{c5::\(\leq u-t\)}} \(\Longrightarrow\) {{c5::\(s+t\)}}&nbsp; \(\leq u\), where \(u\) was an upper bound. Therefore, \(s+t=\sup A+B\) is the least upper bound.

    \section*{9. (Abbott 1.3.6)}<br><br>Lemma 1.3.8. Assume \(s \in \mathbf{R}\) is an upper bound for a set \(A \subseteq \mathbf{R}\). Then, \(s=\sup A\) if and only if, for every choice of \(\epsilon&gt;0\), there exists an element \(a \in A\) satisfying \(s-\epsilon&lt;a\).<br><br>(a) Let \(s=\sup A\) and \(t=\sup B\). Show that \(s+t\) is an upper bound for \(A+B\).<br><br>(b) Let \(u\) be an upper bound for \(A+B\) and fix \(a \in A\). Show that \(t \leq u-a\).<br><br>(c) Show that \(\sup A+B=s+t\).<br><br>(d) Find another proof of (c) using Lemma 1.3.8.<br><br>(d) Let \(\epsilon&gt;0\) be given. Then, by Lemma 1.3 .8 there are elements \(a \in A\) and \(b \in B\) such that {{c1::\(s-\epsilon / 2\)}} &lt; {{c2::\(a\)}} and {{c3::\(t-\epsilon / 2\)}} &lt; {{c4::\(b\)}}. Adding the equations together we find that<br><br><ul><li>{{c5::\((s+t)-\epsilon\)}} &lt; {{c5::\(a+b\)}}</li></ul><br>Therefore, applying Lemma 1.3.8, we conclude that \(s+t=\sup A+B\).<br>

    10. (Abbott 1.3.8) Compute the suprema and infima of the following sets if they exist.     What is the formal definition of Q?<br><ul><li>Q is the set of {{c4::al ordered pairs}} in {{c4::Z x Z /{0}}} representing an {{c3::equivalence class}} determined as&nbsp; {{c3::(p,q)}} {{c4::~}} {{c3::(m,n)}} if {{c5::{{c1::pn}}}} = {{c5::{{c2::qm}}}}</li></ul>

    1. (Abbott 1.4.2) Let \(A \subset \mathbb{R}\) be non-empty and bounded above, and let \(s \in \mathbb{R}\) have the property that for all \(n \in \mathbb{N}, s+\frac{1}{n}\) is an upper bound for \(A\) and \(s-\frac{1}{n}\) is not an upper bound for \(A\). Show \(s=\sup A\).<br><br>Solution. Suppose, for contradiction, that \(s\) is not an upper bound for \(A\). Then there is \(a \in A\) such that \(s&lt;a\). By the {{c1::Archimedean Property}}, there is an {{c2::\(n \in \mathbb{N}\)}} such that {{c2::\(\frac{1}{n}\)}} {{c5::{{c3::\(&lt;\) \(a-s\).}}}} This implies that \(s+\frac{1}{n}\) {{c5::{{c4::\(&lt;a\)}}}} contradicting the assumption that \(s+\frac{1}{n}\) is an upper bound for all \(n\). Thus, \(s\) is an upper bound.

    1. (Abbott 1.4.2) Let \(A \subset \mathbb{R}\) be non-empty and bounded above, and let \(s \in \mathbb{R}\) have the property that for all \(n \in \mathbb{N}, s+\frac{1}{n}\) is an upper bound for \(A\) and \(s-\frac{1}{n}\) is not an upper bound for \(A\). Show \(s=\sup A\).<br><br>(Now we will show that \(s\) is the l.u.b. by using Lemma 1.3.8.)<br><br>Let \(\epsilon&gt;0\) be given. By the Archimedean Property, there is an {{c1::\(n \in \mathbb{N}\)}} such that {{c1::\(\frac{1}{n}\)}} &lt; {{c2::\(\epsilon\)}}. By assumption {{c1::\(s-\frac{1}{n}\)}} is not an upper bound. So there is an {{c5::{{c3::\(a \in A\)}}}} such that \(s-\frac{1}{n}\) &lt; {{c5::{{c3::\(a\)}}}}. Then, {{c5::{{c4::\(s-\epsilon\)}}}} &lt; {{c3::\(a\)}}.<br><br>Therefore, by Lemma 1.3.8 \(s=\sup A\).

    2. (Abbott 1.4.3) Prove that \(\bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty}(0,1 / n)=\emptyset\).<br><br>Solution. Suppose, for contradiction, that \(\bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty}(0,1 / n) \neq \emptyset\). Let \(x \in\) {{c4::\(\bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty}(0,1 / n)\)}}. Then, \(x\) \(\in\) {{c3::\((0,1 / n)\)}} for {{c5::{{c2::all \(n \in \mathbb{N}\)}}}}. It follows that {{c5::{{c2::\(0&lt;x&lt;\frac{1}{n}\)}}}} for {{c2::all \(n \in \mathbb{N}\)}}. This contradicts the {{c5::{{c1::Archimedean Property}}}}.

    3. (Abbott 1.4.4) Let \(a&lt;b\) be real numbers and consider the set \(T=\mathbb{Q} \cap[a, b]\). Show that \(\sup T=b\).<br><br>By definition, if {{c1::\(t \in T\)}} then {{c2::\(t \leq b\)}}. So {{c5::{{c4::\(b\)}}}} is an {{c5::{{c3::upper bound for \(T\).}}}}

    3. (Abbott 1.4.4) Let \(a&lt;b\) be real numbers and consider the set \(T=\mathbb{Q} \cap[a, b]\). Show that \(\sup T=b\).<br><br>By definition, if \(t \in T\) then \(t \leq b\). So \(b\) is an upper bound for \(T\).<br><br>Let \(\epsilon&gt;0\) be given. Then, by the {{c1::density of \(\mathbb{Q}\) in \(\mathbb{R}\)}} there is a {{c1::rational \(r \in \mathbb{Q}\)}} such that<br><br><ul><li>{{c2::\(b-\epsilon\)}} {{c5::{{c3::&lt;}}}} {{c1::\(r\)}} {{c3::&lt;}} {{c2::\(b \text {. }\)}}</li></ul><br>Moreover, this implies that {{c5::{{c4::\(r \in T\)}}}}. Therefore, by Lemma 1.3.8 \(b=\sup T\).<br>
