    4. (Abbott 1.4.8)<br><br>(a) Give an example of two sets \(A, B \subset \mathbb{R}\) with \(A \cap B=\emptyset\), \(\sup A=\sup B\), \(\sup A \notin A\) and \(\sup B \notin B\).<br><br>(a) Let \(A=\left\{1-\frac{1}{2 j}: j \in \mathbb{N}\right\}\) and \(B=\left\{1-\frac{1}{2 k+1}: k \in \mathbb{N}\right\}\). By construction, \(1 \notin A\) and \(1 \notin B\).<br><br>First, we will show that \(A \cap B=\emptyset\). Suppose \(x \in A \cap B\). Then, \(x \in A\) and \(x \in B\). Thus, there are \(j, k \in \mathbb{N}\) such that \(x=\frac{1}{2 j}\) and \(x=\frac{1}{2 k+1}\). It follows that \(2 j=2 k+1 \Longrightarrow\) {{c1::\(j-k\)}}= {{c2::\(\frac{1}{2}\)}}, but this is {{c2::impossible since \(j-k\) is an integer}}. Thus, \(A \cap B=\emptyset\).<br><br>Next, we show that \(\sup A=1=\sup B\).<br><br>Let \(\epsilon&gt;0\) be given. Then, by {{c3::the Archimedean Property}} there are natural numbers \(j, k \in \mathbb{N}\) such that {{c4::\(\frac{1}{\epsilon}\)}} &lt; {{c5::\(2 j\)}} and {{c5::\(\frac{1}{\epsilon}\)}} &lt; {{c4::\(2 k+1\)}}. It follows that<br><br>{{c5::\[<br>1-\epsilon&lt;1-\frac{1}{2 j}&lt;1 \quad \text { and } \quad 1-\epsilon&lt;1-\frac{1}{2 k+1}&lt;1<br>\]}}<br><br>By Lemma 1.3.8, \(\sup A=1=\sup B\).

============================================================

  

    4. (Abbott 1.4.8)<br>(b) Prove or disprove the following: There exists a sequence of closed bounded (not necessarily nested) intervals \(I_{1}, I_{2}, I_{3}, \ldots\) with the property that \(\bigcap_{n=1}^{N} I_{n} \neq \emptyset\) for all \(N \in \mathbb{N}\), but \(\bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty} I_{n}=\emptyset\).<br><br><br>(b) Suppose there is a sequence of closed bounded intervals as in the problem, \(I_{n}=\left[a_{n}, b_{n}\right]\). Define the sequence of sets \(J_{N}\) = \(\bigcap_{n=1}^{N} I_{n}\) for \(N \in \mathbb{N}\). We will show that \(\left\{J_{N}\right\}\) is a nested sequence of intervals. By assumption \(J_{2}\) is non-empty, thus the intervals \(I_{1}\) and \(I_{2}\) must intersect one another. The only possibility is that \(J_{2}\) is a closed bounded interval of the following form, {{c1::\(I_{1}, I_{2},\left[a_{1}, b_{2}\right]\) or \(\left[a_{2}, b_{1}\right]\)::4 possible options}} and moreover, \(J_{2}\) = {{c2::\(I_{1} \cap I_{2}\)}} = {{c3::\(J_{1} \cap I_{2} \subset J_{1}\)}}, that is, the intervals are {{c3::nested}}. For induction, suppose \(J_{N}\) is a closed bounded set and nested such that \(J_{N+1} \subset J_{N}\). Then,<br><br><ul><li>\(J_{N+1}\) = \(\bigcap_{n=1}^{N+1} I_{n}\) = \(\left(\bigcap_{n=1}^{N} I_{n}\right) \cap I_{N+1}\) = {{c4::\(J_{N} \cap I_{N+1}\)}}</li></ul><br>Since \(J_{N}\) and \(I_{N+1}\) are {{c4::closed}} {{c4::bounded}} and \(J_{N+1}\) is {{c4::non-empty}}, it follows from a similar argument to above that \(J_{N+1}\) is a {{c4::closed bounded}} interval. Moreover, \(J_{N+1} \subset J_{N}\) are {{c3::nested}}. But, we also have that<br><br>{{c5::\[<br>\bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty} J_{n}=\bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty} I_{n}=\emptyset<br>\]}}<br><br>This contradicts the nested interval property which states that {{c5::\(\bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty} J_{n}\)}} should be {{c5::non-empty.}}<br>

============================================================

  

    (a) Prove if \(A_{1}, \ldots, A_{m}\) are countable sets then \(A_{1} \cup \cdots \cup A_{m}\) is countable.<br><br>(a) Let \(B, C\) be disjoint countable sets. We use the same trick as with the integers and list them as<br><br><ul><li>{{c2::\(B \cup C\)}} = {{c1::\(\left\{b_{1}, c_{1}, b_{2}, c_{2}, \ldots\right\}\)}}</li></ul><div>Meaning {{c2::\(B \cup C\)}} is countable, and {{c2::\(A_{1} \cup A_{2}\)}} is also countable since we can let \(B=A_{1}\) and \(C\) = {{c5::{{c3::\(A_{2} \backslash A_{1}\).}}}}<br><br>Now induction: suppose \(A_{1} \cup \cdots \cup A_{n}\) is countable, \(\left(A_{1} \cup \cdots \cup A_{n}\right) \cup A_{n+1}\) is {{c5::{{c4::the union of two countable sets}}}} which by above is {{c5::{{c4::countable}}}}.<br></div>

============================================================

  

    5. (Abbott 1.5.3(c)) Prove that if \(A_{n}\) is a countable set for each \(n \in \mathbb{N}\) then \(\bigcup_{n=1}^{\infty} A_{n}\) is countable. (See the book for a hint.)<br><br>Solution. From the hint in the book, we can visualize \(\bigcup_{n=1}^{\infty} A_{n}\) as a grid of numbers that is similar to the Cartesian product \(\mathbb{N} \times \mathbb{N}\). Moreover, in class we showed that \(\mathbb{N} \times \mathbb{N} \sim \mathbb{N}\) is countable.<br><br>Let \(f_{n}\): {{c1::\(A_{n}\)}} \(\rightarrow\) {{c1::\(\mathbb{N}\)}} be a bijection. Define a function \(f\): {{c2::\(\bigcup_{n=1}^{\infty} A_{n}\)}} \(\rightarrow\) {{c3::\(\mathbb{N} \times \mathbb{N}\)}} as follows. Since \(a\) could possibly appear in more than one of the sets \(A_{n}\), we must be careful so our function is well-defined. For each \(a \in \bigcup_{n=1}^{\infty} A_{n}\), let \(n_{a}\) be the {{c4::smallest number such that \(a \in A_{n_{a} }\) ( if \(n&lt;n_{a}\) then \(a \notin A_{n_{a} }\) )}}. Then,<br><br><ul><li>\(f(a)\) = {{c5::\(\left(n_{a}, f_{n_{a} }(a)\right)\)}}</li></ul><br>Every element of \(a \in \bigcup_{n=1}^{\infty} A_{n}\) has exactly one image in the range and thus, \(f\) is well-defined.<br><br>

============================================================

  

    5. (Abbott 1.5.3(c)) Prove that if \(A_{n}\) is a countable set for each \(n \in \mathbb{N}\) then \(\bigcup_{n=1}^{\infty} A_{n}\) is countable. (See the book for a hint.)<br><br>Solution. From the hint in the book, we can visualize \(\bigcup_{n=1}^{\infty} A_{n}\) as a grid of numbers that is similar to the Cartesian product \(\mathbb{N} \times \mathbb{N}\). Moreover, in class we showed that \(\mathbb{N} \times \mathbb{N} \sim \mathbb{N}\) is countable.<br><br>Let \(f_{n}: A_{n} \rightarrow \mathbb{N}\) be a bijection. Define a function \(f: \bigcup_{n=1}^{\infty} A_{n} \rightarrow \mathbb{N} \times \mathbb{N}\) as follows. Since \(a\) could possibly appear in more than one of the sets \(A_{n}\), we must be careful so our function is well-defined. For each \(a \in \bigcup_{n=1}^{\infty} A_{n}\), let \(n_{a}\) be the smallest number such that \(a \in A_{n_{a}}\) ( if \(n&lt;n_{a}\) then \(a \notin A_{n_{a}}\) ). Then,<br><br>\[<br>f(a)=\left(n_{a}, f_{n_{a}}(a)\right)<br>\]<br><br>Every element of \(a \in \bigcup_{n=1}^{\infty} A_{n}\) has exactly one image in the range and thus, \(f\) is well-defined.<br><br>We claim that \(f\) is an {{c1::injection}}. Suppose that {{c1::\(f(a)=f(b)\)}}. It follows that<br><br>{{c2::\[<br>\begin{gathered}<br>\left(n_{a}, f_{n_{a} }(a)\right)=\left(n_{b}, f_{n_{b} }(b)\right) \\<br>\Longleftrightarrow \quad n_{a}=n_{b} \quad \text { and } \quad f_{n_{a} }(a)=f_{n_{a} }(b) .<br>\end{gathered}<br>\]}}<br><br>Since \(f_{n_{a}}\) is {{c1::injective}}, it must follow that {{c3::\(a=b\)}}. We conclude that \(f\) is an {{c1::injective}} function.<br><br>The set \(\bigcup_{n=1}^{\infty} A_{n}\) is infinite, since \(A_{1}\) is countably infinite and \(A_{1} \subset \bigcup_{n=1}^{\infty} A_{n}\). Therefore, by a lemma* we proved in class and by using that \(\sim\) is an equivalence relation we have that<br><br><ul><li>\(\bigcup_{n=1}^{\infty} A_{n}\) \(\sim\) {{c4::\(f\left(\bigcup_{n=1}^{\infty} A_{n}\right)\)}} \( \sim\) {{c5::\(\mathbb{N} \times \mathbb{N} \sim \mathbb{N}\)}}</li></ul><br>

============================================================

  

    Lemma. Let \(A\) be an {{c3::infinite}} set and \(B\) be {{c2::countable}}. If there is a function \(f: A \rightarrow B\) that is {{c5::{{c1::injective}}}}, then {{c5::{{c4::\(A \sim B\)}}}} is {{c2::countable}}.

============================================================

  

    6. (Abbott 1.5.5) This problem shows that \(\sim\) is an equivalence relation.<br><br>(a) Show that \(A \sim A\) for every set \(A\).<br><br><br>(a) Let \(\iota: A \rightarrow A\) be the {{c1::identity}} function defined as {{c2::\(\iota(a)=a\)}}.<br><br>If \(\iota(a)=\iota(b)\) then \(a=b\). Thus, \(\iota\) is {{c5::{{c3::\(1-1\)}}}}. If \(a \in A\) then \(\iota(a)=a\). Thus, \(\iota\) is {{c5::{{c4::onto}}}}.<br><br>Therefore, \(\iota\) is bijective and \(A \sim A\).

============================================================

  

    (b) Show that if \(A \sim B\) show that \(B \sim A\).<br><br>(b) Let \(f: A \rightarrow B\) be bijective. Define the inverse function \(f^{-1}: B \rightarrow A\) as follows: Let \(b \in B\). Since \(f\) is {{c1::onto}}, there is an \(a \in A\) such that \(f(a)=b\). This leads to the definition {{c1::\(f^{-1}(f(a))\)}} = {{c2::\(a\).}}<br><br>Suppose \(f^{-1}(b)=f^{-1}\left(b^{\prime}\right)\). Let \(a, a^{\prime} \in A\) be such that \(f(a)=b\) and \(f\left(a^{\prime}\right)=b^{\prime}\). Then, the left hand side of the equation gives \(f^{-1}(b)=f^{-1}(f(a))=a\) and the right hand side gives \(f^{-1}\left(b^{\prime}\right)=f^{-1}\left(f\left(a^{\prime}\right)\right)=a^{\prime}\). Thus, \(a=a^{\prime}\) and \(b=f(a)=f\left(a^{\prime}\right)=b^{\prime}\). Therefore \(f^{-1}\) is {{c3::\(1-1\)}}.<br><br>Let \(a \in A\). Then, {{c5::\(f^{-1}(f(a))\)}} = {{c4::\(a\).}} Therefore, \(f^{-1}\) is {{c1::onto}}.<br><br>We conclude that \(f^{-1}\) is bijective and \(B \sim A\).

============================================================

  

    (c) Show that if \(A \sim B\) and \(B \sim C\) show that \(A \sim C\).<br><br>(c) Let \(f: A \rightarrow B\) and \(g: B \rightarrow C\) be bijective functions. Consider {{c1::the composition \(g \circ f: A \rightarrow C\)}} defined by {{c2::\(g \circ f(a)\)}}={{c3::\(g(f(a))\) }}.<br><br>Suppose \(g \circ f(a)=g \circ f\left(a^{\prime}\right)\). Since {{c4::\(g\) is injective}} we have that \(f(a)=f\left(a^{\prime}\right)\). Since {{c5::\(f\) is injective}} we have that \(a=a^{\prime}\). Thus, \(g \circ f\) is {{c4::\(1-1\)}}.<br><br>Let \(c \in C\). Since {{c4::\(g\) is onto}} there is a \(b \in B\) such that \(g(b)=c\). Since {{c5::\(f\) is onto}} there is a \(a \in A\) such that \(f(a)=b\). Thus, \(g \circ f(a)=g(f(a))=g(b)=c\). Therefore, \(g \circ f\) is {{c4::onto}}.<br><br>We have shown that \(A \sim C\).

============================================================

  

    7. (Abbott 1.5.8) Let \(B\) be a set of positive real numbers with the property that adding together any finite subset of elements from \(B\) always gives a sum of 2 or less. Show that \(B\) must be finite or countable.<br><br><div>Suppose \(B\) is an infinite set (we want to show that \(B\) is countable). If \(b \in B\) then we can think of \(b\) as adding one element of \(B\). It follows that \(b \leq 2\). Thus, 2 is an upper bound for \(B\). Hence, \(B\) is non-empty and bounded above. By the axiom of completeness, let \(b_{1}=\sup B\).<br><br>We claim that \(b_{1} \in B\). Suppose for contradiction that \(b_{1} \notin B\). Then, by Lemma 1.3.8, for all \(n \in N\) there are elements \(a_{n} \in B\) such that<br>{{c1::<br>\[<br>b_{1}-\frac{1}{n}&lt;a_{n}&lt;b_{1}<br>\]}}<br><br>Choose \(N \in \mathbb{N}\) such that<br><br><ul><li>{{c1::\(\frac{1}{N}\)}} &lt; {{c2::\(\frac{b_{1} }{3} \quad \text { (by Archimedean Property). }\)}}</li></ul><br>It follows that<br><br>{{c3::\[<br>\begin{aligned}<br>2 &amp; &lt;N b_{1}-1=N b_{1}-\sum_{n=1}^{N} \frac{1}{N} \\<br>&amp; \leq N b_{1}-\sum_{n=1}^{N} \frac{1}{n} \\<br>&amp; \leq \sum_{n=1}^{N}\left(b_{1}-\frac{1}{n}\right) \\<br>&amp; \leq \sum_{n=1}^{N} a_{n} .<br>\end{aligned}<br>\]}}<br><br>This contradicts the assumption that {{c4::summing finitely many elements is always less than or equal to 2}}. Therefore, \(b_{1}=\sup B=\max B \in B\).<br></div><div><br></div><div>Consider the set \(B_{2} \equiv B \backslash\left\{b_{1}\right\}\). Then, since \(B_{2}\) is obtained from \(B\) by removing one element and \(B\) is infinite it follows that \(B_{1}\) is infinite. Moreover, since \(B_{2} \subset B\) it follows that \(B_{2}\) is upper bounded by 2. By the axiom of completeness, let \(b_{2}=\sup B_{2}\). By a similar argument to above we can show that \(b_{2}=\max B_{2} \in B_{2}\).<br></div><div><br></div><div>Consider the set \(B_{n} \equiv\) {{c5::\(B \backslash\left\{b_{1}, b_{2}, \ldots, b_{n-1}\right\}\)}} and define inductively \(b_{n}\) = {{c5::\(\max B_{n}\)}}. We have constructed a subset<br><br>{{c5::\[<br>\left\{b_{1}, b_{2}, b_{3}, \ldots\right\} \subset B<br>\]}}<br><br>where \(b_{1}&gt;b_{2}&gt;b_{3}&gt;\ldots\) Finally, we claim that \(B\) = {{c5::\(\left\{b_{1}, b_{2}, b_{3}, \ldots\right\}\).}}</div>

============================================================

  

    7. (Abbott 1.5.8) Let \(B\) be a set of positive real numbers with the property that adding together any finite subset of elements from \(B\) always gives a sum of 2 or less. Show that \(B\) must be finite or countable.<br><br><br>Consider the set \(B_{n} \equiv B \backslash\left\{b_{1}, b_{2}, \ldots, b_{n-1}\right\}\) and define inductively \(b_{n}\) = {{c1::\(\max B_{n}\)}}. We have constructed a subset<br><br>\[<br>\left\{b_{1}, b_{2}, b_{3}, \ldots\right\} \subset B<br>\]<br><br>where {{c1::\(b_{1}&gt;b_{2}&gt;b_{3}&gt;\ldots\)}} Finally, we claim that \(B\) = {{c2::\(\left\{b_{1}, b_{2}, b_{3}, \ldots\right\}\).<br>}}<br>Suppose, for contradiction, that \(b \in B\) and \(b \neq b_{n}\) for all \(n \in \mathbb{N}\). By assumption on the set \(B\) we have that \(0&lt;b\). We claim that {{c3::\(b&lt;b_{n}\)}} for all \(n \in \mathbb{N}\). (Base case) since \(b_{1}=\max B\) and \(b \neq b_{1}\) it follows that \(b&lt;b_{1}\).<br><br>Suppose that \(b&lt;b_{n}\). Then, \(b_{n+1}\) = {{c1::\(\max B \backslash\left\{b_{1}, b_{2}, \ldots, b_{n}\right\}\)}}. By assumption, \(b \neq b_{n+1}\). Then, either \(b&gt;b_{n+1}\) or \(b&lt;b_{n+1}\). If \(b&gt;b_{n+1}\) then {{c4::\(b\) must be one of the previous maximums}}, but that cannot be true. Thus, {{c3::\(b&lt;b_{n}\)}}.<br><br>Choose \(N\) large enough so that<br><br>{{c5::\[<br>\frac{1}{N}&lt;\frac{b}{2} \quad \text { (by Archimedean Property). }<br>\]}}<br><br>It follows that<br><br>{{c5::\[<br>\begin{aligned}<br>2 &amp; &lt;N b=b+b+\cdots+b \quad(N \text { times }) \\<br>&amp; \leq b_{1}+b_{2}+\cdots+b_{N} .<br>\end{aligned}<br>\]}}<br><br>The right hand side is a finite sum of elements of \(B\) and we reach a contradiction.<br><br>Therefore, \(B=\left\{b_{1}, b_{2}, b_{3}, \ldots\right\}\) and \(B\) is a countable set.

============================================================

  

    \section*{8. (Abbott 1.6.10(a)(b))}<br><br>(a) Is the set of all functions from \(\{0,1\}\) to \(\mathbb{N}\) countable or uncountable?<br><br>(a) Let \(F(\{0,1\}, \mathbb{N}) \equiv\{f:\{0,1\} \rightarrow \mathbb{N}\}\) denote the set of all functions. Each function \(f \in F(\{0,1\}, \mathbb{N})\) is {{c1::uniquely determined}} by {{c2::the pair \((f(0), f(1))\)}}. Define a function, \(\phi: F(\{0,1\}, \mathbb{N}) \rightarrow \mathbb{N} \times \mathbb{N}\) by \(\phi(f)\) = {{c2::\((f(0), f(1))\)}}. We claim that \(\phi\) is bijective.<br><br>Suppose \(\phi(f)=\phi(g)\). Then, {{c3::\((f(0), f(1))\)}} = {{c4::\((g(0), g(1))\)}} \( \Longleftrightarrow\)&nbsp; {{c4::\(f(0)\)}} = {{c3::\(g(0)\)}} and {{c3::\(f(1)\)}} = {{c4::\(g(1)\)}} \(\Longleftrightarrow\) \(f=g\). Thus, \(\phi\) is \(1-1\).<br><br>Let \((m, n) \in \mathbb{N} \times \mathbb{N}\). Define the function \(f_{m, n} \in F(\{0,1\}, \mathbb{N})\) by \(f_{m, n}\) {{c4::\((0)\)}} = {{c5::\(m\)}} and \(f_{m, n}\) {{c5::\((1)\)}} = {{c4::\(n\)}}. It follows that<br><br>{{c5::\[<br>\phi\left(f_{m, n}=\left(f_{m, n}(0), f_{m, n}(1)\right)=(m, n)\right.<br>\]<br>}}<br>Therefore, \(\phi\) is onto.<br><br>We conclude that \(\phi\) is a bijection and that \(F(\{0,1\}, \mathbb{N}) \sim \mathbb{N} \times \mathbb{N} \sim \mathbb{N}\) is countable.

============================================================

  

    (b) Is the set of all functions from \(\mathbb{N}\) to \(\{0,1\}\) countable or uncountable?<br><br>High-level argument:<br><ul><li>A function to binary values can be expressed as {{c2::a mask}}</li><li>If you claim you have a list of all such {{c2::masks}} I can create a new {{c2::mask}} which {{c3::differs from every mask in the list}} {{c5::{{c4::at 1 position}}}} via {{c5::{{c1::diagonalization}}}}</li></ul>

    A typical task in analysis is to decipher whether a property possessed by every term in a convergent sequence is necessarily inherited by the limit. Assume \(\left(a_{n}\right) \rightarrow a\), and determine the validity of each claim.     A typical task in analysis is to decipher whether a property possessed by every term in a convergent sequence is necessarily inherited by the limit. Assume \(\left(a_{n}\right) \rightarrow a\), and determine the validity of each claim. Try to produce a counterexample for any that are false.<br><br>(b) If every \(a_{n}\) is in the complement of the interval \((0,1)\), then \(a\) is also in the complement of \((0,1)\).<br><br><ul><li>(b) {{c5::{{c3::True}}}}, since if \(a \in(0,1)\) then there would exist an {{c4::{{c1::\(\epsilon\)-neighborhood}}}} inside \((0,1)\) that {{c4::{{c2::\(a_{n}\)}}}} would {{c4::{{c1::have to fall in}}}}, contradicting the fact that {{c2::\(a_{n}\)}} {{c5::{{c3::\(\notin(0,1)\).}}}}</li></ul>

    (Abbott 2.5.2) Decide whether the following propositions are true or false, providing a short justification for each conclusion (full proof is not needed but please provide a convincing argument).<br>(a) If every proper subsequence of \(\left(x_n\right)\) converges, then \(\left(x_n\right)\) converges as well.<br><br><ul><li>{{c1::True}}.&nbsp;</li><li>Consider the proper subsequence obtained by removing the {{c1::first term \(\left(x_{k+1}\right)\)}}.&nbsp;</li><li>Here we give a more detailed proof. Let \(\epsilon&gt;0\) be given. There exists \(N \in \mathbb{N}\) such that if \(k&gt;N\) then {{c2::\(\left|x_{k+1}-x\right|\)}} &lt; \(\epsilon\)<br></li><li>If \(n&gt;N+1\) then \(n-1&gt;N\).     (c) If \(\left(x_n\right)\) is bounded and diverges, then there exist two subsequences of \(\left(x_n\right)\) that converge to different limits.<br><br>True or false?<br><ul><li>Since \(\left(x_n\right)\) is bounded, we can apply the Bolzano-Weierstrass theorem to conclude there exists a {{c1::convergent subsequence.&nbsp;}}</li><li>Let \(\left(x_{n_k}\right)\) converge to \(x\).&nbsp;</li><li>However, since \(\left(x_n\right)\) does not converge to \(x\), there is an \{{c3::(\epsilon-\) neighborhood \(V_\epsilon(x)\)}} such that infinitely many terms of \(\left(a_n\right)\) are {{c2::outside of \(V-\epsilon(x)\).}}&nbsp;</li><li>Applying Bolzano-Weierstrass to terms of the sequence outside of \(V_\epsilon(x)\) gives a {{c1::subsequence that converges}}. But this new {{c1::subsequence}} cannot {{c5::converge to \(x\)}} by the {{c4::order limit}} laws. A more detailed proof is found in problem 2.<br></li></ul>

============================================================

  

    (d) If \(\left(x_n\right)\) is monotone and contains a convergent subsequence, then \(\left(x_n\right)\) converges.<br><br>True/false?<br><br><ul><li>(d)&nbsp; Suppose, without loss of generality that \(\left(x_n\right)\) is increasing.</li><li>&nbsp;Let \(\left(x_{n_k}\right)\) be a convergent subsequence. It follows that \(\left(x_{n_k}\right)\) is bounded.</li><li>&nbsp;Let \(M&gt;0\) be such that \(\left|x_{n_k}\right|&lt;M\) for all \(k \in \mathbb{N}\).&nbsp;</li><li>Since \(k \leq n_k\) and \(\left(x_n\right)\) is increasing, we have that {{c1::\(x_1\)}} \(\leq\) x_k \(\leq\) {{c2::\(x_{n_k}\)}} \(&lt;\) {{c3::\(M\)}} for all \(k \in \mathbb{N}\).&nbsp;</li><li>Thus, \(\left|x_n\right|&lt;\) {{c4::\(\max \left(M,\left|x_1\right|+1\right)\)}}.&nbsp;</li><li>By the {{c5::Monotone Convergence}} Theorem, \(\left(x_n\right)\) is {{c5::bounded}} and {{c5::increasing}} so therefore \(\left(x_n\right)\) converges.</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    (Abbott 2.5.5) Assume that \(\left(a_n\right)\) is a bounded sequence with the property that every convergent subsequence of \(\left(a_n\right)\) converges to the same limit \(a \in \mathbb{R}\). Show that \(\left(a_n\right)\) must converge to \(a\).<br><br>Solution:<br><ul><li>If it converges, it cannot converge to a different limit since the subsequence must {{c5::{{c1::eventually}}}} {{c4::{{c2::intersect the main sequence}}}} after {{c4::{{c3::a constant Nk&nbsp;}}}}</li><li><br></li></ul>

============================================================

  

    (Abbott 2.5.5) Assume that \(\left(a_n\right)\) is a bounded sequence with the property that every convergent subsequence of \(\left(a_n\right)\) converges to the same limit \(a \in \mathbb{R}\). Show that \(\left(a_n\right)\) must converge to \(a\).<br><br>Solution if it diverges<ul><li>A convergent subsqeuence must exist by Bolzano<br></li><li>\(\left(a_n\right)\) does not converge to \(a\) if there exists \(\epsilon&gt;0\) such that for every \(k \in \mathbb{N}\) there exists a number \(m\) such that \(m \geq k\) with {{c5::\(\left|a-a_m\right| \geq \epsilon\).}}<br></li><li>Construct a new subsequence \(\left(a_{m_k}\right)\) as follows. Let \(m_1 \geq 1\) be such that {{c1::\(\left|a-a_{m_1}\right|\)&nbsp;}} \(\geq \epsilon\).&nbsp;</li><li>And define \(m_k\) inductively by, \(m_k\) {{c2::\(\geq m_{k-1}+1\)}} be such that {{c3::\(\left|a-a_{m_k}\right|\)}} \(\geq \epsilon\).&nbsp;</li><li>We apply Bolzano-Weierstrass to the subsequence \(\left(a_{m_k}\right)\) to get that there exists a subsequence of \(\left(a_{m_k}\right)\) that converges.&nbsp;</li><li>Every subsequence of \(\left(a_{m_k}\right)\) is also {{c4::a subsequence of \(\left(a_n\right)\)}}.&nbsp;</li><li>Abusing notation, we call this convergent subsequence \(\left(a_{m_k}\right)\) and let \(a^{\prime}\) be the limit.<br></li><li>Now we show that \(a \neq a^{\prime}\). Since {{c3::\(\left|a-a_{m_k}\right|\)}} \(\geq \epsilon\) for all \(k \in \mathbb{N}\) we have that either {{c5::\(a-a_{m_k}\) \(&gt;\epsilon\)}} or {{c4::\(a_{m_k}-a\)}} \(&gt;\epsilon\) for all \(k \in \mathbb{N}\). By monotonicity of order, we have that {{c4::\(a-a^{\prime}\)}} \(\geq \epsilon\) or {{c5::\(a^{\prime}-a\)}} \(\geq \epsilon\). In either case, {{c4::\(a \neq a^{\prime}\)}}. Therefore we have constructed two convergent subsequences \(a_{n_k}\) and \(a_{m_k}\) that converge to distinct limits.<br></li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Let \(\left(a_n\right)\) be a bounded sequence. Recall from HW4, we defined a sequence \(y_n=\sup \left\{a_k: k \geq n\right\}\) and proved that \(\left(y_n\right)\) is bounded and monotone. Thus, by the Monotone Convergence Theorem \(\left(y_n\right)\) converges. Let \(y=\lim _{n \rightarrow \infty} y_n\).<br>Prove that there exists a subsequence \(\left(a_{n_k}\right)\) of \(\left(a_n\right)\) that converges to \(y\).<br><br>Proof:<br><ul><li>Since&nbsp;\(y_{n_i}\) is always a supremum of a set</li><li>There always exists</li><li>{{c1::\(y_{n_{k-1}+1}-\frac{1}{k}\)&nbsp;}}\(\leq\)&nbsp;\(a_{n_{k}}\)&nbsp;\(\leq\)&nbsp;{{c2::\(y_{n_{k-1}+1}\)}}<br></li><li>Since {{c2::\(y_{n_{k-1}+1}\)}}={{c5::\(\sup A_{n_{k-1}+1}\) \(a_{n_k}\)}} there exists such an&nbsp;\(a_{n_k}\) from&nbsp; \(A_n=\left\{a_k: k \geq n\right\}\),&nbsp;</li><li>Since:</li><ul><li>{{c2::\(\left(y_{n_{k-1}+1}\right)\)}} must {{c3::converge to y}}&nbsp;</li><li>And {{c3::&nbsp;\(\lim \frac{1}{k}\)}} = {{c4::\(0\)&nbsp;}}</li></ul><li>By the {{c5::sequeze}} theorem&nbsp;\(a_{n_k}\) must also converge to y</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    If \(\left(x_n\right)\) and \(\left(y_n\right)\) are Cauchy sequences prove directly that \(\left(x_n y_n\right)\) is a Cauchy sequence.<br><br>Solution:<br><ul><li>Both are bounded by {{c5::some bound M}} = {{c5::\(\max(M_x,M_y)\)}}</li><li>Let:</li><ul><li>{{c2::\(\left|x_n-x_m\right|\)}}&nbsp;\(\leq\)&nbsp;{{c1::\(\frac{\epsilon}{2 M}\)}}<br></li><li>{{c2::\(\left|y_n-y_m\right|\)}}&nbsp;\(\leq\)&nbsp;{{c1::\(\frac{\epsilon}{2 M}\)}}</li></ul><li>\(\left|x_n y_n-x_m y_m\right| \) \(\leq\) {{c3::\(\left|x_n y_n-x_m y_n\right|+\left|x_m y_n-x_m y_m\right|\)}}<br>(by {{c2::triangle inequality}})<br></li><li>=&nbsp;{{c4::\(\left|x_n-x_m\right|\left|y_n\right|+\left|x_m\right|\left|y_n-y_m\right|\)}}</li><li>=&nbsp;{{c1::\(\frac{\epsilon}{2 M} M+M \frac{\epsilon}{2 M}\)&nbsp;}}</li><li>=&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    6. Suppose that \(\left(x_n\right)\) is a sequence such that \(\left|x_{n+1}-x_n\right|&lt;\frac{1}{n^2}\) for all \(n \in \mathbb{N}\). Prove that \(\left(x_n\right)\) is a Cauchy sequence.<br><br>Solution:<br><ul><li>We known&nbsp;{{c4::\(\sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \)}} {{c1::\(\frac{1}{k^2}\)}} converges</li><li>Thus by the Cauchy criterion for series&nbsp;</li><li>{{c5::{{c2::\(\left|\frac{1}{m^2}+\frac{1}{(m+1)^2}+\cdots+\frac{1}{(n-1)^2}\right|\)}}}}&nbsp;\(&lt;\)&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)<br></li><li>Therefore:</li><li>\(\left|x_m-x_n\right|\)<br></li><li>\(\leq\)&nbsp;{{c5::{{c3::\(\sum |x_m - x_{m+1}&nbsp; |\)}}}}<br></li><li>\(&lt;\)&nbsp;&nbsp;{{c5::{{c2::\(\frac{1}{m^2}+\frac{1}{(m+1)^2}+\cdots+\frac{1}{(n-1)^2}\)}}}}</li><li>\(&lt;\)&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)<br></li></ul>

    (a) Show directly from the definition that \(\left(\frac{n+1}{n}\right)\) is a Cauchy sequence.<br><br>Solution:<br><ul><li>{{c4::\(\frac{m+1}{m}-\frac{n+1}{n}\)&nbsp;}}</li><li>=&nbsp;{{c2::\(\frac{1}{m}-\frac{1}{n}\).}}<br></li><li>\(\leq\)&nbsp; {{c5::{{c1::\(\frac{1}{m}+\frac{1}{n}\)}}}}<br></li><li>\(&lt;\) {{c5::{{c3::\(\epsilon/2\) <span style="font-size: 16.6667px;">+&nbsp;</span>\(\epsilon /2\)}}}}<br></li></ul>

    (a) Show directly from the definition that \(\left(\frac{n+1}{n}\right)\) is a Cauchy sequence.<br><br>Solution:<br><ul><li>\(\frac{m+1}{m}-\frac{n+1}{n}\)&nbsp;</li><li>=&nbsp;\(\frac{1}{m}-\frac{1}{n}\).<br></li><li>\(\leq\)&nbsp;{{c5::{{c1::\(1 - \frac{1}{n}\)}}}} Since&nbsp;\(\frac{1}{m}\) is {{c4::{{c2::bounded by 1}}}}</li><li>For a given \(\epsilon\), given that \(\frac{1}{n} \to 0\), there exists&nbsp;\(N\) such that&nbsp;\(\frac{1}{n}\)&nbsp;\(\leq\){{c4::{{c3::&nbsp;\(1 - \epsilon\)}}}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Show directly from the definition that&nbsp;\(( n+\frac{(-1)^n}{n})\) is not a cauchy sequence:<br><br>Solution:<br><ul><li>\(|\) {{c1::\( n+\frac{(-1)^n}{n}\) -&nbsp;\(m+\frac{(-1)^m}{m}\)&nbsp;}}\(|\)<br></li><li>=&nbsp;\(|\)&nbsp;{{c2::\((n-m)\)}} +&nbsp;{{c1::\( (\frac{(-1)^n}{n} - \frac{(-1)^m}{m}) \)}} \(|\)&nbsp;</li><li>\(\geq\)&nbsp;{{c3::\((n-m)-\frac{1}{n}-\frac{1}{m}\)}} with the caveat that&nbsp; the previous {{c4::inequality}} could have been resolved in {{c4::four}} ways<br></li><li>Since the fractions go to 0, the gap between them is {{c5::at-least 1 minus a diminishing term}} and thus they are not cauchy sequences</li></ul>

If true, give a brief explanation (a complete proof is not required), and if false, give a counterexample.<br><br>c. \((T / F)\) If the sequence \(\left(a_n\right)\) converges and the sequence \(\left(a_n+b_n\right)\) converges then the sequence \(\left(b_n\right)\) converges.<br><br>Solution:<br><ul><li>{{c4::\(|a_n + b_n - c|\)&nbsp;}}\(&lt;\)&nbsp;{{c5::\(\epsilon/2\)}}</li><li>{{c5::\(|a_n - a|\)}}&nbsp;\(&lt;\)&nbsp;{{c5::\(\epsilon/2\)}}</li><li>\(b_n\) =&nbsp;{{c1::\(a_n + b_n - a_n\)}}</li><li>= {{c2::\(|a_n + b_n - a_n - c + a|\)}} by taking b={{c2::c-a}}</li><li>= {{c3::\(|a_n + b_n - c + - (a_n - a)|\)&nbsp;}}&nbsp;</li><li>\(\leq\) {{c4::\(|a_n + b_n - c|\)&nbsp;}} +&nbsp; {{c5::\(|a_n - a|\)&nbsp;}} by {{c4::triangle inequality}}<br></li><li>\(\leq\)&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)<br></li></ul>

    <div>Problem 3. Recall that \(n!=1 \cdot 2 \cdots n\) is the product of the first \(n\) natural numbers.<br>a. (15 pts) Use a., or any other method, to show that \(\lim _{n \rightarrow \infty} \frac{2^n}{n!}=0\).<br></div><div><br></div><div>Solution:</div><div><ul><li>\(2^n / n!\)&nbsp;\( \leq\)&nbsp;{{c1::\(3 (n-1)! / n!\)&nbsp;}}\(\leq\)&nbsp;{{c2::\(3 / n\)}}<br></li><li>{{c2::\(3/n - 0\)&nbsp;}}\(&lt;\)&nbsp;\(\epsilon\) implies&nbsp;\(n\) &gt;{{c5::{{c3::&nbsp;\(3/\epsilon\)}}}}<br></li><li>Since the numbers are positive, or by squeeze theorem using{{c5::{{c4::&nbsp;\(2^{n-1}/n!\)}}}}, it must converge to 0&nbsp;</li></ul></div>

    Problem 4. Define a sequence recursively by \(x_1=1\) and<br>\[<br>x_{n+1}=\sqrt{6+x_n} \quad \text { for } \quad n \in \mathbb{N} \text {. }<br>\]<br><br>c. (5 pts) Compute the limit: \(x=\lim _{n \rightarrow \infty} x_n\).<br><br>Solution:<br><ul><li>The sequence&nbsp;\(x_n\) and subsequence&nbsp;\(x_{n+1}\) must converge to {{c1::the same limit since}} the sequence is {{c1::bounded}} and {{c1::monotonic}}</li><li>Thus:</li><li>{{c2::\(L\)}} =&nbsp;{{c3::\(\sqrt{6+L}\)}}<br></li><li>{{c4::\(L^2 - L - 6\)}} = \(0\)<br></li><li>{{c5::\(L(L-1)\)}} = \(6\)<br></li><li>Thus L = 2 or 3, it cannot be 2 since&nbsp;\(x_2 = \sqrt{7}\) &gt; \(2\) and the sequence is increasing</li></ul>

    b. \((T / F)\) If \(\sum a_n\) converges absolutely and \(\left(b_n\right)\) converges then \(\sum a_n b_n\) converges absolutely.<br><br>Solution:<br><ul><li>Since&nbsp;\(b_n\) converges then the sequence is {{c1::bounded}}, it follows that&nbsp;</li><li>{{c4::\(0 \)&nbsp;}}</li><li>\(\leq \) {{c5::\(\left|a_n b_n\right|\)&nbsp;}}</li><li>= {{c3::\(|a_n||b_n|\)&nbsp;}}</li><li>&lt; {{c1::\(M|a_n|\).}}<br></li><li>Thus, if we choose {{c2::\(\epsilon / M\)}} as the&nbsp;\(\epsilon\) of&nbsp; \(\sum | a_n|\) we get convergence&nbsp;</li></ul>

    Problem 4. Define a sequence recursively by \(x_1=2\) and<br>\[<br>x_{n+1}=\frac{1}{2}\left(x_n+\frac{3}{x_n}\right) \quad \text { for } \quad n \in \mathbb{N} \text {. }<br>\]<br><br>c.     What are the properties of a dedekind cut?<br><ul><li>A partition of Q into two sets A and B</li><li>Such that {{c5::{{c1::a &lt; b}}}} for {{c4::{{c3::all a in A and b in B}}}}</li><li>And A contains {{c4::{{c2::no greatest element}}}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Dedekind defines {{c5::{{c3::R}}}} as the set of all {{c4::{{c2::dedekind cuts}}}} of {{c4::{{c1::the rationals}}}}

============================================================

  

    Which {{c3::ordered}} fileds have the {{c4::least upper bound}} property?<br><ul><li>{{c5::{{c1::R}}}} and fields {{c5::{{c2::isomporphic to}}}} {{c1::R}}</li></ul>

    (A) \(\forall a \in A\)&nbsp; \(\alpha\)&nbsp; {{c5::{{c1::&lt;}}}} \(\gamma\) \(\rightarrow\)&nbsp; {{c4::{{c3::\(\sup A\)}}}} {{c4::{{c2::\(\leqslant\)}}}} \(\gamma\)

    Principle of Induction (PoI)<br>Let \(S\) be a {{c5::subset of \(N\)}} such that<br><ul><li>{{c4::\(1\)}} \(\in S\),</li><li>if {{c1::\(k\)}} \(\in\) \(S\), then {{c2::\(k+1\)}} \(\in S\) implies that \(S\) = {{c3::\(N\).}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Proof principle of induction must follow if you assume the well-ordering princple:<br><ul><li>Proof&nbsp; by contradiction: Assume that&nbsp;\(1 \in S\) and&nbsp;\(k \in S \implies (k+1)\in S\), however&nbsp;&nbsp;\(S\)&nbsp;\(\neq\)&nbsp;\(N\)</li><li>Make A =&nbsp;\(N\)&nbsp;\(/\)&nbsp;\(S\) and assume it is {{c5::non-empty&nbsp;}}</li><li>By the well-ordering principle A has a {{c1::smallest element&nbsp;\(i\)}}</li><li>However by the {{c2::recursive}} rule&nbsp;{{c1::\(i-1\)}}&nbsp; must {{c3::be in S}} which would then imply {{c4::\(i\) is in A}}</li><li>Thus A has to be {{c5::empty}} which is a contradiction</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    How does induction relate to the natural numbers?<br><ul><li>We look at the set of {{c5::{{c1::all natural numbers}}}} for which {{c4::{{c2::a statement is true}}}} and we want to prove it equals {{c4::{{c3::N}}}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    What is the principle of {{c5::strong}} induction?<br><ul><li>If&nbsp;{{c4::\(1\)&nbsp;}}\(\in\) S</li><li>And&nbsp;{{c1::\(\{1,...,k\} \) \(\in S\)}} implies {{c2::\(k+1\)}}&nbsp;\(\in S\) then&nbsp;\(S\) =&nbsp;{{c3::\(N\)}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    A point in the {{c4::codomain}} of a function may have {{c2::multiple}} {{c5::{{c1::preimages}}}} meaning that the {{c5::{{c3::inverse}}}} is not necessarily a function

    \(A\)&nbsp;{{c1::\(\sim\)}}&nbsp;\(B\)&nbsp;\(\implies\)&nbsp;{{c2::\(\mathrm{card} A\)}}{{c5::{{c4::&nbsp;\(=\)}}}}&nbsp;{{c5::{{c3::\(\mathrm{card} B\)}}}}

============================================================

  

    {{c1::Counting}} a set A implies defining a {{c3::function}} from {{c5::{{c4::A}}}} to {{c5::{{c2::N}}}}

============================================================

  

    Any {{c4::sequence}} {{c2::indexed}} by {{c5::{{c3::N}}}} is {{c5::{{c1::countable}}}}

============================================================

  

    Any set of objects that can be {{c5::{{c2::listed}}}} in {{c4::{{c3::sequence}}}} must be {{c4::{{c1::countable}}}}

============================================================

  

    Any set that can be {{c5::{{c3::put in bijection with N}}}} is {{c4::{{c2::countable}}}} and {{c4::{{c1::infinite}}}}

============================================================

  

    Proof that&nbsp;\(N\) is infinite:<br><ul><li>I.H: If a finite set&nbsp;\(J_{n+1}\) can be put into bijection with&nbsp;\(N\) then so can&nbsp;\(J_{n}\)</li><li>Base case:&nbsp;\(J_1\) cannot be put into bijection with N since no function from {{c5::a single element}} to N can be {{c5::onto}}</li><li>Inductive case:</li><ul><li>Assume&nbsp;\(\exists f\) s.t \(J_{n+1}\)&nbsp;\(\sim\)&nbsp;\(N\)</li><li>This implies that&nbsp;\(\exists\) \( f^\prime\) : {{c1::\(J_n\)}} \(\to\) {{c2::\(N \setminus \{f(n+1)\}\)}}</li><li>However since \(\exists g\) sucht that {{c2::\(N \setminus \{x\} \)}}\(\sim\)&nbsp;{{c4::\(N\)}}</li><li>Then&nbsp;{{c3::\(g \circ f^\prime\)}} is a bijection between&nbsp;\(N\) and&nbsp;\(J_n\)</li></ul><li>Taking this to the base case leads to a contradiction</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    N is an {{c4::infinite}} set because it can be put into {{c3::one-to-one correspondance}} with a {{c5::{{c1::proper subset}}}} of {{c5::{{c2::itself}}}}

============================================================

  

    Every {{c5::{{c1::infinite}}}} subset of a {{c4::{{c2::countable}}}} set is {{c4::{{c3::countable}}}}

============================================================

  

    Proof that every infinite subset E of a countable set&nbsp; A is countable:<br><ul><li>A can be written as a sequence{{c4::&nbsp;\(\{x_1,x_2, \cdots,&nbsp; \}\)}}</li><li>Let&nbsp;\(n_1\) =&nbsp;{{c1::\(\inf\)}} {{c5::{{c2::\(\{ i: x_i \in E\}\)}}}}, which exists due to the {{c5::{{c3::well-ordering principle}}}} applied over the {{c4::indicies}}</li><li>Let&nbsp;\(n_2\) = {{c1::\(\inf\)}} {{c5::{{c2::\(\{ i: x_i \in E, i&gt;n_1\} \)}}}}</li><li>Let&nbsp;&nbsp;\(n_k\) = {{c1::\(\inf\)}} {{c5::{{c2::\(\{ i: x_i \in E, i&gt;n_{k-1}\} \)}}}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    If A is {{c5::{{c2::countable}}}} then&nbsp;\(A\)&nbsp;{{c4::{{c1::\(\times\)}}}}&nbsp;\(A\) is {{c4::{{c3::countable}}}}

============================================================

  

    A natural question to ask is whether the theorems we have proved about sequences, series, and functions in \(\mathbf{R}\) have analogues in the plane \(\mathbf{R}^{2}\) or in even higher dimensions. Looking back over the proofs, one crucial observation is that most of the arguments depend on just a few basic properties of the {{c3::absolute value}} function. Interpreting the statement " {{c4::\(|x-y|\)}} " to mean the "{{c5::{{c1::distance}}}} from \(x\) to \(y\) in {{c5::{{c2::\(\mathbf{R}\)}}}}," our aim is to experiment with other ways of measuring {{c1::distance}} on other sets such as \(\mathbf{R}^{2}\) and \(C[0,1]\), the space of continuous functions on \([0,1]\).

============================================================

  

    Definition 8.2.1. Given a set \(X\), a function \(d: X \times X \rightarrow \mathbf{R}\) is a metric on \(X\) if for all \(x, y \in X\) :<br><br><ul><li>(i) \(d(x, y)\)&nbsp; \(\geq\) \(0\) with \(d(x, y)\) = \(0\) if and only if \(x\) = \(y\),</li><li>(ii) {{c1::\(d(x, y)\)}} = {{c2::\(d(y, x)\)}}, and</li><li>(iii) for all \(z \in X\), {{c1::\(d(x, y)\)}} {{c5::\(\leq\)}} {{c3::\(d(x, z)\)}}{{c2::+}}{{c4::\(d(z, y)\).}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    A {{c5::{{c2::metric space}}}} is a {{c4::{{c3::set \(X\)}}}} together with a {{c4::{{c1::metric \(d\).}}}}

============================================================

  

    &nbsp;The next two exercises illustrate the point that the same set \(X\) can be home to {{c1::several different metrics}}. When referring to a {{c3::metric space}}, we must specify the {{c5::{{c4::set}}}} and the {{c5::{{c2::particular distance function \(d\).}}}}

============================================================

  

    (a) \(d(x, y)\) ={{c1::\(\sqrt{\left(x_{1}-y_{1}\right)^{2}+\left(x_{2}-y_{2}\right)^{2} }\).}}<br><br>The metric in part (a) of the previous exercise is the familiar {{c3::Euclidean}} distance between two points in the plane. This is often referred to as the "{{c4::usual}}" or "{{c4::standard}}" metric on {{c5::\(\mathbf{R}^{2}\)}}. The usual metric on {{c4::\(\mathbf{R}\)}} is our old friend \(d(x, y)=\) {{c2::\(|x-y|\).}}

============================================================

  

    Definition 8.2.2. Let \((X, d)\) be a metric space. A sequence \(\left(x_{n}\right) \subseteq X\) {{c5::converges}} to an element \(x \in X\) if for all {{c2::\(\epsilon\)}} {{c4::\(&gt;0\)}} there exists an {{c3::\(N \in \mathbf{N}\)}} such that {{c1::\(d\left(x_{n}, x\right)\)}} {{c5::\(&lt;\)}} {{c2::\(\epsilon\)}} whenever {{c3::\(n\)}} {{c4::\(\geq N\).}}

============================================================

  

    Definition 8.2.3. A sequence \(\left(x_{n}\right)\) in a metric space \((X, d)\) is a {{c2::Cauchy}} sequence if for all {{c3::\(\epsilon\)}} {{c5::\(&gt;0\)}} there exists an {{c4::\(N \in \mathbf{N}\)}} such that \(d\) {{c1::\(\left(x_{m}, x_{n}\right)\)}} \(&lt;\) {{c3::\(\epsilon\)}} whenever {{c4::\(m, n\)}} {{c5::\(\geq N\).}}

============================================================

  

    The Cauchy Criterion, as it is called in \(\mathbf{R}\), was an "if and only if" statement. In the general {{c5::metric space}} setting, however, the converse statement does not always hold. Recall that, in \(\mathbf{R}\), the assertion that "Cauchy sequences converge" was shown to be equivalent to the {{c1::Axiom of Completeness}}. In order to transport the {{c1::Axiom of Completeness}} into a {{c5::metric space}}, we would need to have an {{c2::ordering}} on our space so that we could discuss such things as {{c2::upper bounds}}. It is an interesting observation that not every set can be {{c2::ordered}} in a satisfying way (the points in \(\mathbf{R}^{2}\) for example). Even without an {{c2::ordering}}, we are still going to want {{c3::completeness}}. For {{c5::metric spaces}}, the {{c4::convergence}} of {{c4::Cauchy}} sequences is taken to be the definition of {{c3::completeness}}.

============================================================

  

    Definition 8.2.4. A {{c5::metric}} space \((X, d)\) is {{c1::complete}} if every {{c3::Cauchy sequence}} in \(X\) {{c4::converges to}} {{c2::an element of \(X\).}}

============================================================

  

    For the metric space {{c4::C[0,1]}} of {{c5::all functions on [0,1]}} the most common metric is:<br><ul><li>{{c1::\(\|\)}} f-g {{c1::\(\|_{\infty}\)}} =&nbsp;\(d(f, g)\) =&nbsp;{{c3::\(\sup\)&nbsp;}} {{c2::\(\{|f(x)-g(x)|: x \in[0,1]\}\)}}<br></li></ul>

============================================================

  

    \[<br>\|f-g\|_{\infty}=d(f, g)=\sup \{|f(x)-g(x)|: x \in[0,1]\}<br>\]<br><br>Setting \(g\) = {{c1::\(0\)}} gives the so-called "{{c4::sup}} norm"<br><br><ul><li>{{c5::\(\|\)}} f {{c5::\(\|_{\infty}\)}} =&nbsp;{{c2::\(d(f, 0)\)&nbsp;}} = {{c3::\(\sup \{|f(x)|: x \in[0,1]\}\)}}<br></li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Definition 8.2.5. Let \(\left(X, d_{1}\right)\) and \(\left(Y, d_{2}\right)\) be metric spaces. A function \(f\) : \(X \rightarrow Y\) is {{c1::continuous}} at \(x \in X\) if for all {{c3::\(\epsilon\)}} \(&gt;0\) there exists a {{c4::\(\delta\)}} {{c5::\(&gt;0\)}} such that {{c2::\(d_{2}(f(x), f(y))\)}} {{c4::\(&lt;\)}} {{c3::\(\epsilon\)}} whenever {{c1::\(d_{1}(x, y)\)}} {{c3::\(&lt;\)}} {{c4::\(\delta\).}}

============================================================

  

    Definition 8.2.6. Given {{c3::\(\epsilon\)}} {{c4::\(&gt;0\)}} and an element \(x\) in the metric space \((X, d)\), the {{c3::\(\epsilon\)}}-{{c4::neighborhood}} of \(x\) is the set {{c5::{{c2::\(V_{\epsilon}(x)\)}}}} = {{c5::{{c1::\(\{y \in X: d(x, y)&lt;\epsilon\}\).}}}}

============================================================

  

    With the definition of an \(\epsilon\)-neighborhood, we can now define open sets, limit points, and closed sets exactly as we did before. A set \(O \subseteq X\) is {{c3::open}} if for every \(x \in O\) we can find a {{c1::neighborhood \(V_{\epsilon}(x)\)}} {{c2::\(\subseteq O\)}}. A point \(x\) is a {{c4::limit}} point of a set \(A\) if every {{c1::\(V_{\epsilon}(x)\)}} intersects \(A\) in some {{c5::point other than \(x\)}}. A set \(C\) is {{c5::closed}} if it contains its {{c4::limit}} points.

============================================================

  

    Definition 8.2.7. A subset \(K\) of a metric space \((X, d)\) is {{c1::compact}} if every {{c5::sequence}} in \(K\) has a {{c2::convergent}} {{c4::subsequence}} that {{c2::converges}} to a {{c5::limit}} {{c3::in \(K\).}}

============================================================

  

    An extremely useful characterization of compactness in \(\mathbf{R}\) is the proposition that a set is {{c4::compact}} if and only if it is {{c2::closed}} and {{c5::{{c3::bounded}}}}. For abstract metric spaces, this proposition only holds in the {{c5::{{c1::forward}}}} direction.

============================================================

  

    (b) Show that if \(K\) is a {{c5::{{c1::compact}}}} subset of the metric space \((X, d)\), then \(K\) is {{c4::{{c2::closed}}}} and {{c4::{{c3::bounded}}}}.

============================================================

  

    It is possible for a {{c1::closed}} and {{c2::bounded}} subset of an {{c5::{{c4::abstract metric space}}}} not to be {{c5::{{c3::compact}}}}

============================================================

  

    Exercise 8.2.13. If \(E\) is a subset of a metric space \((X, d)\), show that {{c4::\(E\)}} is {{c3::nowhere-dense}} in \(X\) if and only if {{c5::{{c1::\(\bar{E}^{c}\)}}}} is {{c5::{{c2::dense}}}} in \(X\).

============================================================

  

    Definition 8.2.9. A set \(A \subseteq X\) is {{c5::dense}} in the metric space \((X, d)\) if {{c1::\(\bar{A}\)}} = {{c2::\(X\)}}. A subset \(E\) of a metric space \((X, d)\) is {{c4::nowhere}}-{{c5::dense}} in \(X\) if {{c3::\(\bar{E}^{\circ}\)}} is {{c4::empty}}.

============================================================

  

    Definition 8.2.8. Given a subset \(E\) of a metric space \((X, d)\):<br><ul><li>The closure {{c4::\(\bar{E}\)}} is the {{c2::union of \(E\)}} together with {{c3::its limit points.&nbsp;}}</li><li>The interior of \(E\) is denoted by {{c5::\(E^{\circ}\)}} and is defined as</li><ul><li>{{c5::\(E^{\circ}\)}} = {{c1::\(\left\{x \in E: \text { there exists } V_{\epsilon}(x) \subseteq E\right\}\)}}</li></ul></ul>

============================================================

  

    Exercise 8.2.11. <br><ul><li>(a) Show that \(E\) is {{c5::closed}} if and only if {{c3::\(\bar{E}\)}} = {{c2::\(E\)}}. Show that \(E\) is {{c4::open}} if and only if {{c1::\(E^{\circ}\)}} = {{c2::\(E\).}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    In Section 3.5, we proved {{c5::Baire's}} Theorem, which states that it is impossible to write the {{c4::real numbers \(\mathbf{R}\)}} as the {{c1::countable}} {{c2::union}} of {{c3::nowhere-dense}} sets.

============================================================

  

    We knew that \(\mathbf{R}\) was too big to be written as the countable union of {{c1::single points}} ( \(\mathbf{R}\) is {{c1::uncountable}}), but {{c5::Baire's}} Theorem improves on this by asserting that the only way to make \(\mathbf{R}\) from a countable union of arbitrary sets is for the {{c2::closure}} of {{c4::at least one}} of these sets to contain {{c3::an interval.<br>}}

============================================================

  

    Theorem 8.2.10. Let \((X, d)\) be a complete metric space, and let \(\left\{O_{n}\right\}\) be a {{c4::countable}} collection of {{c5::dense}}, {{c5::open}} subsets of \(X\). Then, {{c1::\(\bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty}\)}} {{c3::\(O_{n}\)}} is {{c2::not empty.}}

============================================================

  

    Theorem 8.2.10. Let \((X, d)\) be a complete metric space, and let \(\left\{O_{n}\right\}\) be a countable collection of dense, open subsets of \(X\). Then, \(\bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty} O_{n}\) is not empty:<br><br>Proof. When we proved this theorem on \(\mathbf{R}\), completeness manifested itself in the form of the Nested Interval Property. We could derive something akin to NIP in the metric space setting, but instead let's take an approach that uses the convergence of Cauchy sequences (because this is how we have defined completeness).<br><br>Pick \(x_{1} \in O_{1}\). Because \(O_{1}\) is open, there exists an {{c4::\(\epsilon_{1}\)}} {{c5::\(&gt;0\)}} such that {{c1::\(V_{\epsilon_{1}}\) \(\left(x_{1}\right)\)}} {{c3::\(\subseteq\)}} {{c2::\(O_{1}\).}}<br><br>

============================================================

  

    Theorem 8.2.10. Let \((X, d)\) be a complete metric space, and let \(\left\{O_{n}\right\}\) be a countable collection of dense, open subsets of \(X\). Then, \(\bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty} O_{n}\) is not empty.<br><br>Proof. When we proved this theorem on \(\mathbf{R}\), completeness manifested itself in the form of the Nested Interval Property. We could derive something akin to NIP in the metric space setting, but instead let's take an approach that uses the convergence of Cauchy sequences (because this is how we have defined completeness).<br><br>Pick \(x_{1} \in O_{1}\). Because \(O_{1}\) is open, there exists an \(\epsilon_{1}&gt;0\) such that \(V_{\epsilon_{1}}\left(x_{1}\right) \subseteq O_{1}\).<br><br>Exercise 8.2.14. <br><br><ul><li>(a) Give the details for why we know there exists a point \(x_{2} \in\) {{c1::\(V_{\epsilon_{1} }\left(x_{1}\right)\)}} {{c5::\(\cap\)}} {{c4::\(O_{2}\)}} and an \(\epsilon_{2}&gt;0\) satisfying \(\epsilon_{2}&lt;\epsilon_{1} / 2\) with {{c3::\(V_{\epsilon_{2} }\left(x_{2}\right)\)}} contained in {{c4::\(\mathrm{O}_{2}\)}} and</li><ul><li>{{c2::\(\overline{V_{\epsilon_{2} }\left(x_{2}\right)}\)}} \(\subseteq\) {{c1::\(V_{\epsilon_{1} }\left(x_{1}\right)\)}}</li></ul></ul>

============================================================

  

    Theorem 8.2.10. Let \((X, d)\) be a complete metric space, and let \(\left\{O_{n}\right\}\) be a countable collection of dense, open subsets of \(X\). Then, \(\bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty} O_{n}\) is not empty.<br><br>Proof. When we proved this theorem on \(\mathbf{R}\), completeness manifested itself in the form of the Nested Interval Property. We could derive something akin to NIP in the metric space setting, but instead let's take an approach that uses the convergence of Cauchy sequences (because this is how we have defined completeness).<br><br>Pick \(x_{1} \in O_{1}\). Because \(O_{1}\) is open, there exists an \(\epsilon_{1}&gt;0\) such that \(V_{\epsilon_{1}}\left(x_{1}\right) \subseteq O_{1}\).<br><br>Exercise 8.2.14. (a) Give the details for why we know there exists a point \(x_{2} \in V_{\epsilon_{1}}\left(x_{1}\right) \cap O_{2}\) and an \(\epsilon_{2}&gt;0\) satisfying \(\epsilon_{2}&lt;\epsilon_{1} / 2\) with \(V_{\epsilon_{2}}\left(x_{2}\right)\) contained in \(\mathrm{O}_{2}\) and<br><br>\[<br>\overline{V_{\epsilon_{2}}\left(x_{2}\right)} \subseteq V_{\epsilon_{1}}\left(x_{1}\right)<br>\]<br><br><br>(b) Proceed along this line and use the {{c5::{{c1::completeness}}}} of \((X, d)\) to produce a single point \(x\) \(\in\) {{c4::{{c2::\(O_{n}\)}}}} for every {{c4::{{c3::\(n \in \mathbf{N}\).}}}}

============================================================

  

    Theorem 8.2.11 ({{c5::Baire Category}} Theorem). A {{c4::complete}} {{c5::metric space}} is not the {{c2::union}} of a {{c3::countable}} collection of {{c1::nowhere-dense}} sets.

============================================================

  

    This result is called the Baire Category Theorem because it creates two categories of size for subsets in a metric space. A set of "{{c4::first}} category" is one that can be written as a {{c1::countable union}} of {{c2::nowhere-dense}} sets. These are the {{c3::small}}, intuitively {{c3::thin}} subsets of a metric space. We now see that if our metric space is {{c5::complete}}, then it is necessarily of "{{c4::second}} category," meaning it cannot be written as a {{c1::countable union}} of {{c2::nowhere-dense}} sets.

============================================================

  

    Given a subset \(A\) of a complete metric space \(X\), showing that \(A\) is of {{c5::{{c1::first}}}} category is a mathematically precise way of demonstrating that \(A\) constitutes a very {{c4::{{c2::minor}}}} portion of the set \(X\). The term "{{c4::{{c3::meager}}}}" is often used to mean a set of {{c1::first}} category.

============================================================

  

    With the stage set, we now outline the argument that continuous functions that are {{c5::{{c2::differentiable}}}} at {{c4::{{c3::even one}}}} point of \([0,1]\) form a {{c4::{{c1::meager}}}} subset of the metric space \(C[0,1]\).

    Theorem 8.2.12. The set<br><br>\[<br>D=\left\{f \in C[0,1]: f^{\prime}(x) \text { exists for some } x \in[0,1]\right\}<br>\]<br><br>is a set of first category in \(C[0,1]\).<br><br>Proof.     A point&nbsp;\(p \in X\), with X a metric space, is a {{c1::limit point}} of a subset&nbsp;\(E\), if every {{c2::neigbourhoud of p}} contains {{c3::a point \(q\)}} {{c4::\(\neq p\)}} such that&nbsp;{{c3::\(q\)}} {{c5::\(\in E\)}}

============================================================

  

    An {{c5::{{c1::open}}}}-ball is a {{c4::{{c2::neighbourhood}}}} and can be used to define {{c4::{{c3::limit}}}} points&nbsp;

============================================================

  

    For metric spaces endowed with a {{c5::discrete}} metric, {{c3::open}} balls are {{c1::single points}} it {{c4::r&lt;1}} or {{c2::the entirety of the space}}

============================================================

  

    For metric spaces endowed with a {{c4::discrete}} metric, {{c3::closed}} balls are {{c5::{{c1::equivalent}}}} to {{c5::{{c2::open}}}} balls

============================================================

  

    A point \(p \in X\) is a {{c5::limit}} point of a subset E of X if {{c1::every}} {{c2::neighbourhood}} contains {{c3::some point q in E}} {{c4::different from p}}

============================================================

  

    A {{c4::limit}} point {{c1::does not have to}} {{c5::{{c2::belong to}}}} the {{c5::{{c3::subset}}}} it is {{c4::limiting}}

    Which points are limit points?<br><img src="paste-4f295a4aa9d76d0f2289e551c2b12ede6654b8b0.jpg"><br><ul><li>All points on the {{c2::solid}} {{c3::contour}}</li><li>All points on the {{c4::dotted}} {{c3::contour}} since a point {{c4::does not have to be in the subset to be a limit point}}</li><li>All points on the {{c1::inside}}</li><li>The point in the {{c5::middle of the circle}}</li></ul>

    A {{c4::collection}} of {{c1::open}} {{c5::{{c2::balls}}}} is called a {{c5::{{c3::topology}}}}

    <ul><li>\(p \in R\) is an {{c5::{{c1::isolated}}}} point of&nbsp;\(E \subset R\) if&nbsp;\(p\) {{c4::{{c3::\(\in E\)}}}} and&nbsp;\(p\) is {{c4::{{c2::not a limit point of E}}}}&nbsp;</li></ul>

    <ul><li>\(p \in R\) is an {{c1::interior}} point of&nbsp;\(E \subset R\) if there {{c4::exists}} a {{c5::{{c2::neighbourhood \(N\)}}}} of p&nbsp; such that&nbsp;{{c5::{{c2::\(N(p)\)}}}}{{c5::{{c3::&nbsp;\(\subset E\)}}}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    <ul><li>If p is a {{c4::limit}} point of E then {{c2::every}} {{c5::{{c3::neighbourhood of p}}}} contains {{c5::{{c1::infinitely}}}} many points<br></li></ul>

============================================================

  

    <div>Proof for: If p is a limit point of E then every neighbourhood of p contains infinitely many points:<br></div><div><ul><li>Assume there exists a neighbourhood&nbsp;\(N\) of p with {{c3::finitely}} many points:{{c5::&nbsp;\(e_1, \cdots, e_n\)}}</li><li>Let&nbsp;\(r\) ={{c2::&nbsp;\(\min\)}}&nbsp;\(\{\)&nbsp;{{c1::\(d(p, e_i)\)}} &nbsp;\(\}\) because {{c3::finite}} sets have a {{c2::minimum}}</li><li>However, the neighbourhood&nbsp;&nbsp;\(N_r\) has {{c4::no points of E}}</li><li>Contradiction</li></ul><div><img src="paste-1a8a22820ca822360396441e2a16ddf120e0f218.jpg" style="width: 230.817px;"><br></div></div>

============================================================

  

    <ul><li>A set E in a metric space is {{c5::{{c3::open}}}} if {{c4::{{c2::every}}}} point of E is an {{c4::{{c1::interior}}}} point of E</li><li><img src="paste-0008dee878ec461632b6698b81f8ef73fe4716fc.jpg"><br></li></ul>

    <div>{{c5::{{c3::Taking out}}}} a {{c4::{{c2::finite}}}} number of points from an {{c4::{{c1::open}}}} set keeps it {{c1::open}}</div>

    <ul><li>A set E is {{c5::{{c3::closed}}}} if it contains {{c4::{{c1::all}}}} its {{c4::{{c2::limit points}}}}</li><li><img src="paste-23362f43b8c7ccc3ab55d67629eb1b0d350b31fa.jpg"></li></ul>

    <ul><li>A set that is {{c4::both}} {{c2::open}} and {{c5::{{c3::closed}}}}&nbsp; is called {{c5::{{c1::clopen}}}}</li></ul>

    If a subset in a metric space is {{c5::open}} then we can {{c1::perturb}} any point in the subset whilst {{c2::remaining in the subset}}, it is possible to {{c3::move in all directions}} by {{c4::infinitesimal}} amounts {{c2::without exiting the set}}

    Is the set of non-colinear triplets open or closed?<br><ul><li>{{c1::Open}}, meaning we can {{c2::perturb}} any of the points {{c5::{{c3::by small amounts}}}} whilst {{c5::{{c4::still not becoming colinear&nbsp;}}}}</li></ul>

    The {{c5::{{c2::closure}}}} of a set{{c4::{{c3::&nbsp;\(\overline{A}\)}}}} is a {{c4::{{c1::closed}}}} set

============================================================

  

    Why are neighbourhoods open?<br><ul><li>The distance between any two points,&nbsp;\(d(p,q)\) =\(a\) must be less than {{c5::the radius&nbsp;\(r\) of the neighbourhood}}</li><li>Define&nbsp;\(r^\prime\) =&nbsp;{{c5::\(r-a\)}}</li><li>Thus&nbsp;\(N_{r^\prime}\)&nbsp;\(\subset\)&nbsp;\(N\)</li><ul><li>If&nbsp;\(d(x,q)\)&nbsp;\(&lt; r^\prime\)</li><li>Then&nbsp;\(d(x,p)\)&nbsp;\(&lt;\)&nbsp;{{c1::\(d(x,q)\)}} +{{c2::&nbsp;\(d(q,p)\)}} by {{c1::triangle inequality}}</li><li>\(&lt;\)&nbsp;{{c3::\(r^\prime\)&nbsp;\(+\)&nbsp;\(a\)}} = {{c4::r}}</li></ul></ul>

============================================================

  

    A set is {{c1::closed}} iff it is {{c4::equal}} to {{c5::{{c3::its own}}}} {{c5::{{c2::closure}}}}
