    If&nbsp;\(E\){{c2::&nbsp;\(\subset\)}} {{c4::\(F\),}} with F {{c5::{{c3::closed}}}}, then&nbsp;{{c5::{{c1::\(\bar{E}\)}}}}&nbsp;{{c2::\(\subset\)}}&nbsp;{{c4::\(F\)}}

============================================================

  

    If a set E is contained in a closed {{c3::set}}, its {{c1::closure}} must also be {{c5::{{c4::contained}}}} in the {{c3::closed}} set. THus the {{c1::closure}} is the {{c5::{{c2::smallest}}}} {{c3::closed}} set {{c5::{{c4::containing E}}}}

============================================================

  

    The closure of a set&nbsp;\(\bar{E}\) is the smallest closed set containing a subset E of a closed set F:<br>Proof<br><ul><li>Since F is a closed set, if p is a limit point of E then it is a {{c1::limit point of F}}</li><li>Since F {{c2::contains}} all its {{c1::limit points}}, it {{c2::contains}} {{c5::{{c3::all}}}} its {{c1::limit points}} including the {{c5::{{c4::limit points of E}}}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    A set is {{c5::{{c2::open}}}} iff its {{c4::{{c3::complement}}}} is {{c4::{{c1::closed}}}}

============================================================

  

    What is the complement of a subset \(E\) of a metric space \(X,d\)?<br><ul><li>{{c2::\(E^c\)}} ={{c4::&nbsp;\(X\)}} {{c5::{{c1::\(\setminus\)}}}} {{c5::{{c3::\(E\)}}}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Proof that a set is closed iff its complement is open:<br>Proof:<br><ul><li>Assume E is open</li><li>Which means any point is an interior point</li><li>\(\forall\)&nbsp;\(x\)&nbsp;\(\in\)&nbsp;\(E\),&nbsp;\(\exists\)&nbsp;\(N(x)\) s.t&nbsp;\(N(x)\){{c5::&nbsp;\(\subset\)&nbsp;\(E\)}}<br></li><li>\(N(x)\)&nbsp;\(\subset\)&nbsp;\(E\)&nbsp;\(\implies \) \(N(x)\) is {{c1::disjoint from \(E^c\)&nbsp;}}<br></li><li>Pictographically:</li><ul><li>{{c2::<img src="paste-ed4668c47721b35d7232d909a780fffed0004256.jpg">}}<br></li></ul><li>Thus&nbsp;\(\forall\)&nbsp;\(x\)&nbsp;\(\in\)&nbsp;\(E\),&nbsp;\(x\) is {{c4::not}} a {{c3::limit point of&nbsp;\(E^c\)}}</li><li>Which means&nbsp;\(E^c\) contains all of its limit points</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    If a set is {{c3::clopen}} its {{c4::complement}} is also {{c3::clopen}}, e.g,&nbsp;{{c5::{{c1::\(\emptyset\)}}}} and&nbsp;{{c5::{{c2::\( \emptyset^c&nbsp; = R\)}}}}

============================================================

  

    Why are the rationals not open?<br><ul><li>There is no guarantee that we can find an {{c4::open set}} consiting of {{c3::only rationals}} around a rational</li><li>In other words, if we {{c5::{{c1::perturb}}}} a rational there is no guarantee it will {{c5::{{c2::remain rational}}}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    If a set is {{c1::neither}} {{c2::closed}} nor {{c5::{{c3::open}}}} then its {{c5::{{c4::complement}}}} must be {{c1::neither}} {{c2::closed}} nor {{c3::open}}

============================================================

  

    Every point of the union of {{c1::two}} {{c5::open}} sets was {{c2::interior}} to {{c3::one of the sets}}, meaning that they will be {{c2::interior}} to the {{c4::union}}

============================================================

  

    The {{c4::union}} of a {{c1::finite}} number of {{c5::{{c3::closed}}}} sets is {{c5::{{c2::closed}}}}

============================================================

  

    <ul><li>The {{c2::arbitrary}} union of {{c4::open}} sets is {{c5::open}}&nbsp;</li><ul><li>The arbitray intersection is not: \(\cap^\infty(-1/n,1/n)\) =&nbsp;{{c1::\([0]\)}}</li></ul><li>The {{c2::arbitrary}} intersection of {{c5::closed}} sets is {{c4::closed}}</li><ul><li>The arbitrary union is not:&nbsp; &nbsp;\(\cup^\infty(0,n)\) =&nbsp;{{c1::\([0, \infty )\)}}</li></ul><li>The {{c3::finite}} intersection of {{c4::open}} sets is {{c5::open}}:&nbsp;</li><li>The {{c3::finite}} union of {{c5::closed}} sets is {{c4::closed}}</li></ul><br>

============================================================

  

    The arbitrary union of open sets is open:<br><ul><li>\(x \in \cup_{\alpha} O_\alpha\)<br></li><li>This means&nbsp;\(x\)&nbsp;\(\in\) some&nbsp;{{c4::\(O_{\alpha}\)}} which is open<br></li><li>Thus&nbsp;\(\exists\) a {{c2::neighbourhood \(N(x)\)}} s.t&nbsp;{{c2::\(N(x)\)}}&nbsp;{{c5::{{c1::\(\subset\)&nbsp;\(O_{\alpha}\)}}}}</li><li>However, {{c4::\(O_{\alpha}\)&nbsp;}}\(\in\)&nbsp;\(\) {{c5::{{c3::\(\cup_{\alpha} O\)}}}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    The finite intersection of open sets is open:<br>Proof:<br><ul><li><img src="paste-53471e9e1c1d5fc6f7406e8422bd75ce884e4a00.jpg"><br></li><li>If you have a point which is in the intersection you can take the {{c3::minimum-radius}} {{c4::neighbourhood}} accross {{c1::all open sets}} that {{c2::form the intersection}}</li><li>If there are infinitely many this is impossible since the {{c3::minimum}} may {{c5::not exist}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    The finite intersection of open sets is open:<br>Proof:<br><ul><li><img src="paste-53471e9e1c1d5fc6f7406e8422bd75ce884e4a00.jpg" style="width: 599.247px;"><br></li><li>Assume&nbsp;\(x \in \cap O_i\)</li><li>\(\exists\){{c2::&nbsp;\(N_{r_i}(x) \in&nbsp;O_i \) }}\(\forall\) \(O_i\)<br></li><li>let&nbsp;\(r\)&nbsp;\(=\){{c1::&nbsp;\(\min (r_i)\)}}</li><li>Then&nbsp;{{c5::\(N_r(x)\)}} shows that x is {{c3::interior}} to&nbsp;{{c4::\(\cap O_i\)}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    A subset E in a metric space X is {{c3::dense}} if {{c4::every point of X}} is a {{c5::{{c1::limit point of E}}}} or {{c5::{{c2::in E}}}}

============================================================

  

    A subset E of a metric space X is {{c5::{{c3::dense}}}} in X if&nbsp;{{c4::{{c2::\(\bar{E}\)}}}} =&nbsp;{{c4::{{c1::\(X\)}}}}

============================================================

  

    A subset E of a metric space X is {{c3::dense}} if {{c4::every}} {{c5::{{c2::open}}}} set of X contains {{c5::{{c1::a point of E}}}}

============================================================

  

    {{c3::Finite}} sets are {{c4::trivially}} {{c5::{{c1::closed}}}} and {{c5::{{c2::bounded}}}}

============================================================

  

    {{c3::Finite}} sets contain {{c4::both}} their {{c5::{{c2::supremum}}}} and {{c5::{{c1::infimum}}}}, thus they must have a {{c5::{{c2::maximum}}}} and a {{c5::{{c1::minimum}}}}

============================================================

  

    When processing on {{c5::{{c2::finite}}}} sets, the process is {{c4::{{c3::guaranteed}}}} to {{c4::{{c1::terminate}}}}

============================================================

  

    The concept of a {{c1::compact}} set is meant to extend our {{c4::intuition}} from {{c5::{{c2::finite}}}} sets to {{c5::{{c3::infinite}}}} sets

============================================================

  

    <ul><li>An {{c4::open cover}} of&nbsp;\(E\)&nbsp;\(\subset\)&nbsp;\(X\), with X a metric space, is a collection of {{c3::open}} sets \(O_{\alpha}\) whose {{c5::{{c2::union}}}} contains E</li><li>\(E\)&nbsp;\(\in\)&nbsp;{{c5::{{c2::\(\cup\)}}}} {{c5::{{c1::\(O_{\alpha}\)}}}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    <ul><li>A {{c3::subcover}} {{c2::\(O_{\alpha_{\gamma}}\)}} of&nbsp; an {{c5::open cover \(O_{\alpha}\)}} of E in a metric space is a {{c4::sub-collection}} that {{c1::still covers E}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    What is the open cover of&nbsp;\([1/2, 1 )\)?<br><ul><li>\(V_n\)&nbsp;\(=\)&nbsp;{{c1::\((\frac{1}{n}\)}}, {{c2::\(1- \frac{1}{n} )\)}}</li><li>Pictographically: {{c3::<img src="paste-42bcafb8cd534bca23f8a16b307f99c37de8b32a.jpg">}}</li><li>Thus, you get ever-{{c4::increasing}} intervals of open sets that eventually covers every point close to {{c5::1}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    What is the open cover of&nbsp;\([1/2, 1 )\)?<br><ul><li>\(V\) = {{c5::{{c1::\(\{ (0, 2) \}\)}}}} which is a {{c4::{{c2::single set}}}} covering {{c4::{{c3::all of it}}}}<br></li></ul>

============================================================

  

    What is the open cover of&nbsp;\([1/2, 1 )\)?<br><ul><li>\(\{\)&nbsp;\(W_{x}\) {{c1::\(\}_{x \in [1/2, 1]}\)}} such that&nbsp;\(W_x\) =&nbsp;{{c2::\((x - \frac{1}{10}\)}},&nbsp; {{c3::\(x+ \frac{1}{10})\)}}<br></li><li>Pictographically: {{c4::<img src="paste-469125cd5f89a49e0a3abc6f4773dfec42999f10.jpg">}}</li><li>There are in-fact {{c5::uncountably}} many elements in the open cover as there are {{c5::uncountably}} many balls of a {{c5::finite}} radius on the interval</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    {{c1::Subcovers}} may be the {{c2::original}} {{c5::{{c3::covers}}}} {{c5::{{c4::themselves}}}}

============================================================

  

    For the set&nbsp;\([0,1]\) in R<br><ul><li>This set has a {{c5::{{c1::finite}}}} sub-cover if we just cover the {{c4::{{c2::endpoints}}}} making sure to intersect the {{c4::{{c3::radius}}}} around the {{c4::{{c2::end-point}}}} sets</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    A set K of a metric space is {{c5::{{c3::compact}}}} if every {{c4::{{c2::open cover}}}} of K has a {{c4::{{c1::finite subcover}}}}

============================================================

  

    To show that a set is {{c4::not-compact}} you just need to show there {{c5::exists}} a {{c2::single}} {{c3::open-cover}} without a {{c1::finite subcover}}

============================================================

  

    Every set has a {{c4::guaranteed}} {{c3::finite}} cover in the form of the set containing the {{c1::entire metric space {X}}}, this is why {{c5::compactness}} requires {{c2::all}} open covers to have {{c3::finite sub-covers}}

============================================================

  

    Warning: a set which has a {{c2::finite cover}} is not necessarily {{c1::compact}}, {{c5::{{c4::all}}}} {{c5::{{c3::open covers}}}} must have {{c2::finite subcovers}} for {{c1::compactness}} to hold

============================================================

  

    Z in R is {{c5::not compact}} since it has an {{c4::open cover}} which {{c3::does not have a finite subcover:}}<br><ul><li>{{c2::\(V_r(z)\)}} for all&nbsp;\(z\)&nbsp;{{c3::\(in\)&nbsp;\(Z\)}} with {{c1::any}} radius<br></li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Theorem: Finite sets are automatically {{c4::compact}}<br><ul><li>Consider some open cover&nbsp;\(\{G_{\alpha}\}\)&nbsp; convering&nbsp;&nbsp;\(\{x_1,\cdots, x_n\}\)</li><li>Pictographically: {{c1::<img src="paste-d8f93bfd724ccccc10e88432cdaa95b32dc2ae67.jpg">}}</li><li>Since the set is finite:</li><li>\(\forall x_i\) Pick one \( G_{\alpha_i}\) from the collection which {{c5::{{c3::contains&nbsp;\(x_i\)}}}}<br></li><li>Then:&nbsp;{{c5::{{c2::\(\{G_{\alpha_i}: i \in 1,\cdots, n \}\)}}}} covers the set&nbsp;</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Definition: A set&nbsp;\(K\) in a metric space&nbsp;\(X,d\) is {{c5::bounded}} if&nbsp;\(\exists\) a&nbsp; point \(p\) and radius&nbsp;\(r\) such that&nbsp;\(K\)&nbsp;{{c2::\(\subset\)}}&nbsp;{{c3::\(N_r(p) \)}},&nbsp; where {{c3::\(N_r(p) \)}} is the {{c4::r-radius ball around p}}<br>Pictographically:&nbsp; {{c1::<img src="paste-083edbdd71173d007966dbefa31a246e3060de01.jpg">}}

============================================================

  

    <div>For a finite set, you can form a finite subcover by taking the {{c5::{{c2::maximum}}}} {{c4::{{c1::pairwise}}}} distance between any two {{c4::{{c3::balls}}}} covering the set&nbsp;</div>

============================================================

  

    Suppose K is a compact set in the metric space X, proof it is bounded<br><ul><li>Let&nbsp;\(B(x)\)&nbsp;\(=\)&nbsp;{{c4::\(N_{1}(x)\)}}, i.e the {{c4::ball of radius one}}</li><li>One open cover of K is&nbsp;\(\{\){{c5::&nbsp;\(B(x): x \in K\)}} \(\}\)</li><li>By compactness of K there exists a finite subcover&nbsp;</li><li>&nbsp;\(\{\)&nbsp;\(B(x_i): i \in 1,\cdots,n\)\(\}\)&nbsp;</li><li>Let&nbsp;\(R\) =&nbsp;{{c1::\(\max\)}} ({{c2::\(d(x_i,x_j) : i,j \in 1, \cdots, n\)}} )</li><ul><li>This maximum exists because&nbsp;\(1,\cdots,n\) is {{c3::finite}}</li></ul><li>Then&nbsp;\(N_{R+2}(x_1)\) contains all of&nbsp;\(K\)</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    An {{c4::open}} set in R is no longer {{c3::necessarily}} {{c4::open}} once you go to {{c5::{{c2::higher dimensions}}}}<br><br>Pictographically: {{c5::{{c1::<img src="paste-4e88b9a510f63335ce3379ba8becf0460f1249de.jpg">}}}}

============================================================

  

    Being an {{c2::open}} set depends on the {{c3::metric space}} in which you are {{c5::{{c4::embedded}}}} (think of an {{c2::open}} set of R being translated to R^2), whilst being a {{c5::{{c1::compact}}}} set does not

============================================================

  

    RELATIVE OPEN SETS:<br><ul><li>If&nbsp;\(Y\)&nbsp;\(\subset\)&nbsp;\(X\), where&nbsp;\(X\) is a metric space, then&nbsp;\(Y\) is also a metric space since it inherits the notion of distance</li><li>Pictographically: {{c1::<img src="paste-40ea846f0a2b4c0a6bfc1fe414fc8dbcb43d6300.jpg">}}</li><li>The notion of an open ball can differ between the two, e.g: an open ball in the embedded space may not be open in the embedding space</li><li>When will a set in Y be open?&nbsp;</li><ul><li>Some points may still have a ball around it {{c2::<img src="paste-ec66f70cabb82d3df42ef9ca9097967ea4b3c71f.jpg">}}</li><li>Even if a point is on the edge of Y then a ball around it is still open since there are {{c4::no points of X around}}</li><li>{{c3::<img src="paste-745ca383ce066751921ead16c4f76786abc1e88d.jpg">}}<br></li><li>However the same edge point would be {{c5::closed}} in X</li></ul></ul>

============================================================

  

    Definition: A set&nbsp;\(U \subset Y\) is {{c2::open}} {{c1::relative to}}&nbsp;\(Y\) if every point of&nbsp;\(U\) is an {{c4::interior}} point of{{c5::&nbsp;\(U\)}} using neighbourhouds in&nbsp;{{c3::\(Y\)}}

============================================================

  

    Theorem:&nbsp; If \(E\)&nbsp;{{c5::\(\subset\)&nbsp;}}\(Y\){{c5::\( \subset\)&nbsp;}}\(X\), then&nbsp;\(E\) is {{c4::open}} in&nbsp;\(Y\) iff&nbsp;\(E\) =&nbsp;{{c2::\(Y\)}}&nbsp;{{c1::\(\cap\)}}{{c2::&nbsp;\(G \)}} for some&nbsp;{{c2::\(G\)}} {{c3::open}} in X

============================================================

  

    When is a set&nbsp;\(E\)&nbsp;\(\subset Y \subset X\) open in&nbsp;\(Y\)?<br><ul><li>Iff</li><li>\(E\)&nbsp; =&nbsp;\(Y\)&nbsp;\(\cap\)&nbsp;\(G\) for some&nbsp;\(G\) open in&nbsp;\(X\)</li></ul><div>Proof:</div><div><ul><li>Backward direction: \(Y\)&nbsp;\(\cap\)&nbsp;\(G\) must be open since you can always use the {{c4::same neighbourhood as in X}} but with {{c3::only points that are part of Y}}<br></li><li>Forward: if a set E is open in Y, every point has a neighourhood in E, \(N_r(x)\) \( \subset\) {{c5::\(Y \cap E\)}}, a set G can be obtained as:</li><ul><li>G = {{c1::\(\cup_{x \in E}\)}} {{c2::\(N_{r_x}(x)\)}}&nbsp;\(\subset\)&nbsp;\(X\) which is the {{c1::union}} of open balls in X and thus open<br></li></ul></ul><div>&nbsp;</div></div>

============================================================

  

    {{c1::Compactness}} as a {{c4::property of a set}} does {{c5::not actually depend}} on the {{c2::metric space}} in which {{c3::the set is included}}

============================================================

  

    Theorem: For a subset&nbsp;\(E\) \(\subset\)\(Y\)&nbsp;&nbsp;\(\subset\)&nbsp;\(X\), then&nbsp;\(E\) is {{c5::open}} in {{c4::\(Y\)}} iff&nbsp;\(E\) =&nbsp;{{c4::\(Y\)}}&nbsp;{{c1::\(\cap \)}}{{c3::&nbsp;\(G\)}} for {{c3::some&nbsp;\(G\)}} {{c5::open}}{{c2::&nbsp;in&nbsp;\(X\)}}

============================================================

  

    Theorem: If \(Y\)&nbsp;{{c2::\(\subset\)}}{{c4::&nbsp;\(X\)}}, then&nbsp;\(K\) {{c1::compact}} {{c5::in&nbsp;\(Y\)}} is equvalent to&nbsp;\(K\) being {{c1::compact}} {{c3::in X}}

============================================================

  

    Theorem: If \(Y\)&nbsp;\(\subset\)&nbsp;\(X\), then&nbsp;\(K\) compact in&nbsp;\(Y\) is equvalent to&nbsp;\(K\) being compact in X<br><br><b>Forward</b> Proof that \(K\) compact in&nbsp;\(Y\) is equvalent to&nbsp;\(K\) being compact in X<br><ul><li>Assume K is compact in Y</li><li>Consider an open cover of K in X, \(O_{\alpha}\), we need to find a finite subcover</li><li>Since K is also&nbsp; subset of Y, we can consider {{c1::\(O_{\alpha} \cap Y\)}}</li><li>Since every set {{c1::\(O_{\alpha} \cap Y\)}} is {{c2::in&nbsp;\(Y\)}}, then the open cover admits a {{c3::finite sub-cover \(O_{i} \cap Y\)}}<br></li><li>Then the {{c4::\(O_{i}\) original}} sets cover&nbsp;\(K\) {{c5::in X}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Theorem: If \(Y\)&nbsp;\(\subset\)&nbsp;\(X\), then&nbsp;\(K\) compact in&nbsp;\(Y\) is equvalent to&nbsp;\(K\) being compact in X<br><br><b>Backward</b> Proof that \(K\) compact in&nbsp;\(Y\) is equvalent to&nbsp;\(K\) being compact in X<br><ul><li>Assume K is compact in X</li><li>Assume an open cover in Y exists&nbsp;\(O_{\alpha}\)</li><li>Every element&nbsp;&nbsp;\(O_{\alpha}\) =&nbsp;\(Y\)&nbsp;\(\cap\)&nbsp;{{c1::\(G_{\alpha}\)}} for some {{c2::open}} {{c1::\(G_{\alpha}\)}} in {{c3::X}}</li><li>Then {{c1::\(G_{\alpha}\)}} is an open cover in X and thus admits a {{c5::finite subcover}} {{c4::\(G_{i}\)}}</li><li>Then {{c4::\(O_{i}\)}} is a {{c5::finite subcover}} for K in {{c5::Y}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Compactness is an intrinsic property of a set, since {{c1::compactness}} in a {{c4::subset}} always implies {{c1::compactness}} in the {{c3::enclosing set}} and {{c5::vice-versa}} (as long as the {{c2::same metric}} is used in both)

============================================================

  

    {{c3::Compact}} sets in an {{c5::abstract}} {{c4::metric space}} are {{c1::closed}} and {{c2::bounded}}<br><br>Pictographically:<br><ul><li>{{c2::<img src="paste-d3410d72664b18a4d8450b47ee162094cf8d0ddc.jpg">}}<br></li><li>I.e we can show the complements of compact sets are {{c1::open}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Proof that compact sets are closed:<br><ul><li>Pictographically: {{c1::<img src="paste-864c826b4377fd119e0a7488b59bc23b51c3a0fb.jpg">}}<br></li><li>Let K be compact and consider&nbsp;\(p\)&nbsp;\(\not \in\)&nbsp;\(K\)</li><li>We want to show that&nbsp;\(p\) has a neighbourhood that does not intersect K which is the same as showing it is not a limit point of K i.e it is interior to&nbsp;\(K^c\)</li><li>Or it is the same as showing that the complement of K is open</li><li>\(\forall\)&nbsp;\(q\)&nbsp;\(in\)&nbsp;\(K\), let&nbsp;{{c2::\(V_q\)}} =&nbsp;{{c1::\(N_{r/2} (q)\)}} be a {{c2::ball around q}} and {{c1::\(U_p\)}} =&nbsp;{{c2::\(N_{r/2} (p)\)}}</li><ul><li>&nbsp;Where&nbsp;\(r\) =&nbsp;{{c3::\(d\)&nbsp;\( (p,q)\)}}</li></ul><li>Notice that the collection {{c2::\({V_q}\)}} is an {{c4::open cover}} of K</li><li>By {{c5::compactness}} of K there exists a finite subcover&nbsp;\(V_{q_{n} }\)</li><li>Which allows us to pick the equivalent finite set from the complement of K</li><li>Define&nbsp;\(W\) =&nbsp;\(\cap_n\) \(U_{q_{n} }\) which is open because it is a finite intersection of open sets</li><li>Specifically it is a ball whose radius is the minimum distance between&nbsp;\(p_i\) and&nbsp;\(q_i\)</li><li>Pictographically: {{c3::<img src="paste-40a5b413ea121ae49567ef8eefa6c56396c2bf5e.jpg">}}</li><li>\(W\) \(\cap\) \(V_{q_i}\) =&nbsp;\(\emptyset\) because&nbsp;\(W\)&nbsp;\(\in\)&nbsp;\(U_{q_i}\) and&nbsp; \(U_{q_i}\) &nbsp;\(\cap\)\(V_{q_i}\) =&nbsp;\(\emptyset\)</li><li>Thus W is the desired neighbourhood showing that any arbitrary point p is interrior&nbsp;to&nbsp;\(K^c\) meaning that&nbsp; \(K^c\) is open, making&nbsp;\(K\) closed</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    {{c3::Closed}} sets are not necessarily {{c1::compact}}, for example the {{c5::{{c2::entire real line}}}} is not {{c5::{{c4::bounded}}}} despite being {{c3::closed}}

============================================================

  

    A {{c1::closed}} {{c4::subset}} B of a {{c5::{{c2::compact}}}} set A is {{c5::{{c3::also compact}}}}

============================================================

  

    A closed set subset B of a compact set K is also compact<br>Proof:<br><ul><li>Pictographically: {{c2::<img src="paste-79cebeddeb9dc2c92d2259a1776cd7cc45f6318f.jpg">}}</li><li>Consider an {{c1::open cover}} of B,&nbsp;{{c1::\( {O_{\alpha} }\)}}</li><li>Pictographically:&nbsp; {{c1::<img src="paste-93cca5e41f2d3dbccb9633e19f1422c9d6253674.jpg">}}</li><li>Since B is {{c2::embedded in K}},&nbsp;&nbsp;{{c1::&nbsp;\( {O_{\alpha} }\)}} {{c2::\(\in\) \(K\)}}</li><li>Since {{c4::B}} is {{c3::closed}},{{c3::&nbsp;\(B^c\)}} is {{c4::open}}&nbsp;</li><li>Pictographically: {{c3::<img src="paste-eea0fe6c837389cef626738870dd46c4af1264bf.jpg">}}</li><li>This means that&nbsp;{{c1::\(O_{\alpha}\)}}&nbsp;\(\cup\)&nbsp;{{c3::\(B^c\)}} is an open cover of K</li><li>By compactness, there must be a finite subcover {{{c5::\(O_{\alpha_{i} }\)}} {{c3::\(B^c\)}}}</li><li>Since&nbsp;{{c4::\(B\)}} \(\cap\) {{c3::\(B^c\)}} = \(\emptyset\), the finite subcover&nbsp; {{c5::\(O_{\alpha_{i} }\)}} covers B&nbsp;&nbsp;</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    If you have a {{c4::closed}} set F and a {{c3::compact}} set K in a metric space X, then their {{c1::intersection}} is going to be {{c2::compact}}<br>Pictographically:{{c5::&nbsp;<img src="paste-49a5ebdc62c0cf09e18d7c2c5fecd8865482da91.jpg">}}

============================================================

  

    Theorem:The {{c5::intersection}} of {{c3::nested}} {{c4::closed}} {{c1::intervals}} in {{c2::R}} is not empty, which means the {{c5::intersection}} {{c4::closed}} {{c3::nested}} {{c2::\(K\)-cells}} in&nbsp;{{c1:: \(R^k\) }} is {{c5::not empty}}<br>Pictographically:&nbsp; {{c3::<img src="paste-790e5ba446bf632b069fa2f8363543fc1d621e51.jpg">}}

============================================================

  

    Theorem:The {{c5::intersection}} of {{c3::nested}} {{c4::closed}} {{c1::intervals}} in {{c2::R}} is not empty, which means the {{c5::intersection}} {{c4::closed}} {{c3::nested}} {{c2::\(K\)-cells}} in&nbsp;{{c1:: \(R^k\) }} is {{c5::not empty}}<br>Pictographically:&nbsp; {{c3::<img src="paste-790e5ba446bf632b069fa2f8363543fc1d621e51.jpg">}}

============================================================

  

    Proof for the nested interval property:<br><ul><li>Pictographically:&nbsp; {{c2::<img src="paste-7d1661b37ebc51d59c54e68c0e319a11b2c3fdc0.jpg">}}</li><li>\(I_n\) =&nbsp;\([a_n, b_n]\)<br></li><li>\(I_{n+1}\)&nbsp;\(\subset\)&nbsp;\(I_n\)<br></li><li>Let x =&nbsp;{{c1::\(\sup\)}}{{c2::&nbsp;\({a_i}\)}} which exists because {{c3::all intervals are bounded by&nbsp;\(b_1\)}}</li><li>\(x\)&nbsp;\(\geq\)&nbsp;\(a_i\) because it is the {{c1::supremum}}<br></li><li>\(x\)&nbsp;\(\ &lt; \)&nbsp;{{c4::\(b_n\)}} for {{c4::all&nbsp;\(n\)}} because&nbsp;{{c4::\(b_n\)}}&nbsp;{{c5::\(\geq\)&nbsp;\(a_i\)}} for {{c5::all i}}<br></li><li>Thus&nbsp;\(x\)&nbsp;\(\in\)&nbsp;\(I_n\) for all n and thus&nbsp;\(x\)&nbsp;\(\in\)&nbsp;\(\cap I_n\)</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Proof that R is uncountable:<br><ul><li>Pictographically: {{c1::<img src="paste-475b17f8a3bfbb503aaa63e15f3725581f074712.jpg">}}</li><li>Suppose it were countable and we have a list of all elements&nbsp;\(\{x_i \}\)</li><li>We can choose&nbsp;{{c2::\(I_1\)}} such that{{c3::&nbsp;\(x_i\)}}&nbsp;{{c4::\(\not \in\)}}&nbsp;{{c2::\(I_1\)}}</li><li>Then choose&nbsp;{{c3::\(I_n\)}}&nbsp;\(\subset\)&nbsp;\(I_{n-1}\) such that&nbsp;{{c2::\(x_n\)}}&nbsp;{{c4::\(\not \in\)}}&nbsp;{{c3::\(I_n\)}}</li><li>By the nested interval property there exists an element in&nbsp;{{c5::\(\cap_n\)}}&nbsp;{{c3::\(I_n\)}} which is {{c1::not on the original list}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    &nbsp;Theorem: Any {{c1::closed}} {{c2::interval}} in {{c5::{{c4::R}}}} is {{c5::{{c3::compact}}}}

============================================================

  

    Theorem: Any {{c1::k-cell}} is {{c2::compact}} in{{c3::&nbsp;\(R^k\)}}, because they are {{c5::products}} of {{c4::closed}} {{c5::intervals}} (i.e {{c5::boxes}})

============================================================

  

    Proof that any closed interval [a,b] in R is compact:<br><ul><li>Assume the interval is not compact</li><li>Then there exists an open cover \(O_{\alpha}\) without a finite subcover&nbsp;</li><li>Pictographically: <img src="paste-f454a7e35dc3ea3bcc0fdc1e9066cf9636efb0ae.jpg"></li><li>Divide the interval into&nbsp;\([a, c_1]\) and&nbsp;&nbsp;\([c_1, b]\)</li><li>Thus&nbsp;\(O_{\alpha}\) covers&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;\([a, c_1]\)&nbsp; and \([c_1, b]\)</li><li>At least one of these intervals must also not have a finite subcover otherwise you coult just union two finite subcovers<br></li><li>Pick the one without a finite subcover&nbsp;\(I_1\) (WLOG \([a,c_1]\)</li><li>Then repeat the process splitting&nbsp;\(I_1\) into two halves by&nbsp;\(c_2\) to get&nbsp;\(I_2\)</li><li>Generally&nbsp;\(I_{n+1}\)&nbsp;\(\subset\)&nbsp;\(I_n\) created by difviding&nbsp;\(I_{n}\) by&nbsp;\(c_n\) such that we choose the </li><li>half without a finite subcover</li><li></li><li>By the nested interval property there exists \(x\) such that {{c5::\(\cap_n I_n\)}} =&nbsp;\(x\)&nbsp;<br></li><li>Pictographically: {{c2::<img src="paste-dd9c9e556ab016c8ec5ac318d70901050a90a0c3.jpg">}}</li><li>\(x\)&nbsp;\(\in\)&nbsp;\(O_{\alpha^\prime}\) for some&nbsp;\(\alpha^\prime\)<br></li><li>So there exists&nbsp;\(r\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(0\) such that&nbsp;\(\exists\)&nbsp;{{c2::\(N_{r}(x)\)}}&nbsp;\(\subset\)&nbsp;\(\)&nbsp;{{c1::&nbsp;\(O_{\alpha^\prime}\)}}</li><li>Since the intervals get arbitrarily small, there {{c3::exists&nbsp;\(I_m\)&nbsp;}}\(\subset \)&nbsp;&nbsp;{{c2::\(N_{r}(x)\)}}&nbsp; meaning that&nbsp; {{c1::\(O_{\alpha^\prime}\)}} {{c4::covers}} {{c3::\(I_m\)}}</li><li>Which contradicts \(I_m\) not having a finite subcover</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    What is the {{c5::Heine-Borel}} Theorem?<br><ul><li>In {{c4::R}} or{{c4::&nbsp;\(R^n\),}} a set K is {{c1::compact}} iff it is {{c2::closed}} and {{c3::bounded}}&nbsp;<br></li></ul>

============================================================

  

    <div>In {{c4::arbitrary metric spaces}}, the {{c3::Heniel-Borel}} theorem is {{c5::{{c1::not true}}}} as {{c5::{{c2::closed}}}} and {{c5::{{c2::bounded}}}} sets are {{c5::{{c1::not necessarily compact}}}}</div>

============================================================

  

    Proof for the Heinel-Borel theorem in R: A set is K compact iff it is closed and bounded<br><ul><li>Backward direction, assume a set is closed and bounded</li><li>Since K is bounded&nbsp;\(K\)&nbsp;{{c5::\(\subset\)}}&nbsp;{{c1::\([-r, r]\)}} or of an {{c2::n-cell}} if operating in {{c4::higher dim spaces}}</li><ul><li>Since {{c1::\([-r, r]\)}}&nbsp; is {{c3::compact}} then K must be {{c3::compact}}&nbsp;</li></ul></ul>

============================================================

  

    <ul><li>Cosinder be the set of all bounded functions&nbsp;\(f:\)&nbsp;\(R\)\(\to\)&nbsp;\(R\)</li><li>With&nbsp;\(d\)&nbsp;\((f,g)\) =&nbsp;\(\sup\)&nbsp;\(|f(x) - g(x) |\)</li><li>The neighbourhood around a particular function will look like: {{c1::<img src="paste-45363832d073aaba672aa7b66d3c264bca6dcb11.jpg">}}&nbsp;i.e the neighbourhood is the {{c3::ribbon of all functions}} with {{c4::the same shape}} with&nbsp;{{c5::\(\epsilon\) bias from&nbsp;\(f\)}}</li><li>An example of a closed and bounded set which is not compact, pictographically: {{c2::<img src="paste-98212ab2e5dc2d95e9da8b3984f4e40614d57c18.jpg">}}</li><li>Such a set is closed and bounded, however it is not compact becuase it is equivalent to the discrete metric as it has no limit points</li></ul><br>

============================================================

  

    <div>Theorem \(K\) is {{c4::compact}} iff every {{c3::infinite subset}} of&nbsp;\(K\) has a {{c5::{{c1::limit point}}}} {{c5::{{c2::in&nbsp;\(K\)}}}}<br></div>

============================================================

  

    A set is compact iff every infinite subset has a limit point also in K:<br>Proof:<br><ul><li>Assume a set is compact K</li><li>If no point of K is a limit point of an infinite subset E</li><li>Then each point&nbsp;\(q\)&nbsp;\(\in\)&nbsp;\(E\) has a {{c1::neighbourhood&nbsp;\(V_q\)}} that contains {{c2::no other point than&nbsp;\(q\)}}</li><li>However, {{c1::\(\{V_q\}\)}} {{c4::cover&nbsp;\(E\)}} with {{c3::no finite subcover}}</li><li>Pictographically: {{c5::<img src="paste-f5ccb2108a396b744cad823a630c8b975c93422b.jpg">}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    A set is compact iff every infinite subset has a limit point also in K,&nbsp;<br>Proof for \(R^n\) only, but the idea generalizes to all abstract metric spaces:<br><ul><li>Assume that every infinite subset of K has a limit point also in K, we want to show that K is closed and bounded</li><li>Assume it is not closed and bounded:</li><li>If K is not bounded, then we can choose a sequence&nbsp;\(x_n\) such that&nbsp;{{c3::\(|x_n - 0|\)}}&nbsp;{{c2::\(&gt;\)}}&nbsp;{{c1::\(n\)}}</li><ul><li>For any point in the sequence, we would need to have neighbourhoods containing {{c5::infinitely many points}} which is not possible as {{c4::there would only be finitely many to the left}}</li><li>Pictographically:{{c2::<img src="paste-4cd5e8b6e379c85deb4a6d57d8f9c18b90a47ba9.jpg">}}</li></ul><li>Suppose K is not closed:</li><ul><li>Then there exists&nbsp;\(p\)&nbsp;\(\not \in\)&nbsp;\(K\) which is a limit point of an infinite subset E</li><li>Choose&nbsp;\(x_n\) such that&nbsp;{{c4::\(d(x_n,p)\)}}{{c1::&nbsp;\(&lt;\)&nbsp;}}{{c2::\(\frac{1}{n}\)}}</li><li>This sequence has {{c3::a limit point in p and no other}}</li><li>Pictographically:{{c1::<img src="paste-bf5b688bbcabcac6a4024e4505b35840046a9dfb.jpg">}}</li></ul></ul>

============================================================

  

    {{c5::Blozano-Weirstrass}} theorem in higher dimensions:<ul><li>Every infinite {{c1::bounded}} {{c4::subset}} of&nbsp;{{c3::\(R^n\)}} has {{c2::a limit point}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Proof for Bolzano-Weirstrass: every infinite bounded subset of&nbsp;\(R^n\) has a limit point:<br><ul><li>&nbsp;If a subset&nbsp;\(E\) is {{c5::bounded}} then&nbsp;\(E\) is in some {{c4::compact}}&nbsp;{{c2::\(K\)-cell&nbsp;}}</li><li>Since it is a {{c5::bounded}} subset of a {{c4::compact}} set, it has a {{c1::limit point}} in the&nbsp;{{c2::\(K\)-cell}} by the {{c3::Heinel-Borel}} theorem</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    What is Cantor's Finite Intersection property?<br><ul><li>Take a collection of {{c5::compact}} subsets&nbsp;\(K_{\alpha}\) of some arbitrary metric space&nbsp;\(X\)</li><li>If any {{c4::finite}} sub-collection has a {{c2::non-empty}} {{c3::intersection}}, then {{c1::\(\cap_{\alpha} K_{\alpha}\)}}&nbsp;{{c2::\(\neq\)&nbsp;\(0\)}}</li><li>This is a generalization of the nested interval property to arbitrary metric spaces</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    What is Cantor's Finite Intersection property?<br><ul><li>A collection of compact subsets&nbsp;\(K_{\alpha}\) of some arbitrary metric space&nbsp;\(X\)</li><li>If any finite sub-collection has a non-empty intersection,&nbsp;\(\cap_{\alpha} K_{\alpha}\)&nbsp;\(\neq\)&nbsp;\(\emptyset\)</li><li>This is a generalization of the nested interval property to arbitrary metric spaces</li></ul><div>Proof:</div><div><ul><li>Assume that it is possible for a finite sub-collection to have a non-empty intersection but the intersection of all Ks to be empty</li><li>Let&nbsp;\(U_{\alpha}\) =&nbsp;{{c1::\(K_{\alpha}^c\)}}, which is open</li><li>Pick an arbitrary K in&nbsp;\(K_{\alpha}\)</li><li>If&nbsp;{{c3::\(\cap_{\alpha}\)&nbsp;}}\(K_{\alpha}\) =&nbsp;{{c1::\(\emptyset\)}} then&nbsp;\( \{ U_{\alpha} \}\) would cover&nbsp;\(K\) which we know to be compact</li><ul><li>This is because if no point is in the intersection than all points must be in the complement of one K</li></ul><li>Which implies there exists&nbsp;{{c4::\(\{U_{\alpha_n} \}\)}} {{c5::covering&nbsp;\(K\)}}</li><li>Implying that&nbsp; {{c3::\( \cap_{i=1}^{n}\)}} {{c2::\(K_{\alpha_i}\)}} \(\cap\) \(K\) =&nbsp;{{c1::\(\emptyset\)}}</li></ul></div>

============================================================

  

    Theorem: For \(\{K_{\alpha}\}\) compact subsets of a metric space X, if for any {{c1::finite subcollection}}&nbsp;&nbsp;{{c2::\(\cap_i\)}} {{c1::\(K_{i}\)&nbsp;}}{{c4::\(\neq\)}}{{c3::&nbsp;\(\emptyset\)}} then&nbsp;&nbsp;{{c5::\(\cap_\alpha\)}} \(K_{\alpha}\)&nbsp;{{c4::\(\neq\)}}&nbsp;{{c3::\(\emptyset\)}}&nbsp;

============================================================

  

    The {{c2::intersection}} of a sequence of {{c1::nested}} {{c5::{{c4::compact}}}} subsets of a metric space is {{c5::{{c3::not empty}}}}

============================================================

  

    A space X&nbsp; is {{c1::compact}} iff {{c4::any collection}} of {{c5::{{c2::closed}}}} sets&nbsp;{{c4::\(\{D_{\alpha} \}\)}} in the space satisfies the {{c5::{{c2::finite}}}} {{c5::{{c3::intersection}}}} property&nbsp;

============================================================

  

    &nbsp;A space X is compact iff any collection of closed sets satisfied the finite intersection property<br><br>Proof:<br><ul><li>Assume X is compact and that we have a collection of closed sets&nbsp;\(D_{\alpha}\) \(\subset X\)</li><li>Since these are {{c3::closed}} subsets of a compact space, they are {{c1::also compact}}</li><li>Since they are {{c1::compact}}, it is {{c3::necessarily true}} that any {{c2::finite}} subcollection having {{c5::non}}-{{c4::empty}} {{c2::intersection}} implies&nbsp;&nbsp;{{c2::\(\cap_{\alpha}\)}} \(D_{\alpha} \)&nbsp;{{c5::\(\neq\)}}{{c4::&nbsp;\(\emptyset\)}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    &nbsp;A space X is compact iff any collection of closed sets satisfied the finite intersection property (FIP)<br><br>Proof by contradiction:<br><ul><li>Assume that any collection of closed sets satisfied the FIP</li><li>Then, for any open cover&nbsp;\(O_{\alpha}\) its {{c3::complement&nbsp;&nbsp;\(O_{\alpha}^c\)}} is {{c1::closed}}</li><li>Thus {{c1::\(\cap_{i} \)}} {{c3::\(O_{i}^c\)}} {{c5::\(\neq\)&nbsp;}}{{c2::\(\emptyset\)}} implies {{c2::\(\cap_{\alpha}\)}} {{c4::\(O_{\alpha}^c\)}}&nbsp;{{c5::\(\neq\)}}&nbsp;{{c2::\(\emptyset\)}}</li><li>Negate the entire expression via de-morgan</li><li>If {{c2::\(\cup_{\alpha}\)}} {{c4::\(O_{\alpha}\)}}&nbsp;{{c5::\(=\)}}&nbsp;{{c1::\(X\)}} then {{c1::\(\cup_{i}\)}} {{c3::\(O_{i}\)}}{{c5::&nbsp;\(=\)&nbsp;}}{{c1::\(X\)}}</li><li>Thus every open cover has a finite subcover</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    The cantor set C consists of real numbers whose {{c1::ternary expansion}} contains {{c2::only}} {{c3::0s}} and {{c3::2s}}, which shows that every point is a limit point (consider sequences of more accurate {{c1::ternary expansions}}), that C is {{c5::uncountable}} ({{c4::diagonalization}} argument), and it contains non-endpoints (consider .02020202 i.e not a number ending in all 2s or all 0s)

============================================================

  

    The cantor set has {{c4::measure}} {{c1::0}}, meaning that&nbsp; for any{{c2::&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)}} {{c3::\(&gt;\) \(0\)}} it can be {{c5::covered}} by intervals of {{c4::total length}}&nbsp;{{c3::\(&lt;\)}} {{c2::\( \epsilon\)}}

============================================================

  

    What is a basis for the topology of a metric space?<br><ul><li>A basis is a {{c5::collection&nbsp;\(\{V_{\alpha}\}\)}} such that<br></li><li>\(\forall\)&nbsp;\(x\)&nbsp;\(\in\)&nbsp;\(U\), where&nbsp;\(U\) is any {{c4::open}} set<br></li><li>\(\exists\){{c1::&nbsp;\(V_{\beta}\)}} such that&nbsp;{{c3::\(x\)&nbsp;\(\in\)}}&nbsp;\(\) {{c1::\(V_{\beta}\)}} and {{c1::\(V_{\beta}\)}}&nbsp;{{c2::\(\subset\)&nbsp;\(U\)}}<br></li><li>Meaning that every {{c4::open}} set is the union of base elements</li><li>Thus any set that that has a countable basis, must be small</li><li>And a compact metric space has a countable basis (countable dense subset)</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    What is a separated set?<br><br><ul><li>Picographically connected vs separated: {{c4::<img src="paste-2f5fb451489476cc9349ed525cfa095a422d333d.jpg">}}</li><li>Two sets&nbsp;\(A\) and&nbsp;\(B \) in a metric space&nbsp;\(X\) are {{c5::separated}} if</li><li>{{c1::\(A \cap \bar{B}\)}} =&nbsp;{{c3::\(\emptyset\)}}<br></li><li>{{c2::\(\bar{A}&nbsp;\cap B\)}} =&nbsp;{{c3::\(\emptyset\)}}</li><li>And thus no limit point of {{c1::one}} is a limit point of {{c2::the other}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    When is a set E {{c5::connected}}?<br><ul><li>A set E is {{c5::connected}} if it is {{c2::not}} the {{c3::union}} of {{c4::two}} {{c1::separated}} sets</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Is the set of all pairs&nbsp; (x,y) of rational x,y connected? Why?<br><br><ul><li>Pictographically : <img src="paste-4a299da253efb63ed253fbc7e6d1895f3c9fb557.jpg"></li><li>Since we are only talking about {{c2::rationals}}, dividing A and B by&nbsp;{{c1::\(\pi\)}} means that {{c3::neither contains}} {{c1::&nbsp;\(\pi\)}} on the x-axis</li><li>Thus, while&nbsp;{{c1::\(\pi\)}} is a {{c4::limit point}} of {{c5::both}}, the two sets are separated&nbsp;</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Proof that the any interval [a,b] is connected<br><ul><li>Assume there exists a separation A,B</li><li>with&nbsp;\(a\)&nbsp;\(\in A\) and&nbsp;\(b\)&nbsp;\(\in\)&nbsp;\(B\)</li><li>Then&nbsp;\(\bar{A} \cap B\)&nbsp;\(\neq\)&nbsp;\(\emptyset\) and vice-versa</li><li>Lets consider&nbsp;\(s\) \(=\) {{c1::\(\sup\)&nbsp;\(A\)}}</li><li>Then \(s\)&nbsp;{{c4::\(\in\)}}&nbsp;{{c2::\(\bar{A}\)}} because it is either {{c3::in the set}} or {{c3::a limit point}}</li><li>Thus&nbsp;&nbsp;\(s\) {{c5::\(\not \in\)&nbsp;}}{{c4::\(B\)}}</li><li>Since \([a,b]\) =&nbsp;\(A \cup B\), then&nbsp;\(s\) must be in&nbsp;\(A\)</li><li>If&nbsp;\(s\)&nbsp;{{c4::\(\in\)}}&nbsp;\(A\) then&nbsp;&nbsp;\(s\)&nbsp;{{c5::\(\not \in\)}}&nbsp;\(\bar{B}\)</li><li>Then \(\exists \epsilon\) such that&nbsp;{{c3::\(V_{\epsilon}(s)\)}}&nbsp;{{c5::\(\not \in \)}}&nbsp;\(B\)&nbsp;</li><li>Since&nbsp;&nbsp;\([a,b]\) =&nbsp;\(A \cup B\),&nbsp; {{c3::\(V_{\epsilon}(s)\)&nbsp;}}\(\in\)&nbsp;\(A\)</li><li>Which contradicts&nbsp;\(s\) being the supremum as a larger number exists in the nighbourhood.</li><li>If&nbsp;\(s\) is one of the endpoints we can use the infimum of the other set</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    <ul><li>A collection of sets has the {{c1::Finite Intersection Condition}} if every {{c4::finite}} subcollection has a {{c2::non-empty}} {{c3::intersection}}</li><li>A set is compact iff any collection of {{c5::closed}} sets&nbsp;\(K_{\alpha}\) that has the {{c1::finite intersection condition}} has a {{c2::non-empty}} {{c3::intersection}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    A {{c4::sequence}}&nbsp;{{c3::\(\{p_n\}\)}} in a metric space X is a {{c5::function}}&nbsp;\(f:\) {{c1::\(N\)}} {{c5::\(\to\)}} {{c2::\(X\)}} such that&nbsp;\(f\){{c1::\((n)\)}} =&nbsp;{{c3::\(p_n\)}}

============================================================

  

    <ul><li>A sequence&nbsp;\(p_n\) in a metric space \(X\) converges if&nbsp;\(\exists\)&nbsp;\(p\)&nbsp;\(\in\)&nbsp;\(X\) such that&nbsp;\(\forall\)&nbsp;{{c3::\(\epsilon\)}}{{c5::&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(0\)}},&nbsp;\(\exists\)&nbsp;\(N\) such that&nbsp;\(\forall\)&nbsp;\(n\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(N\) implies {{c2::\(d\)}}{{c1::\((p_n,p)\)}}&nbsp;{{c4::\(&lt;\)}}&nbsp;{{c3::\(\epsilon\)}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    <div><br></div><div><ul><li>{{c2::\(p_n\)}}&nbsp;{{c4::\(\to\)}}&nbsp;{{c1::\(p\)}}<br></li><li>{{c3::\(\lim_{n \to \infty}\)}}&nbsp;{{c2::\(p_n\)}}&nbsp;{{c4::\(=\)}}&nbsp;{{c1::\(p\)}}</li><li>{{c2::\(p_n\)}} converges to {{c1::p}}<br></li><li>{{c1::\(p\)}} is the {{c5::limit}} of&nbsp;{{c2::\(p_n\)}}<br></li></ul></div>

============================================================

  

    <div><div>What does&nbsp;\(p_n = \frac{n+1}{n}\) converge to in R?</div></div><div><ul><li><br></li><li>\(\forall\)&nbsp;\( \epsilon \)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(0\)<br></li><li>\(| \frac{n+1}{n} - 1|\)&nbsp;\(&lt;\)&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)<br></li><li>\(\forall\)&nbsp;\(n\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\) \(N\)<br></li><li>Because we can pick</li><li>\(N\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;{{c1::\(\frac{1}{\epsilon}\)}}<br></li><li>For&nbsp; example&nbsp;</li><li>\(N\)&nbsp;\(=\)&nbsp;{{c2::\(\lceil \frac{1}{\epsilon} \rceil \)}}&nbsp; + {{c3::\(1\)}}</li><li>If&nbsp;\(n\)&nbsp;\(\geq\)&nbsp;\(N\) then&nbsp;\(n\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\){{c1::&nbsp;\(\frac{1}{\epsilon}\)}} hence&nbsp;</li><li>{{c4::\( |\frac{1}{n}| \)}}{{c5::&nbsp;\(&lt;\)}}&nbsp;{{c1::\(\frac{1}{\epsilon}\)}}<br></li></ul></div>

============================================================

  

    <div>Why a sequence of a metric space&nbsp;\(X,d\) must have a unique limit</div><div><ul><li>Pictographically: {{c1::<img src="paste-f588701d705dd4d9983372cdecf8b04e18d959f4.jpg">}}</li><li>Assume&nbsp;\(p_n\) converges to&nbsp;\(p\) and&nbsp;\(q\)</li><li>Let&nbsp;\(\epsilon\) =&nbsp;{{c2::\(d(p,q)\)}}</li><li>\(\exists\){{c3::&nbsp;\(N_p\)}} such that&nbsp;\(n\) &gt;&nbsp;{{c3::\(N_p\)}} implies&nbsp;{{c4::\(d(p_n,p)\)}}&nbsp;\(&lt;\){{c5::&nbsp;\(\frac{\epsilon}{2}\)}}<br></li><li>Also</li><li>\(\exists\)&nbsp;{{c4::\(N_q\)}} such that&nbsp;\(n\) &gt;&nbsp;{{c4::\(N_q\)}} implies&nbsp;{{c3::\(d(p_n,q)\)}}&nbsp;\(&lt;\)&nbsp;{{c5::\(\frac{\epsilon}{2}\)}}</li><li>Finally,</li><li>Let&nbsp;\(N\) =&nbsp;\(\max\) ( {{c4::\(N_p, N_q\)}} )</li><li>Then, \(\forall\)&nbsp;\(n\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(N\)&nbsp;<br></li><ul><li>\(\epsilon\) = \(d(p,q)\)&nbsp;\(&lt;\) {{c4::\(d(p,p_n)\)}} + {{c3::\(d(p_n,q)\)}}&nbsp;\(&lt;\) {{c5::\( \frac{\epsilon}{2}\)}} 7 {{c5::\( \frac{\epsilon}{2}\)}} =&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)<br></li></ul><li>This proof could also have been done by showing that the distance between p,q must always be less than every epsilon, thus 0</li></ul></div>

============================================================

  

    A convergent sequence must be bounded because:<br><ul><li>&nbsp;For&nbsp;\(\epsilon\) =&nbsp;{{c1::\(1\)}}</li><li>\(\exists\)&nbsp;\(N\) sucht that&nbsp;\(d\)&nbsp;\((p_n,p)\)&nbsp;\(&lt;\)&nbsp;{{c1::\(1\)}}<br></li><li>Let&nbsp;\(R\) ={{c2::&nbsp;\(\max\)&nbsp;}}{{c3::\((*d(p_n,p)\forall n\))}}</li><li>So {{c5::all&nbsp;\(p_n\)}} are in&nbsp;{{c4::\(\mathcal{N}_R(p)\)}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    &nbsp;If \(p\) is a {{c4::limit}} point of a set \(E\) \(\subset\) \(X\), there exists a sequence \(p_n\) where {{c2::not all}} \(p_n\) {{c5::{{c3::= \(p\)}}}} such that \(p_n\) {{c5::{{c1::\(\to\) \(p\)}}}}<br>Proof:<br><ul><li>Choose a seuqnece&nbsp;\(p_n\)&nbsp;\(\in\)&nbsp;{{c5::{{c1::\(\mathcal{N}_{\frac{1}{n} }(p)\),}}}} then&nbsp;\(p_n\) {{c2::converges to&nbsp;\(p\)}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    If \(p_n\){{c3::\(\to\)}} {{c4::\(p\)}} then {{c5::every}} neighbourhood of p contains all but {{c2::finitely many}} \(p_n\)&nbsp;<br>pictographically:{{c1::<img src="paste-ed0961b3286e63b7c4b89651b4ce996fed9d24e2.jpg">}}

============================================================

  

    Suppose we have two sequences&nbsp;\(s_n, t_n \in C\) if&nbsp;\(s_n \to s\) and \(t_n \to t\) then&nbsp;\((s_n+t_n)\) \(\to\) \((s+t)\)<br>Proof:<br><ul><li>\(\forall \epsilon &gt; 0\),&nbsp;\(\exists\)&nbsp;\(N_s\) such that {{c5::&nbsp;\( |s_n, s|\)}} \(&lt;\) {{c2::\(\frac{\epsilon}{2}\)}}, analagous for&nbsp;\(t_n\)<br></li><li>Pick&nbsp;\(N\) =&nbsp;{{c1::\(\max\)}}&nbsp;{{c2::\((N_s, N_t)\)}}</li><li>Then</li><li>\(| s_n+t_n - s - t |\)<br></li><li>\(\leq\)&nbsp;{{c3::\(|s_n -s|\)}} {{c4::+}}&nbsp;{{c3::\(|t_n - t|\)}}<br></li><li>\(&lt;\){{c4::&nbsp;\(\epsilon \)}}<br></li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Suppose we have a sequence \(s_n\in C\) if&nbsp;\(s_n \to s\) then&nbsp;\(c\)\(s_n\)&nbsp;\(\to\)&nbsp;\(c\)\(s\)<br><ul><li>For all&nbsp;\(\epsilon &gt;0\),&nbsp;\(\exists\)&nbsp;\(N\) such that&nbsp;\(\forall n &gt; N\),&nbsp; \(|\)&nbsp;\(s_n - s\) \(|\)&nbsp;\(&lt;\)&nbsp;{{c1::\(\frac{\epsilon}{|c|}\)}}</li><li>Then</li><li>&nbsp;\(|\)&nbsp;\(c s_n - c s\) \(|\)<br></li><li>= \(|\)&nbsp;\(c (s_n - s)\) \(|\)</li><li>=&nbsp;{{c2::&nbsp;\(|c|\)}}{{c5::{{c3::\(|\)&nbsp;\(s_n - s\) \(|\)}}}}</li><li>\(&lt;\)&nbsp;{{c2::\(|c|\)}}{{c1::\(\frac{\epsilon}{|c|}\)}}<br></li><li>\(&lt;\)&nbsp;{{c5::{{c4::\(\epsilon\)}}}}<br></li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Suppose we have twos sequencees \(s_n, t_n \in C\) if&nbsp;\(s_n \to s\) and \(t_n \to t\), then&nbsp;\((s_n t_n) \to (st)\)<br><ul><li>Both sequences must have a maximum, pick &nbsp;\(|M|\) =&nbsp;{{c3::\(\max\)}} {{c2::\((|M_s|,|M_t|)\)}}</li><li>\(\forall\)&nbsp;\(\epsilon &gt;0 \),&nbsp;\(\exists\)&nbsp;\(N_s\) such that&nbsp;\(|s_n -s| \)\(&lt;\)&nbsp;{{c1::\(\frac{\epsilon}{|M|}\)}}<br></li><li>Analagous for&nbsp;\(t_n\), pick&nbsp;\(N\) =&nbsp;{{c3::\(\max\)}} {{c4::\((N_s,N_t)\)}}</li><li>\(| s_n t_n - st| \)<br></li><li>= {{c5::\(| s_n t_n + s_n t - s_n t - st| \)}}</li><li>= \(| s_n (t_n - t)&nbsp; + t(s_n - s)| \)<br></li><li>\(\leq\)&nbsp;\(| s_n (t_n - t) |\) +&nbsp;\(|t(s_n - s)|\)<br></li><li>\(\leq\) \(| M (t_n - t) |\) +&nbsp;\(| M(s_n - s)|\)<br></li><li>\(\leq \)&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)<br></li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Suppose we have twos sequencees \(s_n, t_n \in C\) if&nbsp;\(s_n \to s\) and \(t_n \to t\), then&nbsp;\((s_n t_n) \to (st)\)<br><ul><li>Let&nbsp;\(K\) =&nbsp;\(\max\) {{c1::&nbsp;\((s,t,1)\)}}, the 1 is necesasry for later expansion</li><li>\(\forall \epsilon &gt; 0\),\(\exists\)&nbsp;\(N_1, N_2\) such that&nbsp;\(n\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(N-1\) implies that&nbsp;\(s_n - s\)&nbsp;\(&lt;\)&nbsp;{{c2::\(\frac{\epsilon}{3K}\)}}, same for&nbsp;\(t_n\) and&nbsp;\(N_2\)<br></li><li>Pick&nbsp;\(N\) =&nbsp;\(\max\)&nbsp;\((N_1,N_2)\)</li><li>\(| s_n t_n - st|\)&nbsp;</li><li>\(\leq\)&nbsp;\(|\){{c3::\((s_n-s)\)}} {{c4::\((t_n-t)\)}} + {{c5::\(s(t_n-t)\)}} +&nbsp;\(t(s_n-s)\)&nbsp;\(|\)<br></li><li>\(&lt;\)&nbsp;\(\frac{\epsilon^2}{9K^2}\) + {{c2::\(\frac{\epsilon}{3K}\)}} + {{c2::\(\frac{\epsilon}{3K}\)}}<br></li><li>\(&lt;\)&nbsp;&nbsp;\(\frac{\epsilon}{9K}\) + {{c2::\(\frac{\epsilon}{3K}\)}} + {{c2::\(\frac{\epsilon}{3K}\)}}<br></li></ul><div><br></div>

============================================================

  

    Suppose&nbsp;\(\{p_n\}\) is a sequence, let&nbsp;\(n_1 \){{c1::&nbsp;\(&lt;\)&nbsp;}}\(n_2\)&nbsp;{{c1::\(&lt;\)}}&nbsp;\(n_3\)&nbsp;\(\cdots\) in&nbsp;{{c4::\(N\)}} be an {{c1::increasing}} {{c5::{{c2::sequence}}}}, then&nbsp;{{c5::{{c3::\(\{p_{n_i}\}\)}}}} is a {{c1::subsequence}}

============================================================

  

    If a sequence&nbsp;\(p_n\) converges to&nbsp;\(p\) all of its subsequences also converge to \(p\), this is because every {{c1::neighbourhood}} of&nbsp; \(p\) contains {{c4::all but}} {{c2::finitely}} {{c3::many points.}}<br><br>In fact, the same {{c5::constant&nbsp;\(N\)}} will suffice for the subsequence, and an even lower one may also work&nbsp;

============================================================

  

    A sequence which does not {{c5::converge}} may have a {{c5::convergent}} subsequence.<br><br>For example:<br><ul><li>\(1,\pi, \frac{1}{2}, \pi, \frac{1}{4}, \cdots\)<br></li><li>Does {{c3::not converge}} but has {{c4::at least two}} {{c5::convergent}} subsequences</li><li>{{c1::\(\pi \cdots\)}}<br></li><li>And</li><li>{{c2::\(\frac{1}{n}\)}}<br></li></ul>

============================================================

  

    <div>{{c5::{{c2::Limits}}}} of {{c4::{{c3::subsequences}}}} are called {{c4::{{c1::subsequantial}}}} {{c5::{{c2::limits}}}}</div>

============================================================

  

    {{c1::Not}} {{c2::every}} sequence has a {{c5::{{c3::convergent}}}} {{c5::{{c4::subsequence}}}}

============================================================

  

    Every {{c4::bounded}} sequence {{c2::does not necessarily have}} a {{c5::{{c3::convergent subsequence}}}} on {{c5::{{c1::arbitrary metric spaces}}}}

============================================================

  

    For arbitrary metric spaces, not {{c4::every}} {{c5::bounded}} sequence needs to have {{c3::a convergent subsequence:}}<br><ul><li>Example, a sequence approximating&nbsp;\(\pi\) in Q, which {{c1::does not converge}} and {{c2::does not have}} {{c3::any convergent subsequence}}</li><li><br></li></ul>

============================================================

  

    <ul><li>A metric space&nbsp;\((X,d)\) is {{c1::sequentially}} {{c2::compact}} iff {{c5::every}} sequence has a {{c3::convergent subsequence}} with {{c4::limit in \(X\)}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Every {{c3::compact}} set is {{c4::also}} {{c5::{{c1::sequentially}}}} {{c5::{{c2::compact}}}}

============================================================

  

    {{c5::{{c1::Compactness}}}} implies {{c4::{{c2::sequential}}}} {{c4::{{c3::compactness}}}}

============================================================

  

    In a {{c1::compact}} metric space every sequence has a {{c3::convergent subsequence}} with a {{c5::limit}} {{c4::in X}}, which means that if&nbsp;\(X\) is {{c1::compact}} then&nbsp;\(X\) is {{c2::sequentially}} {{c2::compact}}

============================================================

  

    Converging to a point inside a metric space is crucial for understanding {{c5::sequential compactness}}, this is why a series {{c3::approaching&nbsp;\(\pi\)}} in&nbsp;{{c4::\(Q\)}} {{c1::does not converge to&nbsp;\(\pi\)}} since it is {{c2::not in&nbsp;\(Q\)}}

============================================================

  

    Every {{c3::bounded}} sequence in&nbsp;\(R^K\) contains a {{c4::convergent}} {{c5::subsequence}} <br>Proof:<br><ul><li>Despite&nbsp;\(R^K\) not being {{c1::compact}}, since the {{c3::bounded}} sequence lives in a {{c1::compact}} {{c2::subset of \(R^K\)}} and thus a compact subset of the metric space meaning it has a {{c4::convergent}} {{c5::subsequence}} in the compact subset and thus in \(R^K\)</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Proof that compactness implies sequential compactness:<br><ul><li>Pictographically:<img src="paste-47bcc60c56ec526312ca047aa23385809be56662.jpg"></li><li>Consider the \(\mathrm{range}\) of&nbsp;\(\{p_n\}\):<br>&nbsp;</li><ul><li>if this is {{c1::finite}} then the sequence must have a {{c1::convergent subsequence}}. Since if you only have {{c2::finitely many}} things and an {{c3::infinite}} sequence {{c4::at least one}} of the points&nbsp;&nbsp;\(p\) must be hit {{c3::infinitely}} many times. this can be used as the subsequence by&nbsp; the {{c5::pigeonhole}} principle.</li><li>If the range is {{c1::infinite}},since X is compact the range has a limit point p (by previous theorem). Then p must be a&nbsp; limit of the sequence of neighbourhoods of radius \(\frac{1}{n}\), \(\mathcal{N}_{\frac{1}{n} }\). Pick increasing indicies in the original sequence that belong in increasingly smaller neighobourhoods</li></ul></ul>

============================================================

  

    {{c1::Cauchy}} {{c5::sequences}} are an attempt to answer the question: "How can you tell if a {{c5::sequence}} {{c4::converges}} if you {{c2::do not know}} its {{c3::limit}}?"

============================================================

  

    If a sequence does {{c4::converge}}, points in the sequence must be getting {{c1::closer}} to {{c5::{{c2::each other}}}}, in fact {{c5::{{c3::arbitrarily}}}} {{c1::close}}

============================================================

  

    <ul><li>A sequence&nbsp;\(\{p_n\}\) is Cacuchy in an abstract metric space&nbsp;\(X,d\) iff&nbsp;\(\forall\){{c3::&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)}}&nbsp;{{c4::\(&gt;\)}}&nbsp;\(0\),&nbsp;{{c5::\(\exists\)}}&nbsp;\(N\) such that&nbsp;\(\forall\)&nbsp;\(m,n\){{c4::&nbsp;\(&gt;\)}}&nbsp;\(N\),&nbsp;{{c1::\(d\)}} {{c2::\((p_m,p_n)\)}}&nbsp;{{c4::\(&lt;\)}}&nbsp;{{c3::\(\epsilon\)}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    <ul><li>A sequence&nbsp;\(\{p_n\}\) is Cacuchy in an abstract metric space&nbsp;\(X,d\) iff&nbsp;\(\forall\){{c3::&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)}}&nbsp;{{c4::\(&gt;\)}}&nbsp;\(0\),&nbsp;{{c5::\(\exists\)}}&nbsp;\(N\) such that&nbsp;\(\forall\)&nbsp;\(m,n\){{c4::&nbsp;\(&gt;\)}}&nbsp;\(N\),&nbsp;{{c1::\(d\)}} {{c2::\((p_m,p_n)\)}}&nbsp;{{c4::\(&lt;\)}}&nbsp;{{c3::\(\epsilon\)}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    <div><ul><li>If&nbsp;\(\{p_n\}\) converges then&nbsp;\(\{p_n\}\) is also Cauchy</li></ul><div>Proof Idea:</div></div><div><ul><li>{{c1::Bound}}&nbsp;\(d\){{c5::\((p_n,p_m)\)}} based on the distances&nbsp;\(d\){{c2::\((p_n,p)\)}} and \(d\){{c3::\((p_m,p)\)}} using the {{c4::triangle inequality}}</li></ul></div>

============================================================

  

    <div><div><br></div><div>Proof that convegent sequences are Cauchy:</div></div><div><ul><li>Given&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(0\),&nbsp;\(\exists\)&nbsp;\(N\) such that&nbsp;\(d(p_n, p) \)&nbsp;\(&lt;\)&nbsp;{{c1::\(\frac{\epsilon}{2}\)}} for all&nbsp;\(n\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(N\)</li><li>For&nbsp;\(m,n\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(N\):</li><ul><li>\(d\){{c2::\((p_n,p_m)\)&nbsp;}}</li><li>\(\leq\) \(d\){{c3::\((p_n,p)\)}} + \(d\){{c4::\((p_m,p)\)}} by {{c5::triangle inequality}}<br></li><li>\(&lt;\)&nbsp;{{c1::\(\frac{\epsilon}{2}\)}} +&nbsp;{{c1::\(\frac{\epsilon}{2}\)}}<br></li><li>\(&lt;\)&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)<br></li></ul></ul></div>

============================================================

  

    <div><div>{{c1::Cauchy}} sequences are not always {{c2::convergent}} on all metric spaces, for example a series approximating&nbsp;\(\pi\) in&nbsp;{{c5::\(Q\)}} is {{c1::Cauchy}} but not {{c2::convergent}} because while the numbers get {{c4::close to each other}} the limit cannot be&nbsp;\(\pi\) since&nbsp;\(\pi\)&nbsp;{{c3::\(\not \in\)}} {{c5::\(Q\)}}</div></div>

============================================================

  

    A {{c5::metric space}}&nbsp;\(X,d\) is {{c1::complete}} iff every {{c2::Cauchy}} sequence {{c4::converges}} to {{c4::a point}} {{c3::in&nbsp;\(X\)}}

============================================================

  

    In a {{c5::{{c3::complete}}}} metric space, {{c4::{{c1::Cauchy}}}} means {{c4::{{c2::convergent}}}}

============================================================

  

    {{c3::\(Q\)}} is {{c2::not}} a {{c5::{{c1::complete}}}} {{c5::{{c4::metric space&nbsp;}}}}

============================================================

  

    {{c3::Compact}} metric spaces are {{c1::complete}}, meaning that every {{c2::Cauchy}} sequence {{c4::converges to}} {{c5::a limit in the space}}

============================================================

  

    {{c1::Finite}} metric spaces are by definition {{c2::complete}}, since every Cauchy sequence must {{c4::eventually}} contain {{c3::exactly one value}} for all&nbsp;\(n\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(N\) once&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)&nbsp;\(&lt;\)&nbsp;{{c5::\(\min\)}} \((d(p_n,p_m))\)

============================================================

  

    Proof the compact metric spaces are complete:<br><ul><li>Let&nbsp;\(\{x_n\}\) be a Cauchy sequence in the space&nbsp;\(X\)</li><li>&nbsp;Since&nbsp;\(X\) is compact it is also sequentially compact, meaning that every sequence has {{c2::a convergent subsequence&nbsp;}}</li><li>As such there exists \(x_{n_k}\)&nbsp;\(\to\)&nbsp;\(x\)&nbsp;\(\in\)&nbsp;\(X\)</li><li>Pictographically: <img src="paste-22571f69de45ed76447262771338cf6e10c6c260.jpg"></li><li>Fix&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(0\)</li><li>\(x_n\) being Cauchy implies&nbsp;\(\exists\)&nbsp;\(N_1\) such that&nbsp;\(\forall i,j\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(N\) then&nbsp;\(d\)\((x_i,x_j)\)&nbsp;\(&lt;\)&nbsp;{{c5::\(\frac{\epsilon}{2}\)}}<br></li><li>\(\{x_{n_k}\}\)&nbsp;\(\to\)&nbsp;\(x\) implies that&nbsp;\(\forall\)&nbsp;\(n_k\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(N_2\),&nbsp;\(d\)&nbsp;\((x_{n_k},x)\)&nbsp;\(&lt;\)&nbsp;{{c5::\(\frac{\epsilon}{2}\)}}<br></li><li>Let \(N\) =&nbsp;\(\max\)&nbsp;\((N_1,N_2)\)</li><li>\(\forall\)&nbsp;\(n\)&nbsp;\(&gt;N\):<br></li><ul><li>\(d\)\((x_n,x)\)&nbsp;</li><li>\(\leq\)&nbsp;{{c2::\(d\)&nbsp;\((x_n,x_{n_k})\)}} +&nbsp;{{c3::&nbsp;\(d\)&nbsp;\((x_{n_k},x)\)}} for {{c1::any&nbsp;\(n_k\)}}&nbsp;{{c4::\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(N\)}} , {{c4::fix}} one</li><li>\(&lt;\)&nbsp;{{c5::\(\frac{\epsilon}{2}\)}} <span style="font-size: 16.6667px;">+</span>{{c5::<span style="font-size: 16.6667px;">&nbsp;</span>\(\frac{\epsilon}{2}\)}}</li><li>\(&lt;\)&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)<br></li></ul><li>Since&nbsp;\(\epsilon\) was arbitrary, \(X\) is complete</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    [0,1] is a {{c1::complete}} metric space since it is {{c3::compact}}, similarly {{c5::{{c2::k-cells}}}} in&nbsp;{{c5::{{c4::\(R^n\)}}}} are {{c1::complete}}

============================================================

  

    Since {{c5::closed}} subsets of {{c4::compact}} metric spaces are {{c1::also compact}}, they are in fact {{c2::complete}}. This surprisingly implies that the {{c3::cantor}} set is {{c2::complete}}

============================================================

  

    \(R^n\) is complete proof:<br><ul><li>If&nbsp;\(x_n\) is Cauchy it is also {{c4::bounded}} because</li><ul><li>&nbsp;for&nbsp;\(\epsilon\) =&nbsp;\(1\) there exists&nbsp;\(N\) such that&nbsp;\(d\)&nbsp;\((x_n,x_m)\)&nbsp;&nbsp;\(&lt;\)&nbsp;\(1\)&nbsp;\(\forall n,m &gt; N\).&nbsp;</li><li>Let&nbsp;\(R\) =&nbsp;\(\max\)&nbsp;\((d(x_n,x_m),1)\)&nbsp;\(\forall\)&nbsp;\(n,m\)</li><li>Then the sequence is {{c4::bounded}} by {{c1::&nbsp;\(N_{R}(x_n)\)}}</li></ul><li>Due to {{c4::boundedness}}, {{c1::\(N_{R}(x_n)\)}} is inside some {{c2::k-cell}} in&nbsp;\(R^n\), thus {{c1::the ball}} is {{c3::complete}}</li><li>Thus&nbsp;\(x_n\) {{c5::converges}} because the {{c1::k-cell}} is {{c3::complete}}</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Proof that the harmonic series&nbsp;\(x_n\)&nbsp;\(=\)&nbsp;\(\sum_{k=1}^n\)&nbsp;\(\frac{1}{k}\) does not converge using cauchyness:<br><ul><li>Consider, for&nbsp;\(n&gt;m\), \(| x_n - x_m|\)&nbsp;</li><li>=&nbsp;{{c2::\(\sum_{k=n+1}^m\)}}{{c1::\(\frac{1}{k}\)&nbsp;}}</li><li>\(&gt;\) {{c2::\(\sum_{k=n+1}^m\)}}{{c3::\(\frac{1}{n}\)&nbsp;}}</li><li>\(=\)&nbsp;{{c4::\(\frac{n-m}{n}\)}}</li><li>\(=\)&nbsp;{{c5::\(1 - \frac{n}{m}\)}}<br></li><li>Let&nbsp;\(n\) =&nbsp;\(2m\), then&nbsp;\(|x_{2n} - x_{m}|\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(\frac{1}{2}\)</li><li>Thus, considering&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)&nbsp;\(&lt;\)&nbsp;\(\frac{1}{2}\) the sequence can never be cauchy so it does not converge</li><li>This show that we do not need to propose a limit to determine if a sequence converges in a complete metric space</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    You can easily show {{c2::recursive}} sequences to {{c3::converge or not}} using {{c1::cauchyness}} {{c5::without}} {{c4::knowing the limit}}

============================================================

  

    Theorem: Every metric space&nbsp;\((X,d)\) has a {{c1::completion}}, denoted ( {{c2::\(X^*\)}},{{c3::\(d^*\)}} ) such that&nbsp;{{c5::\(X\)}}&nbsp;{{c4::\(\subset\)}}{{c2::\(X^*\)}} and&nbsp;{{c3::\(d^*_{|X}\)}} ={{c4::&nbsp;\(d\)}}

============================================================

  

    An {{c4::abstract metric space}} {{c4::(X,d)::notation}} is {{c3::complete}} if every {{c1::Cauchy}} seq {{c5::converges}} to a point {{c2::in X}}

============================================================

  

    {{c1::Compact}} {{c4::metric spaces}} are {{c5::{{c2::always}}}} {{c5::{{c3::complete}}}}

============================================================

  

    How does convergenece work over finite metric spaces?<br><br><ol><li>Since the list is finite</li><li>As soon as&nbsp;{{c2::\(\epsilon\)}}&nbsp;{{c3::\(\leq\)}}&nbsp;{{c1::\(\min_{x,y \in X\times X} d(x,y)\)}} then {{c5::all points of the sequences}} must become {{c4::equal to the limit}}</li></ol>

============================================================

  

    <ol><li>To show a compact metric space is complete we take an arbitray {{c1::cauchy}} sequence&nbsp;\(\{x_n\}\) in&nbsp;\(X\)</li><li>Since&nbsp;\(X\) is compact, it is {{c2::sequentially}} compact</li><li>Meaning that&nbsp;\(\exists\)&nbsp;&nbsp;{{c2::\(\{x_{n_k}\}\)}}&nbsp;\(\rightarrow\)&nbsp;\(x\), which is a convergent {{c2::subsequence}}<br></li><li>Since \(\{x_n\}\) is {{c1::cauchy}},&nbsp;\(\forall\)&nbsp;\(\epsilon \)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(0\), there exists&nbsp;\(N_1\) such that&nbsp;{{c1::\(d(x_n,x_m)\)}} \(&lt;\) {{c3::\(\frac{\epsilon}{2}\)}}&nbsp;\(\forall\)&nbsp;{{c3::\(m,n &gt; N_1\),&nbsp;}}</li><li>Similarly, for the convergent {{c2::subsequence}} \(d(x_{n_k},x)\) \(&lt;\) {{c3::\(\frac{\epsilon}{2}\)}}&nbsp;\(\forall\){{c3::&nbsp;\(n &gt; N_2\),&nbsp;}}</li><li>Take N=&nbsp;\(\max(N_1,N_2)\)</li><li>For all&nbsp;\(n\)&nbsp;\(\geq\)&nbsp;\(N\) in the {{c2::original}} sequence, then&nbsp;\(d(x_n,x)\)&nbsp;\(\leq\)&nbsp;{{c4::\(d(x_n, x_{n_j})\)}} {{c5::+}} {{c4::\(d(x_{n_j},x)\)}}&nbsp;\(\leq\) {{c5::\(\frac{2 \epsilon}{2}\)}} for&nbsp; all/some \(n_{j}\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(N\)</li><li>Since \(\{x_n\}\) was arbitrary this implies (x,d) is complete</li></ol><br>

============================================================

  

    A {{c1::closed}} subset of a {{c2::compact}} set must be {{c3::complete}} since {{c1::closed}} subsets of {{c2::compact}} sets are in fact {{c4::compact}}, e.g, perhaps surprisingly the {{c5::cantor}} set is {{c3::complete}}

============================================================

  

    Why is&nbsp;\(R^n\) complete? Proof:<br><ul><li>If&nbsp;\(\{x_n\}\) is Cauchy it is {{c1::bounded}},</li><li>\(\forall \epsilon\),&nbsp;\(\exists\)&nbsp;\(N\) such that&nbsp;&nbsp;\(d({x_n}, x_{m})\)&nbsp;\(&lt;\)&nbsp;\(\epsilon\) for all&nbsp;\(m,n\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(N\)<br></li><li>Let R =&nbsp;{{c2::\(\max_{i \in \{1,\cdots, N-1\} }\)}}&nbsp;{{c3::\(d(x_N, x_i)\)}}, a {{c4::ball}} centered at any point in the sequence with this radius {{c1::bounds}} \(\{x_n\}\)</li><li>Since {{c1::bounded}} sequence contain convergent subsequences and in R this implies that they are convergent, this ball is complete</li><li>Since we can {{c5::always construct such a bal}},&nbsp; being cauchy always implies it is complete which implies that&nbsp;\(R^n\) is complete</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    In&nbsp;\(R^n\), {{c5::{{c1::cauchy}}}} {{c4::{{c3::sequences}}}} are {{c4::{{c2::convergent}}}} {{c3::sequences}}

============================================================

  

    Proof that the harmonic series does not converge using cauchy sequences<br><ol><li>\(h_n\) =&nbsp;\(\sum_{i=1}^{n} \frac{1}{n}\)<br></li><li>If it were to be cauchy, then:</li><ol><li>\(\forall\)&nbsp;\(\epsilon\),&nbsp;\(\exists\)&nbsp;\(N\) such that&nbsp;\(\forall\)&nbsp;\(n\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(N\)<br></li><li>\(|h_n - h_m|\)&nbsp;\(&lt;\)&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)<br></li><li>Wlog, assume&nbsp;{{c1::\(n\)&nbsp;\(&gt;m\)}} and compute the diff</li><li>{{c3::\(\sum_{k=m+1}^{n}\)}} {{c2::\(\frac{1}{k} \)}}&nbsp;\(&lt;\)&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)</li><li>However</li><li>&nbsp;{{c3::\(\sum_{k=m+1}^{n}\)}} {{c2::\(\frac{1}{k} \)}}&nbsp;&nbsp;{{c3::\(\geq\)}} {{c4::\(\sum_{k=m+1}^{n} \frac{1}{n} \)}} =&nbsp;{{c5::\(\frac{n-m}{m}\)}} =&nbsp;{{c5::\(1 - \frac{m}{n}\)}}<br></li><li>Meaning that by increasing the distance between terms we can obtain an arbitrarily large bound regardless of how deep in the sequence we are</li><li>Thus the sequence is not cauchy</li></ol></ol>

============================================================

  

    Verify convergence via cauchyness for&nbsp;<br><ul><li>\(x_1\) = 1<br></li><li>\(x_2\) = 2<br></li><li>\(x_n\) =&nbsp;\(\frac{1}{2}\)(\(x_{n-2} + x_{n-1}\))<br></li></ul><div>Proof:&nbsp;</div><div><ul><li>\(|x_{n} - x_{m}|\)&nbsp;\(&lt;\)&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)<br></li><li>Assume&nbsp;\(n\)&nbsp;\(\geq\)&nbsp;\(m\)&nbsp;</li><li>One can prove that&nbsp;\(x_n\) ={{c1::\(&nbsp; \frac{1}{2}^{n-2}\)}} \(\times\) {{c2::\(\mathrm{fib}_{3,7}(n-2) \)}}</li><li>Where&nbsp;{{c2::\(\mathrm{fib}_{3,7}\)}} is a {{c3::fibonnaci}}-like sequence with {{c3::first two terms}} {{c2::3,7}}</li><li>\(|\) {{c4::\(\frac{1}{2}^{n-2}\)}} {{c3::\(\mathrm{fib}_{3,7}(n-2)\)}} - {{c4::\(\frac{1}{2}^{m-2} \)}} {{c3::\(\mathrm{fib}_{3,7}(m-2)\)}} \(|\)<br></li><li>=&nbsp;{{c5::\(|\frac{1}{2}^{m-2}\times( \frac{1}{2}^{n-m} \mathrm{fib}_{3,7}(n-2) - \mathrm{fib}_{3,7} (m-2))|\)}}</li><li>Properly solving this requires expressing&nbsp;\(\mathrm{fib_{3,7}}(n-2)\) in a closed-form solution based on the {{c4::eigenvalues}} of the matrix&nbsp;</li><li>\( A\)&nbsp; = {{c5::\([[1,1],[1,0]]\)}} since&nbsp;{{c2::\(\mathrm{fib}_{3,7}(n)\)}} = \(A^n [7,3]^T\), where the eigenvalues and the eigenvectors are the same as those of fib, the only new thing is expressing&nbsp;\([7,3]^T\) in terms of the {{c4::eigenvectors}}</li><li>Regardless of this, the closed form must grow exponentially in the {{c5::golden ratio}} {{c2::&nbsp;\(\mathrm{fib}_{3,7}(n)\)}} \(\in\) {{c5::\(\mathrm{O}(\phi^n)\)}}&nbsp;</li><li>Thus deltas get increasingly small since they decay exponentially with base&nbsp;\(2\)</li></ul></div>

============================================================

  

    Every metric space&nbsp;\((X,d)\), has a {{c1::completion}}&nbsp;\((\) {{c2::\(X^*, d^*\)}}\()\) such that&nbsp;{{c2::\(d^*(x,y)\)}} = {{c3::\(d(x,y)\)}}&nbsp;\(\forall\)&nbsp;{{c3::\(x,y\)}} \(\in\) {{c4::\(X\)}}, i.e it {{c3::equals&nbsp;\(d\)}} when {{c5::restricted to&nbsp;\(X\)}}

============================================================

  

    Proof that every metric space has a completion:<br><ol><li>Given&nbsp;\(X\), let&nbsp;\(X^*\) be the set of all {{c1::cauchy sequences}} in&nbsp;\(X\) under an {{c2::equivalence relation&nbsp;\(\approx\)}} where&nbsp;{{c1::\(\{p_n\}\)}}&nbsp;{{c2::\(\approx\)}}&nbsp;{{c1::\(\{q_n\}\)}} iff&nbsp;{{c3::\(\lim_{n \infty}\)}} {{c5::\(d(p_n,q_n)\)}} =&nbsp;{{c4::\(0\)}}</li><li>Denote arbitray equivalence classes \(E\) and \(R\) with&nbsp;\(E,R\)&nbsp;\(\in\)&nbsp;\(X^*\)</li><li>For example, we can pick&nbsp;\(\{e_n\}\) and&nbsp;\(\{r_n\}\) as representatives of the equivalence classes&nbsp;\(E,R\)</li><li>Define a distance between them:&nbsp;\(\Delta(E,R)\) =&nbsp;\(\lim_{n \to \infty}\)&nbsp;\(d(e_n,r_n)\) for two representatives where this limit is meaningful since the metric is a real number and thus we are taking the limit in R which is complete</li><li>The limit of the distances is guaranteed to exist because the sequences are cauchy</li></ol>

============================================================

  

    Proof that every metric space has a completion:<br><ol><li>Given&nbsp;\(X\), let&nbsp;\(X^*\) be the set of all cauchy sequences in&nbsp;\(X\) under an equivalence relation&nbsp;\(\approx\) where&nbsp;\(\{p_n\}\)&nbsp;\(\approx\)&nbsp;\(\{q_n\}\) iff&nbsp;\(\lim_{n \infty}\) \(d(p_n,q_n)\) =&nbsp;\(0\)</li><li>Denote arbitray equivalence classes \(E\) and \(R\) with&nbsp;\(E,R\)&nbsp;\(\in\)&nbsp;\(X^*\)</li><li>For example, we can pick&nbsp;\(\{e_n\}\) and&nbsp;\(\{r_n\}\) as {{c1::representatives}} of the equivalence classes&nbsp;\(E,R\)</li><li>Define a {{c4::distance}} between them:&nbsp;{{c4::\(\Delta(E,R)\)}} =&nbsp;{{c3::\(\lim_{n \to \infty}\)}}&nbsp;{{c2::\(d(e_n,r_n)\)}} for two {{c1::representatives}} where this limit is meaningful since the metric is a {{c5::real}} number and thus we are taking the limit in {{c5::R}} which is {{c5::complete}}</li><li>The {{c3::limit}} of the distances is guaranteed to exist because the sequences are {{c4::cauchy}}</li></ol>

    The {{c3::completion}}&nbsp;{{c4::\(X^*\)}} of a space&nbsp;\((X,d)\) is {{c3::complete}} with&nbsp;\(X\) {{c5::{{c1::isometrically}}}} {{c5::{{c2::embedded}}}} in&nbsp;{{c4::\(X^*\)}}

    What are the two common ways of defining the real numbers?<br><ol><li>{{c1::Dedekind cuts}}</li><li>The {{c2::cauchy-sequence}} {{c5::{{c3::completion}}}} of {{c5::{{c4::Q}}}}</li></ol>

    In-short description of the {{c2::cauchy-sequence}} completion process:<br><ol><li>Consider the completion&nbsp;\(X^*\) of metric space \((X,d)\) to be the set of {{c1::equivalence classes}} over {{c2::cauchy seuqnces}}</li><li>Where two {{c2::sequences}} are in the class iff&nbsp;{{c5::\(\lim_{n \to \infty}\)}} {{c4::\(d(p_n,q_n)\)}} =&nbsp;{{c3::\(0\)}}</li><li>Define the distance on&nbsp;&nbsp;\(X^*\)&nbsp; as&nbsp;\(\Delta(E,R)\) =&nbsp;{{c5::\(\lim_{n \to \infty}\)}} {{c3::\(d(e_n,r_n)\)}} where&nbsp;\(e_n,r_n\) are representatives of the equivalence classes&nbsp;\(E,R\)</li></ol>

============================================================

  

    <ul><li>{{c5::{{c1::Monotonically}}}} {{c4::{{c2::increasing}}}} sequences,&nbsp;\(s_n\)&nbsp;{{c4::{{c2::\(\leq\)}}}}&nbsp;\(s_{n+1}\)</li><li>{{c1::Monotonically}} {{c4::{{c3::decreasing}}}} sequences, \(s_n\)&nbsp;{{c4::{{c3::\(\geq\)}}}}&nbsp;\(s_{n+1}\)</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    <div>{{c3::Bounded}} {{c4::monotonically}} {{c1::increasing}} sequnces {{c5::converge}} to their {{c1::supremum}} while {{c4::monotonically}} {{c2::decresing}} sequences {{c5::converge}} to their {{c2::infimum}}</div>

============================================================

  

    Proof that bounded monotonically increasing sequences in R converge to their supremum:<br><ul><li>Since the sequnce is bounded,&nbsp;\(s_n\)&nbsp;\(\leq\)&nbsp;\(M\)</li><li>The elements of the sequence form a bounded nonempty subset of R</li><li>By the axiom of completeness, a {{c1::supremum}} exists&nbsp;\(s\) =&nbsp;{{c1::\(\sup(\{\mathrm{range}(s_n)\})\)}}</li><li>For {{c3::any&nbsp;\(s_n\)}}, \(\exists\)&nbsp;\(\epsilon^\prime &gt;0\) such that&nbsp;{{c2::\(s - \epsilon^\prime \)}} &lt; {{c3::\(s_n\)}} which implies that we&nbsp;{{c2::\(|s - s_n|\)}}&nbsp;\(&lt;\)&nbsp;{{c3::\(\epsilon^\prime\)}}</li><li>Now, given that&nbsp;\(\epsilon^\prime\) exists, we need to prove convergence for any&nbsp;\(\epsilon\) unconditional on {{c3::picking an&nbsp;\(s_n\)}}</li><li>For any&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(0\), choose&nbsp;\(N\) such that{{c4::&nbsp;\(|s_N - s|\)&nbsp;}}\(&lt;\)&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)</li><li>Since the sequence is {{c5::monotonically increasing}},&nbsp;\(\forall\)&nbsp;\(n \geq N\),&nbsp;{{c5::\(|s_n - s|\)&nbsp;}}\(&lt;\)&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)&nbsp;</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    What does&nbsp;\(s_n \to +\infty\) mean?<br><br><ol><li>\(\forall\)&nbsp;\(M\)&nbsp;\(\in\)&nbsp;\(R\),&nbsp;{{c4::\(\exists\)}}&nbsp;\(N\) such that&nbsp;\(n\)&nbsp;{{c5::\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(N\)}} implies&nbsp;{{c1::\(s_n\)}}&nbsp;{{c2::\(&gt;\)}}&nbsp;{{c3::\(M\)}}</li></ol>

    <ol><li>{{c4::\(\forall\)}}&nbsp;\(M\)&nbsp;\(\in\)&nbsp;\(R\),&nbsp;{{c3::\(\exists\)}}&nbsp;\(N\) such that&nbsp;\(n\)&nbsp;{{c5::\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(N\)}} implies&nbsp;\(s_n\)&nbsp;{{c1::\(&lt;\)}}&nbsp;{{c2::\(M\)}}</li></ol>

    Given a sequence&nbsp;\(s_n\), let&nbsp;\(E\) =&nbsp;{{c1::\(&nbsp; \{ \text{set of all possible subsqeuntial limits} \} \)}}, which may include&nbsp;{{c2::\(\pm \infty\)}}<br><ol><li>let&nbsp;\(s^*\) ={{c3::&nbsp;\(\sup E\)}} and&nbsp;\(s_*\) =&nbsp;{{c4::\(\inf E\)}}, which are also called&nbsp;{{c5::\(\lim_{n \to \infty} \sup s_n\)}} and&nbsp;{{c5::\(\lim_{n \to \infty} \inf s_n\)}}, alternatively {{c5::upper limit}} and {{c5::lower limit}} of {{c1::E}}</li></ol><ul><li>\(\lim_{n \to \infty} \sup s_n\) =&nbsp;\(\lim_{n \to \infty}\)(\(\sup_{k \geq n} s_n\)) which means the&nbsp;\(\sup\) of&nbsp;\(S_n\) = \(\{s_k: k \geq n\}\) whose purpose is to cut off the initial behaviour of the sequence</li><li>\(\lim_{n \to \infty} \inf s_n\) =&nbsp;\(\lim_{n \to \infty}\)(\(\inf_{k \geq n} s_n\)) which means the&nbsp;\(\inf\) of&nbsp;\(S_n\) = \(\{s_k: k \geq n\}\) whose purpose is to cut off the initial behaviour of the sequence</li><li>These are equivalnet to the largest and smallest subsequential limits, alternatively</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Given a sequence&nbsp;\(s_n\), let&nbsp;\(E\) =&nbsp;{{c1::\(&nbsp; \{ \text{set of all possible subsqeuntial limits} \} \)}}, which may include&nbsp;{{c2::\(\pm \infty\)}}<br><ul><li>let&nbsp;\(s^*\) ={{c3::&nbsp;\(\sup E\)}} and&nbsp;\(s_*\) =&nbsp;{{c4::\(\inf E\)}}, which are also called&nbsp;{{c5::\(\lim_{n \to \infty} \sup s_n\)}} and&nbsp;{{c5::\(\lim_{n \to \infty} \inf s_n\)}}, alternatively {{c5::upper limit}} and {{c5::lower limit}} of {{c1::E}}</li><li>\(\lim_{n \to \infty} \sup s_n\) =&nbsp;\(\lim_{n \to \infty}\)(\(\sup_{k \geq n} s_n\)) which means the&nbsp;\(\sup\) of&nbsp;\(S_n\) = \(\{s_k: k \geq n\}\) whose purpose is to cut off the initial behaviour of the sequence</li><li>\(\lim_{n \to \infty} \inf s_n\) =&nbsp;\(\lim_{n \to \infty}\)(\(\inf_{k \geq n} s_n\)) which means the&nbsp;\(\inf\) of&nbsp;\(S_n\) = \(\{s_k: k \geq n\}\) whose purpose is to cut off the initial behaviour of the sequence</li><li>These are equivalnet to the largest and smallest subsequential limits, alternatively</li></ul>

============================================================

  

    Given a sequence&nbsp;\(s_n\), let&nbsp;\(E\) =&nbsp;\(&nbsp; \{ \text{set of all possible subsqeuntial limits} \} \), which may include&nbsp;\(\pm \infty\)<br><ol><li>let&nbsp;\(s^*\) =&nbsp;\(\sup E\) and&nbsp;\(s_*\) =&nbsp;\(\inf E\), which are also called&nbsp;\(\lim_{n \to \infty} \sup s_n\) and&nbsp;\(\lim_{n \to \infty} \inf s_n\), alternatively upper limit and lower limit of E</li></ol><ul><li>\(\lim_{n \to \infty} \sup s_n\) =&nbsp;\(\lim_{n \to \infty}\){{c1::(\(\sup_{k \geq n} s_n\))}} which means the&nbsp;\(\sup\) of&nbsp;\(S_n\) = {{c2::\(\{s_k: k \geq n\}\)}} whose purpose is to {{c5::{{c3::cut off the initial behaviour of the sequence}}}}</li><li>\(\lim_{n \to \infty} \inf s_n\) =&nbsp;\(\lim_{n \to \infty}\){{c1::(\(\inf_{k \geq n} s_n\))}} which means the&nbsp;\(\inf\) of&nbsp;\(S_n\) = {{c2::\(\{s_k: k \geq n\}\)}} whose purpose is to {{c3::cut off the initial behaviour of the sequence}}</li><li>These are equivalnet to the largest and smallest {{c5::{{c4::subsequential limits}}}}, alternatively</li></ul>

    When does a sequence converge on an abstract metric space \(X,d\)?<br><ol><li>A sequence&nbsp;\(\{p_n\}\) with&nbsp;\(p_n\)&nbsp;\(\in \)&nbsp;\(X\)&nbsp;&nbsp;\(\forall\)&nbsp;\(n\)&nbsp;\(\in\)&nbsp;\(N\) is said to converge iff&nbsp;\(\forall\)&nbsp;\(\epsilon\) {{c5::\(&gt;0\)}},&nbsp;\(\exists\)&nbsp;\(N\) such that&nbsp;\(n\)\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(N\) implies&nbsp;{{c1::\(d\)}}{{c2::\((p_n,p)\)}}&nbsp;{{c4::\(&lt;\)}}&nbsp;{{c3::\(\epsilon\)}}</li></ol>

============================================================

  

    When is a sequence&nbsp;\(\{p_n\}\) cauchy?<br><ol><li>\(\forall\)&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)&nbsp;{{c4::\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(0\)}}, there exists&nbsp;\(N\) such that&nbsp;\(n,m\)&nbsp;{{c5::\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(N\)}} implies&nbsp;{{c2::\(d\)}}{{c1::\((p_n,p_m)\)}}&nbsp;{{c3::\(&lt;\)&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)}}</li></ol>
