    <ul><li>{{c1::Convergent}} sequence are {{c2::Cauchy}} in {{c5::{{c3::arbitrary}}}} metric spaces.</li><li>In {{c5::{{c4::complete}}}} metric spaces, {{c2::cauchy}} sequences {{c1::converge}}</li></ul><br>

============================================================

  

    We can use the {{c5::{{c1::cauchy}}}} criterion to determine {{c4::{{c2::covergence}}}} in R because R is {{c4::{{c3::complete}}}}

============================================================

  

    What is a series?<br><ol><li>Given a {{c2::sequence&nbsp;\(\{a_n\}\)}}</li><li>Then the {{c2::n-th partial sum of the sequence}} is {{c5::\(s_n\)}} =&nbsp;{{c1::\(\sum_{k=1}^{n}\)}} {{c2::\(a_k\)}}</li><li>{{c5::\(\{s_n\}\)}} forms an associated sequence of {{c2::partial sums}}<br></li><li>An infinite series is&nbsp;{{c3::\(\sum_{k=1}^{\infty}\)}}&nbsp;{{c2::\(a_k\)}}, which may {{c4::not converge}}</li><li>If&nbsp;{{c5::\(\{s_n\}\)}} converges: {{c3::\(\sum_{k=1}^{\infty}\)}}&nbsp;\(a_k\) =&nbsp;{{c4::\(\lim_{n \to \infty}\)}} {{c1::\(\sum_{k=1}^{n}\)&nbsp;}}\(a_k\) = {{c4::\(\lim_{n \to \infty}\)}}&nbsp;{{c5::\(s_n\)}} =&nbsp;\(s\)</li></ol>

============================================================

  

    What is the cauchy criterion for series?<br><ol><li>A series&nbsp;\(\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} a_n\) converges iff&nbsp;\(\forall\)&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)&nbsp;{{c5::\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(0\)}},&nbsp;\(\exists\)&nbsp;\(N\) such that&nbsp;\(\forall\) {{c5::\(n\) \(&gt;\) \(m\) \(&gt;\) \(N\)}} implies that</li><li>{{c3::\(|\)}} {{c1::\(\sum_{k=m}^{n}\)}} {{c2::\(a_k\)}}&nbsp;{{c3::\(|\)}} {{c4::\(&lt;\) \(\epsilon\)}}, which means that past some point sub-sums of the tail of the series are arbitrarily small</li></ol>

============================================================

  

    <ol><li>By Cauchy criterion,&nbsp;\(\forall\)&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)\(&gt;0\),&nbsp;\(\exists N\) such that&nbsp;\(\forall\)&nbsp;\(n&gt;m&gt;N\),&nbsp; \(|\sum_{k=m}^{n} a_k|\)&nbsp;\(&lt;\)&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)</li><li>Let&nbsp;{{c1::\(n\)}}&nbsp;{{c2::\(=\)}}&nbsp;{{c1::\(m\)}} in the cauchy criterion, then&nbsp;{{c3::\(|a_n|\)}}&nbsp;{{c4::\(&lt;\)&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)}},&nbsp;\(\forall\)&nbsp;{{c5::\(\epsilon \)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(0\)}} which implies&nbsp;\(a_n\)&nbsp;{{c5::\(\to\)&nbsp;\(0\)}}</li></ol>

============================================================

  

    <div>How would choosing an incomplete metric space like Q affect the validity of the cauchy criterion for series convergence?</div><div><ol><li>Despite the series potentially not converging if {{c2::the limit is not in Q}}, the terms may still {{c1::get arbitrarily close to each other}}, meaning the cauchy criterion would {{c5::incorrectly classify}} a {{c4::non-convergent}} series as {{c3::convergent}}</li></ol></div>

============================================================

  

    <ol><li>If&nbsp;\(a_n\)&nbsp;{{c1::\(\geq\)}}&nbsp;{{c1::\(0\)}}, then&nbsp;\(\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} a_n\) converges iff its {{c2::partial sums}} {{c5::{{c3::are bounded}}}}</li><li>Because the {{c2::partial sums}} must {{c5::{{c4::increase monotonically}}}} for {{c1::non-negative}} series</li><li>{{c5::{{c3::Bounded}}}} {{c5::{{c4::monotone}}}} sequences converge</li></ol>

============================================================

  

    What are the two comparison tests for series?<br><ol><li>If&nbsp;{{c4::\(|a_n|\)}}{{c5::&nbsp;\(\leq\)&nbsp;\(c_n\)}},&nbsp;{{c3::\(\forall\)&nbsp;\(n\)&nbsp;\(\geq\)&nbsp;\(N\)-constant::condition for n}}</li><ol><li>And&nbsp;{{c2::\(\sum c_n\)}} {{c1::converges}}, then&nbsp;{{c1::\(\sum a_n\)}} {{c2::converges}}</li></ol><li>If&nbsp;{{c5::\(a_n\)}}&nbsp;{{c4::\(&gt;\)&nbsp;&nbsp;\(d_n\)}}{{c4::&nbsp;\(\geq\)&nbsp;\(0\)}},&nbsp;{{c3::\(\forall\)&nbsp;\(n\)&nbsp;\(\geq\)&nbsp;\(N\)-constant::condition for n}}</li><ol><li>If&nbsp;{{c1::\(\sum d_n\)}} {{c2::diverges}}, then&nbsp;{{c2::\(\sum a_n\)}} {{c1::diverges}}</li></ol></ol>

============================================================

  

    <ol><li>If&nbsp;\(|a_n|\)&nbsp;\(\leq\)&nbsp;\(c_n\),&nbsp;\(\forall\)&nbsp;\(n\)&nbsp;\(\geq\)&nbsp;\(N\)-constant</li><ol><li>And&nbsp;\(\sum c_n\) converges, then&nbsp;\(\sum a_n\) converges</li></ol></ol><div>Proof:</div><div><ol><li>We want to show that the cauchy criterion holds for&nbsp;\(\sum a_n\), thus given&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(0\), we need to find an&nbsp;\(N\) such that for&nbsp;\(n &gt; m &gt; N\): \(|\sum _{k=m}^{n} a_k |\)&nbsp;\(&lt;\)&nbsp;\(\epsilon\).</li><ol><li>&nbsp;\(|\sum _{k=m}^{n} a_k |\)&nbsp;\(\leq\) {{c1::\(\sum _{k=m}^{n} | a_k |\)}}&nbsp;by {{c1::triangle inequality}}</li><li>\(\leq\) {{c2::\(\sum _{k=m}^{n} c_n\)}} by {{c2::assumption}}<br></li><li>{{c4::\(&lt;\)&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)}} for {{c3::some constant&nbsp;\(n&gt;m&gt;N_c\)}} because {{c4::\(\sum c_n\) is convergent}}, we just need to chose&nbsp;\(N\)&nbsp;{{c5::\(\geq\)&nbsp;\(N_c\)}}<br></li></ol></ol></div>

============================================================

  

    <div><ol><li>If&nbsp;\(a_n\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;&nbsp;\(d_n\)&nbsp;\(\geq\)&nbsp;\(0\),&nbsp;\(\forall\)&nbsp;\(n\)&nbsp;\(\geq\)&nbsp;\(N\)-constant</li><ol><li>If&nbsp;\(\sum d_n\) diverges, then&nbsp;\(\sum a_n\) diverges</li></ol></ol><div>Proof:</div></div><div><ol><li>We can prove the contrapositive by showing that&nbsp;{{c3::\(\sum a_n\)}} {{c1::convergent}} implies&nbsp;{{c4::\(\sum\)&nbsp;\(d_n\)}} {{c2::convergent}}, using the {{c5::standard comparison test}}&nbsp;</li></ol></div><div><ol></ol></div>

============================================================

  

    <ol><li>If&nbsp;{{c5::\(|x|\)}}&nbsp;{{c4::\(&lt;\)&nbsp;\(1\)}}, then&nbsp;{{c3::\(\sum_{n=1}^\infty\)}} {{c2::\(x^n\)}} =&nbsp;{{c1::\(\frac{1}{1-x}\)}}</li><li>If&nbsp;{{c5::\(|x|\)}}&nbsp;{{c4::\(\geq\)&nbsp;\(1\)}} then&nbsp;{{c3::\(\sum\)}} {{c2::\(x^n\)}} {{c1::diverges}}</li></ol>

============================================================

  

    <div>Proof for the convergence of the geometric series&nbsp;\(\sum x^n\) when&nbsp;\(|x|\)&nbsp;\(&lt;\)&nbsp;\(1\)</div><div><ol><li>Take the partial sums</li><li>\(s_n\) = \(\sum_{k=1}^n\) \(x_k\)<br></li><li>=&nbsp;{{c1::\(\frac{1}{1-x}\)}}&nbsp;{{c2::\((1-x^{n+1})\)}} by {{c1::polynomial arithmetic}}</li><li>\(\lim\)&nbsp;\(s_n\) =&nbsp;{{c3::\(\lim_{n\to\infty}\)}}&nbsp;{{c1::\(\frac{1}{1-x}\)}}&nbsp;\(\times\)&nbsp;{{c3::\(\lim_{n\to \infty}\)}} {{c2::\((1-x^{n+1})\)}}<br></li><li>= {{c1::\(\frac{1}{1-x}\)}} \(\times\) {{c2::\((1-\)}} {{c3::\(\lim\)}} {{c2::\(x^{n+1})\)}}</li><ol><li>Since&nbsp;\(|x|\)&nbsp;\(&lt;\)&nbsp;\(1\), {{c2::\((1 -\)}} {{c3::\(\lim\)}} {{c2::\(x^{n+1})\)}} = {{c4::\(1 - 0\)}}</li></ol><li>= {{c1::\(\frac{1}{1-x}\)&nbsp;}}</li></ol><div>If&nbsp;\(|x|\)&nbsp;\(\geq\)&nbsp;\(1\) diverges because&nbsp;\(x_n\) {{c5::\(\not\) \(\to\)&nbsp;\(0\)}}</div></div>

============================================================

  

    <ol><li>For&nbsp;\(a_1\)&nbsp;{{c5::\(\geq\)}}&nbsp;\(a_2\)\(\ldots\){{c5::\(\geq\)}}\(a_n\){{c5::\(\geq\)}}{{c2::\(0\)}}, so {{c5::monotone decreasing}} to {{c2::0}}</li><li>Then&nbsp;{{c4::\(\sum\)}} {{c3::\(a_k\)}} converges iff&nbsp;{{c4::\(\sum\)}} {{c1::\(2^k\)}} {{c2::\(a_{2^k}\)}} converges</li></ol>

============================================================

  

    <div><ol><li>For&nbsp;\(a_1\)&nbsp;\(\geq\)&nbsp;\(a_2\)\(\ldots\)\(\geq\)\(a_n\)\(\geq\)\(0\), so monotone decreasing to 0</li><li>Then&nbsp;\(\sum a_k\) converges iff&nbsp;\(\sum 2^k a_{2^k}\)</li></ol><div>Proof idea:</div></div><div><ul><li>\(\sum a_k\) =&nbsp;\(a_1\) <span style="font-size: 16.6667px;">+&nbsp;</span>{{c1::\((a_2+a_3)\)}} + {{c2::\((a_4+a_5 + a_6 + a_7)\)}}&nbsp;\(\ldots\)<br></li><li>\(\sum 2^k a_{2^k}\) =&nbsp;\(a_1\)&nbsp;+{{c2::&nbsp;\((a_2+a_2)\)}} + {{c1::\((a_4+a_4 + a_4 + a_4)\)}}&nbsp;\(\ldots\)</li><li>Write the partial sums as&nbsp;\(s_n\) = \(\sum_{k=1}^n a_k\) and \(t_n\) = \(\sum_{k=1}^n 2^k a_{2^k}\)</li><li>Since&nbsp;\(a_k\)&nbsp;\(\geq\)&nbsp;\(a_{k+1}\)</li><ul><li>If&nbsp;\(n\)&nbsp;{{c3::\(&lt;\)&nbsp;\(2^k\)}}</li><li>\(s_n\){{c5::&nbsp;\(\leq\)}}&nbsp;\(t_n\)<br></li><li>By {{c4::comparison}} test&nbsp;\(t_n\) {{c5::converging}} implies&nbsp;\(s_n\) {{c5::converging}}</li></ul></ul></div>

============================================================

  

    <div><ol><li>For&nbsp;\(a_1\)&nbsp;\(\geq\)&nbsp;\(a_2\)\(\ldots\)\(\geq\)\(a_n\)\(\geq\)\(0\), so monotone decreasing to 0</li><li>Then&nbsp;\(\sum a_k\) converges iff&nbsp;\(\sum 2^k a_{2^k}\)</li></ol><div>Proof idea:</div></div><div><ul><li>Write&nbsp;\(s_n\) =&nbsp;\(\sum_{k=1}^n a_k\) and&nbsp;\(t_n\) = \(\sum_{k=1}^n 2^k a_{2^k}\)<br></li><li>{{c1::\(2\)}} \(s_n\) =&nbsp;\(2\)&nbsp;\(a_1\) <span style="font-size: 16.6667px;">+&nbsp;</span>\(2\)&nbsp;\(a_2\)+&nbsp;{{c1::\(2\) (\(a_3+a_4\))}} +&nbsp;{{c2::\(2\) (\(a_5+a_6+a_7+a_8\))}}&nbsp;+&nbsp;\(\ldots\)<br></li><li>\(t_n\) =&nbsp;\(a_1\) + (\(a_2+a_2\)) +&nbsp;{{c2::\(4 a_4\)}} +&nbsp;{{c1::\(8 a_8\)}}+&nbsp;\(\ldots\)<br></li><li>Thus for&nbsp;\(n\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;{{c3::\(2^k\)}}, then&nbsp;\(t_n\){{c4::&nbsp;\(&lt;\)}}&nbsp;\(2\)&nbsp;\(s_n\), since&nbsp;\(a_1\)&nbsp;{{c4::\(&lt;\)}}&nbsp;\(2 a_1\),&nbsp;\(4 a_4\)&nbsp;{{c4::\(&lt;\)}}\(2\) (\(a_3+a_4\)) since&nbsp;\(a_4\)&nbsp;{{c4::\(&lt;\)}}&nbsp;\(a_3\) and so on</li><ul><li>If&nbsp;\(t_n\)&nbsp;{{c4::\(&lt;\)&nbsp;}}\(2 s_n\) and&nbsp;{{c5::\(t_n\) converges}} {{c4::so does&nbsp;\(2\)&nbsp;\(s_n\)}} by {{c5::comparison}} test</li><li>If&nbsp;\(2 s_n\) converges so does&nbsp;\(s_n\)</li></ul></ul></div>

============================================================

  

    <div><ol><li>\(\sum \frac{1}{k^p}\) converges iff&nbsp;\(p\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(1\)<br></li></ol><div>Proof:</div></div><div><ol><li>\(p\)&nbsp;\(\leq\)&nbsp;\(0\) implies terms {{c1::do not go to 0}} so the series diverges<br></li><li>For: \(p\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(0\)<br></li><ol><li>By the {{c5::cauchy condesation test}}, \(\sum \frac{1}{k^p}\) converges iff the following converges:</li><li>\(\sum\) {{c1::\(\frac{2^k}{2^{kp} }\)}} = \(\sum\) {{c2::\(2^{k (1-p)}\)}}<br></li><li>Which is a geometric series that converges iff&nbsp;{{c3::\(2^{1-p}\)}}&nbsp;{{c4::\(&lt;\)&nbsp;\(1\)}} which is true iff {{c3::&nbsp;\(1-p\)}} is {{c4::less than 0}} so&nbsp;\(p\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(1\)</li></ol></ol></div>

============================================================

  

    <div>Proof that sum of factorials \(\sum \frac{1}{n!}\)converges:</div><div><ol><li>\(\sum \frac{1}{n^p}\) converges for all&nbsp;\(p\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(1\)<br></li><li>Take&nbsp;\(\frac{1}{n^2}\),&nbsp;\(\frac{1}{n!}\)&nbsp;\(&lt;\)&nbsp;{{c1::\(\frac{1}{n^2}\)}} for all&nbsp;\(n\)&nbsp;{{c2::\(\geq\)&nbsp;\(4\)}}</li><li>Thus, by the {{c3::comparison}} test, since {{c1::\(\frac{1}{n^2}\)&nbsp;}} converges so does \(\frac{1}{n!}\)</li><li>Alternatively, because the partial sums are {{c4::bounded}} and {{c5::nonegative}} it converges</li></ol></div>

============================================================

  

    Rate of convergence of&nbsp;\(\sum \frac{1}{k!}\) =&nbsp;\(e\):<br><ol><li>\(e\) -&nbsp;{{c1::\(\sum_{k=1}^n\)}} {{c2::\(\frac{1}{k!}\)}}&nbsp;</li><li>\(\leq\)&nbsp;{{c3::\(\frac{1}{(n+1)!}\)}}( \(1\) + {{c4::\(\frac{1}{n+1}\)}} + {{c4::\(\frac{1}{(n+1)^2}\)}} + \(\ldots\))</li><li>Which is a {{c5::gemeotric}} series with {{c4::ratio&nbsp;\(\frac{1}{n+1}\)}} which converges to&nbsp;{{c5::\(\frac{n+1}{n}\)}}</li><li>\(\leq\){{c1::\(\frac{1}{n!n}\)}}<br></li><li>Thus&nbsp;\(e\) is always within {{c1::\(\frac{1}{n!n}\)}} of any partial sum&nbsp;\(s_n\)</li></ol>

============================================================

  

    Proof that e is irrational given that&nbsp;\(|e - \sum_{k=1}^{n}\frac{1}{k!}|\)&nbsp;\(\leq\)&nbsp;\(\frac{1}{n!n}\):<br><ol><li>If&nbsp;\(e\) =&nbsp;\(\frac{m}{n}\), so it is rational</li><li>\(0\) \(\leq\){{c1::\(n!\)}}({{c3::\(|e - \sum_{k=1}^{n} \frac{1}{k!}|\)}} ) \(\leq\)&nbsp;{{c2::\(\frac{1}{n}\)}} by {{c1::multiplication by&nbsp;\(n!\)}}<br></li><li>Since everything is positive and all elements of the partial sum contain {{c4::factorials smaller than&nbsp;\(n!\)}}, all elements of the equation would be {{c5::integers}} if&nbsp;\(e\) where to be rational</li><li>Thus there would have to be an {{c5::integer}} between&nbsp;{{c5::\(0\) and&nbsp;\(\frac{1}{n}\)}}, which is impossible</li></ol>

============================================================

  

    <ol><li>Root test: Given a series&nbsp;\(\sum a_n\), then let&nbsp;\(\alpha\) =&nbsp;{{c5::{{c3::\(\lim\)}}}}&nbsp;{{c4::{{c2::\(\sup\)}}}}&nbsp;{{c4::{{c1::\(\sqrt[n]{a_n}\)}}}}, which can be calculated in the {{c4::{{c2::extended reals}}}}</li><li>Then:</li><ol><li>\(\alpha\)&nbsp;\(\leq\)&nbsp;\(1\)&nbsp;\(\implies\)&nbsp;\(\sum a_n\) converges<br></li><li>\(\alpha\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(1\)&nbsp;\(\implies\)&nbsp;\(\sum a_n\) diverges</li><li>\(\alpha\)&nbsp;\(=\)&nbsp;\(1\)&nbsp;\(\implies\) test inconclusive</li></ol></ol>

============================================================

  

    <div><ol><li>Given a series&nbsp;\(\sum a_n\), then let&nbsp;\(\alpha\) ={{c5::&nbsp;\(\lim\)&nbsp;\(\sup\)&nbsp;\(\sqrt[n]{a_n}\)}}, which can be calculated in the {{c5::extended reals}}</li><li>Then:</li><ol><li>\(\alpha\)&nbsp;{{c1::\(\leq\)&nbsp;\(1\)}}&nbsp;\(\implies\)&nbsp;{{c2::\(\sum a_n\) converges}}<br></li><li>\(\alpha\)&nbsp;{{c2::\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(1\)}}&nbsp;\(\implies\)&nbsp;{{c1::\(\sum a_n\) diverges}}</li><li>\(\alpha\)&nbsp;{{c3::\(=\)&nbsp;\(1\)}}&nbsp;\(\implies\) {{c4::test inconclusive}}</li></ol></ol></div>

============================================================

  

    <div><ol><li>Given a series&nbsp;\(\sum a_n\), then let&nbsp;\(\alpha\) =&nbsp;\(\lim\)&nbsp;\(\sup\)&nbsp;\(\sqrt[n]{a_n}\), which can be calculated in the extended reals</li><li>Then:</li><ol><li>\(\alpha\)&nbsp;\(\leq\)&nbsp;\(1\)&nbsp;\(\implies\)&nbsp;\(\sum a_n\) converges<br></li><li>\(\alpha\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(1\)&nbsp;\(\implies\)&nbsp;\(\sum a_n\) diverges</li><li>\(\alpha\)&nbsp;\(=\)&nbsp;\(1\)&nbsp;\(\implies\) test inconclusive</li></ol></ol><div>Proof:</div></div><div><ol><li>If&nbsp;\(\alpha\)&nbsp;\(&lt;\)&nbsp;\(1\) choose&nbsp;\(\beta\)&nbsp;\(\in\){{c1::&nbsp;\((\alpha,1)\)}}</li><ol><li>Then&nbsp;\(\exists\)&nbsp;\(N\) such that&nbsp;\(\sqrt[n]{a_n}\)&nbsp;\(&lt;\)&nbsp;\(\beta\)&nbsp; by {{c5::definition of&nbsp;\(\lim \sup\)}}</li><li>Which means&nbsp;{{c2::\(|a_n|\)}}&nbsp;\(&lt;\)&nbsp;{{c3::\(\beta^n\)}} for&nbsp;\(n\)&nbsp; \(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(N\)</li><li>Since&nbsp;\(\beta\){{c1::\(&lt;\)\(1\)}},&nbsp;\(\sum\)&nbsp;{{c3::\(\beta^n\)}} converges and thus by {{c4::comparison}} test \(\sum\)&nbsp;\(a_n\)&nbsp; converges</li></ol></ol></div>

============================================================

  

    <ol><ol><li>Given a series&nbsp;\(\sum a_n\), then let&nbsp;\(\alpha\) =&nbsp;\(\lim\)&nbsp;\(\sup\)&nbsp;\(\sqrt[n]{a_n}\), which can be calculated in the extended reals</li><li>Then:</li><ol><li>\(\alpha\)&nbsp;\(\leq\)&nbsp;\(1\)&nbsp;\(\implies\)&nbsp;\(\sum a_n\) converges<br></li><li>\(\alpha\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(1\)&nbsp;\(\implies\)&nbsp;\(\sum a_n\) diverges</li><li>\(\alpha\)&nbsp;\(=\)&nbsp;\(1\)&nbsp;\(\implies\) test inconclusive</li></ol></ol><div>Proof:</div><div><ol><li>If&nbsp;\(\alpha\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(1\)</li><ol><li>There exists a {{c1::subsequence \(\sqrt[n_k]{a_{n_k} }\)&nbsp;}} which converges to&nbsp;\(\alpha\)&nbsp;{{c2::\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(1\)}} which implies&nbsp;\(|a_{n_k}|\)&nbsp;{{c3::\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(1\)}} for {{c4::infinitely many terms}} so \(a_n\) {{c5::\(\not \to\)}}&nbsp;{{c4::\(0\)}} meaning the series {{c2::does not converge}}<br></li></ol></ol></div></ol>

============================================================

  

    <ul><li>If \(\exists\) \(N\) such that \(\forall\)&nbsp;\(n\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(N\):</li><ul><li>Iff&nbsp;{{c2::\(\lim\)&nbsp;\(\sup\)}}&nbsp;{{c3::\(|\)}} {{c1::\(\frac{a_{n+1} }{a_n}\)}} {{c3::\(|\)}}&nbsp;{{c4::\(&lt;\) \(1\)}}, the series {{c3::converges}}</li><li>iff {{c2::\(\lim\)&nbsp;\(\sup\)}}&nbsp;{{c3::\(|\)}} {{c1::\(\frac{a_{n+1} }{a_n}\)}} {{c3::\(|\)}}&nbsp;{{c5::\(\geq\)&nbsp;\(1\)}}, the series {{c3::diverges}}</li></ul></ul>

============================================================

  

    <ul><li>If \(\exists\) \(N\) such that \(\forall\)&nbsp;\(n\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(N\):</li><ul><li>Iff&nbsp;\(\lim\)&nbsp;\(\sup\)&nbsp;\(|\frac{a_{n+1}}{a_n}|\)&nbsp;\(&lt;\) \(1\), it converges</li><li>iff \(\lim\)&nbsp;\(\sup\)&nbsp;\(|\frac{a_{n+1}}{a_n}|\)&nbsp;\(\geq\)&nbsp;\(1\), the series diverges<br></li></ul></ul>Proof:<br><ol><li>Iff&nbsp;\(\lim\)&nbsp;\(\sup\)&nbsp;\(|\frac{a_{n+1}}{a_n}|\) = \(\alpha\)\(&lt;\) \(1\),&nbsp;\(\exists\)&nbsp;\(\beta\)&nbsp;\(\in\)&nbsp;\((\alpha,1)\),&nbsp;\(\forall\)&nbsp;\(n\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(N\)<br></li><ol><li>\(|a_{n+1}|\)&nbsp;\(&lt;\){{c1::&nbsp;\(\beta\)}}&nbsp;\(|a_{n}|\)&nbsp;\(&lt;\)&nbsp;\(\ldots\)&nbsp;\(&lt;\) {{c2::\(\beta^n\)}} \(|a_1|\)<br></li><li>Thus {{c4::\(|a_{N+k}|\)}}&nbsp;\(&lt;\)&nbsp;{{c2::\(\beta^k\)}}&nbsp;{{c5::\(|a_{N}|\)&nbsp;}}</li><li>By cauchy criterion: {{c3::\(\sum_{k=0}^{\infty}\)}} {{c4::\(|a_{N+k}|\)}}&nbsp;\(\leq\)&nbsp;{{c5::\(|a_N|\)}}&nbsp;{{c3::\(\sum_{k=0}^{\infty}\)}}{{c2::\(\beta^k\)}}</li></ol><li>Iff&nbsp;\(\lim\)&nbsp;\(\sup\)&nbsp;\(|\frac{a_{n+1}}{a_n}|\) = \(\alpha\)\(&gt;\) \(1\): the {{c5::terms}} do {{c5::not got to 0}}<br></li></ol>

============================================================

  

    <div>What is a {{c2::power}} series?</div><div><ol><li>{{c5::\(\sum_{n=0}^{\infty}\)}}&nbsp;{{c1::\(c_n\)}}&nbsp;{{c2::\(z^n\)}}&nbsp; for {{c1::\(c_n\)}}, {{c2::\(z\)}}&nbsp;\(\in\)&nbsp;{{c5::\(C\)}}</li><li>Where {{c1::\(c_n\)}} {{c3::are coefficients}} and&nbsp;{{c2::\(z\)}} a {{c4::complex variable}}</li></ol></div>

============================================================

  

    <ol><li>\(\sum_{n=0}^{\infty}\)&nbsp;\(c_n\)&nbsp;\(z^n\)&nbsp; for \(c_n, z\)&nbsp;\(\in\)&nbsp;\(C\)</li><li>Where \(c_n\) are coefficients and&nbsp;\(z\) a complex variable</li></ol><div>For what values of&nbsp;\(z\) does such power a series converge?</div><div><ol><li>If&nbsp;\(\alpha\) =&nbsp;{{c3::\(\lim \sup\)&nbsp;}}{{c1::\(\sqrt[n]{|c_n|}\)}}, let&nbsp;\(R\) =&nbsp;{{c2::\(\frac{1}{\alpha}\)}}</li><li>Then&nbsp;\(\sum c_n z^n\)</li><ol><li>{{c4::Converges}} if&nbsp;\(|z|\)&nbsp;{{c3::\(&lt;\)}} \(R\)</li><li>{{c3::Diverges}} if \(|z|\)&nbsp;{{c4::\(&gt;\)}} \(R\)</li></ol><li>Where R is the {{c5::radius of convergence}}, and the region for which the series converges forms a {{c5::disk}}&nbsp;</li></ol></div>

============================================================

  

    <ol><li>\(\sum_{n=0}^{\infty}\)&nbsp;\(c_n\)&nbsp;\(z^n\)&nbsp; for \(c_n, z\)&nbsp;\(\in\)&nbsp;\(C\)</li><li>Where \(c_n\) are coefficients and&nbsp;\(z\) a complex variable</li></ol><div>Proof for the radius of convergence:</div><div><ol><li>{{c3::\(\lim\) \(\sup\)}} {{c2::\(\sqrt[n]{|c_n z^n|}\)}} = {{c3::\(\lim\) \(\sup\)}} {{c2::\(|z|\)}}&nbsp;{{c1::\(\sqrt[n]{|c_n|}\)}}</li><li>By root test, this converges if&nbsp;</li><li>{{c2::&nbsp;\(|z|\)}}&nbsp;{{c1::\(\sqrt[n]{|c_n|}\)}}&nbsp;{{c5::\(&lt;\)&nbsp;\(1\)}}</li><li>Which means</li><li>{{c2::\(|z|\)}} {{c5::\(&lt;\)}} {{c4::\(\frac{1}{ \lim \sup \sqrt[n]{|c_n|} }\)}}&nbsp;= R</li></ol></div>

============================================================

  

    <div>What is summation by parts?</div><div><ul><li>Let&nbsp;\(A_n\) =&nbsp;\(\sum_{k=0}^n\)&nbsp;\(a_k\) for&nbsp;\(n\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(0\), let&nbsp;\(A_{n-1}\) = 0 as well</li><li>Then for two series&nbsp;\(\{a_n\}\),&nbsp;\(\{b_n\}\)</li><li>\(\sum_{p}^q\)&nbsp;\(a_n\)\(b_n\) =&nbsp;\(\sum_{p}^{q-1}\) \(A_n\) (\(b_n - b_{n+1}\)) +&nbsp;\(A_q\)&nbsp;\(b_q\) -&nbsp;\(A_{p-1}\)&nbsp;\(b_p\)</li><li>This is similar to integration by parts:</li><li>\(\int\)&nbsp;\(u\)&nbsp;\(d_v\) = {{c5::\(uv\) - \(\int\)&nbsp;\(v\)&nbsp;\(d_u\)&nbsp;&nbsp;}}<br></li><li>&nbsp;exchaning&nbsp;\(\int\) for&nbsp;\(\sum\), with&nbsp;{{c1::\(A_n\)}} playing the role of&nbsp;{{c2::\(v\)}} and&nbsp;{{c2::\(a_n\)}} being&nbsp;{{c1::\(d_v\)}}, with&nbsp;{{c3::\(b\)}} playing the role of&nbsp;{{c4::\(u\)}} and&nbsp;{{c4::\(b_n - b_{n+1}\)}} playing the role of&nbsp;{{c3::\(d_u\)}}</li></ul></div>

============================================================

  

    <ul><li>Let&nbsp;\(A_n\) =&nbsp;\(\sum_{k=0}^n\)&nbsp;\(a_k\) for&nbsp;\(n\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(0\), let&nbsp;\(A_{n-1}\) = 0 as well</li><li>Then for two series&nbsp;\(\{a_n\}\),&nbsp;\(\{b_n\}\)</li><li>\(\sum_{p}^q\)&nbsp;\(a_n\)\(b_n\) =&nbsp;\(\sum_{p}^{q-1}\) \(A_n\) (\(b_n - b_{n+1}\)) +&nbsp;\(A_q\)&nbsp;\(b_q\) -&nbsp;\(A_{p-1}\)&nbsp;\(b_p\)</li></ul><div>Geometric proof for summation by parts:</div><div><ol><li>Imagine a square broken up into small pieces on two dimensions</li><li>For the bottom dimension we {{c1::break into&nbsp;\(n\) segments}}</li><ol><li>{{c1::<img src="paste-b9474460dc125c003be21a1f976b1f247a99b88d.jpg">}}</li></ol><li>For the vertical dimension we {{c2::create increasing intervals&nbsp;}}</li><ol><li>{{c2::<img src="paste-6559e4eab3eaa9668262387444a2b285d7658328.jpg">}}</li></ol><li>Summing the two series {{c3::adds up the ares of the regions defined&nbsp; by the series}}<br></li><ol><li>{{c3::<img src="paste-a730999c2ecbd31b25ac1d6a65dedc936c8a6f3a.jpg">}}</li></ol><li>Integration by parts then says to {{c3::get the area of those parts}}, {{c4::take the area of the whole box}} and {{c5::subtract the complements}}</li><ol><li>{{c4::<img src="paste-6eaf2e7425f0f08dbbc616762781576753f1cda4.jpg">}}</li></ol><li>I.e the integral of udv&nbsp; is thus {{c4::UV}} - {{c5::the integral of v du}}</li><li>So what we are performing is a {{c3::vertical}} vs {{c5::horizontal}} integration switch</li></ol></div>

============================================================

  

    If the {{c2::partial sums}} \(A_n\) = {{c2::\(\sum_{k=0}^n\)}} {{c2::\(a_k\)}} are {{c1::bounded}}, and&nbsp;\(b_n\)&nbsp;{{c4::\(\to\)}}&nbsp;{{c3::\(0\)}}, then&nbsp;\(\sum\){{c5::\(a_n\)&nbsp;\(b_n\)}} converges

============================================================

  

    Proof that if&nbsp;\(\sum_{k=0}^n a_k\) are bounded and&nbsp;\(b_n\)&nbsp;\(\to\)&nbsp;\(0\) then&nbsp;\(\sum\)\(a_n\)&nbsp;\(b_n\) converges:<br><ol><li>\(|A_n|\) = \(\sum_{k=0}^n |a_k|\)&nbsp;&nbsp;\(\leq\)&nbsp;\(M\)</li><li>There exists&nbsp;\(N\) such that&nbsp;\(b_N\)&nbsp;\(\leq\){{c1::&nbsp;\(\frac{\epsilon}{2M}\)}} since&nbsp;\(b_n\)&nbsp;{{c2::\(\to\)&nbsp;\(0\)}}</li><li>Then for&nbsp;\(q\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(p\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(N\) by integration by parts</li><li>Integration by parts formula&nbsp;\(\leq\)&nbsp;\(M\)&nbsp;\(| \)&nbsp;\(\sum\) ({{c3::\(b_n - b_{n+1}\)}} ) +&nbsp;{{c4::\(b_p\)}} +&nbsp;{{c5::\(b_q\)}}&nbsp;\(|\)</li><li>\(\leq\)&nbsp;{{c1::\(2\)&nbsp;\(M\)}}&nbsp;{{c4::\(b_p\)}}<br></li><li>\(\leq\)&nbsp;{{c1::\(2\)&nbsp;\(M\)}}&nbsp;{{c2::\(b_N\)}}<br></li><li>\(\leq\)&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)<br></li></ol>

============================================================

  

    <ol><li>If&nbsp;{{c2::\(|c_n|\)&nbsp;}}{{c3::\(\geq\)}}{{c1::&nbsp;\(|c_{n+1}|\)}}, \(\forall\)&nbsp;\(n\)</li><li>And&nbsp;{{c2::\(c_n\)}}&nbsp;\(\to\)&nbsp;{{c4::\(0\)}} with {{c5::alternating sign}}</li><li>Then&nbsp;\(\sum\)&nbsp;{{c2::\(c_n\)}} converges</li></ol>

============================================================

  

    <div><ol><li>If&nbsp;\(|c_n|\)&nbsp;\(\geq\)&nbsp;\(|c_{n+1}|\), \(\forall\)&nbsp;\(k\)</li><li>And&nbsp;\(c_n\)&nbsp;\(\to\)&nbsp;\(0\) with alternating sign</li><li>Then&nbsp;\(\sum\)&nbsp;\(c_n\) converges</li></ol><div>Proof by Dirichlet test</div></div><div>Take&nbsp;\(a_n\) =&nbsp;{{c1::\((-1)^{n+1}\)}} and&nbsp;\(b_n\) to be&nbsp;{{c2::\(|c_n|\)}}, one has {{c5::{{c3::bounded partial sums}}}} the other {{c5::{{c4::goes to 0}}}}</div>

============================================================

  

    Motivation for the product of two serise: power series<br><ol><li>\(\sum a_k z^k\)&nbsp;\(\sum b_k z^k\)<br></li><li>={{c1::&nbsp;\(a_0 b_0\)}} +&nbsp;{{c2::\((a_0 b_1 + a_1 b_0)z\)}}&nbsp;\(\ldots\)</li><li>=&nbsp;\(\sum\) {{c2::\(d_k\) \(z^k\)}}</li><li>where&nbsp;{{c2::\(d_n\)}} =&nbsp;{{c5::\(\sum_{k=0}^n\)}} {{c4::\(a_k\)}} {{c3::\(b_{n-k}\)}}</li></ol>

============================================================

  

    What is the product of two series&nbsp;\(\sum a_n\) and&nbsp;\(\sum b_n\)?<br><br><ol><li>{{c4::\(d_n\)}} =&nbsp;{{c3::\(\sum_{k=0}^n\)&nbsp;}}{{c2::\(a_k\)}}&nbsp;{{c1::\(b_{n-k}\)}}<br></li><li>\(\sum a_n\)&nbsp;\(\sum b_n\) =&nbsp;{{c5::\(\sum\)}}&nbsp;{{c4::\(d_k\)}}</li></ol>

============================================================

  

    The {{c1::product}} of two {{c3::series}} may {{c5::{{c4::not converge}}}} even if {{c5::{{c2::the indepndent series do}}}}

============================================================

  

    <br>If&nbsp;\(\sum a_n\) and&nbsp;\(\sum\)&nbsp;\(b_n\) converge {{c1::absolutely}} to&nbsp;\(A,B\) then&nbsp;{{c3::\(\sum a_n\)&nbsp;\(\sum b_n\)}} =&nbsp;\(\sum d_k\) =&nbsp;\(\sum_{n=0}^\infty\) {{c5::\( \sum_{k=0}^n\)&nbsp;}}{{c4::\(a_k\)}}&nbsp;{{c3::\(b_{n-k}\)}} converges to&nbsp;{{c2::\(A\)\(B\)}}

============================================================

  

    <br><ol><li>A series&nbsp;{{c3::\(\sum\)}} {{c4::\(a_n\)}} {{c5::converges}} {{c1::absolutely}} iff&nbsp;{{c3::\(\sum\)}} {{c2::\(|a_n|\)}} {{c5::converges}}</li></ol>

============================================================

  

    If a series&nbsp;\(\sum\)&nbsp;\(a_n\) {{c5::{{c3::converges}}}} {{c4::{{c1::absolutely}}}} then it {{c4::{{c2::converges}}}}

============================================================

  

    Proof that absolute convergence implies convergence:<br><ol><li>Apply {{c5::Cauchy}} criterion</li><li>{{c1::\(|\)}} {{c4::\(\sum_{k=m}^n\)}} \(a_k\)&nbsp;{{c1::\(|\)}} \(\leq\){{c4::&nbsp;\(\sum_{k=m}^n\)&nbsp;}}{{c1::\(|\)}}&nbsp;\(a_k\) {{c1::\(|\)}}&nbsp;{{c2::\(\leq\)&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)}} by {{c3::absolute convergence}}<br></li></ol>

============================================================

  

    {{c4::Rearrangements}} of a {{c5::conditionally}} convergent series do {{c1::not have to converge}}, and if {{c3::they do converge}} they {{c1::do not have to converge}} to {{c2::the same limit}}

============================================================

  

    If a series convegerges {{c1::conditionally}}, then a {{c5::rearragenement}} can be made to have an {{c3::arbitrary}}&nbsp; {{c2::limit}},{{c3::&nbsp;\(\lim \sup\)}}, or&nbsp;{{c4::\(\lim\)&nbsp;\(\inf\)}}

============================================================

  

    If a series converges {{c1::absolutely}} all {{c4::rearrangements}} have {{c5::{{c2::the same}}}} {{c5::{{c3::limit}}}}

============================================================

  

    <ol><li>For \( (X,d_x),(Y,d_y)\) metric spaces, take subsets \(E\) \(\subset\) \(X\) with \(p\) a limit point of \(E\), let \(f\) \(\in\) \(E\) \(\to\) \(Y\)</li><li>Then \(\lim_{x \to p}\)\(f(x)\) \(=\)&nbsp;\(q\) if:</li><ol><li>\(\forall\)\(\epsilon\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(0\)<br></li><li>\(\exists\)\(\delta\)\(&gt;\)\(0\) such that:</li><li>\(\forall\)&nbsp;\(x\)&nbsp;\(\in\)\(E\), {{c1::\(0\)\(&lt;\)}}&nbsp;{{c4::\(d_x\)\((x,p)\)}}{{c2::\(&lt;\)\(\delta\)}} implies:</li><ol><li>{{c5::\(d_y\)\((f(x),q)\)}}&nbsp;{{c3::\(&lt;\)&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)}}</li></ol><li>Requireing {{c1::\(0\)\(&lt;\)}}&nbsp;\(d_x\)\((x,p)\) makes sure&nbsp;{{c2::\(x\)&nbsp;\(\neq\)&nbsp;\(p\)}}</li></ol><li>Pictographically</li><ol><li><img src="paste-3d438b371969899cbfc04c7f726ad7097b48fa1e.jpg"></li></ol></ol>

    \(\lim_{x \to p}\)&nbsp;\(f(x) \)=\(q\) iff
<br><ol><li>\(\forall\) seq&nbsp;\(\{p_n\}\) in&nbsp;\(E\) with&nbsp;\(p\) \(\not \in \)&nbsp;\(\{p_n\}\)<br></li><ol><li>\(p_n\)&nbsp;\(\to\)&nbsp;\(p\) implies&nbsp;\(f\)(\(p_n\))\(\to\)&nbsp;\(q\)<br></li></ol></ol>Proof forward:<br><ol><li>\(\exists\)&nbsp;\(\delta\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(0\) such that&nbsp;\(0\)&nbsp;\(&lt;\)&nbsp;\(d(x,p)\)&nbsp;\(&lt;\)&nbsp;\(\delta\)<br></li><ol><li>Implies&nbsp;\(d(f(x),q)\)&nbsp;\(&lt;\)&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)</li></ol><li>For a seq&nbsp;\(\{p_n\}\) in&nbsp;\(E\) with&nbsp;\(p\) \(\not \in \)&nbsp;\(\{p_n\}\)</li><ol><li>There exists&nbsp;\(N\) such that&nbsp;\(\forall\)&nbsp;\(n\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(N\)</li><li>{{c2::\(d\)\((p_n,p)\)}}{{c1::&nbsp;\(&lt;\)&nbsp;\(\delta\)}} because{{c3::&nbsp;\(p_n\) is convergent}}<br></li><li>Which implies {{c4::\(d(f(p_n),q)\)}}{{c5::&nbsp;\(&lt;\)&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)}}</li></ol></ol>

============================================================

  

    \(\lim_{x \to p}\)&nbsp;\(f(x) \)=\(q\) iff
<br><ol><li>\(\forall\) seq&nbsp;\(\{p_n\}\) in&nbsp;\(E\) with&nbsp;\(p\) \(\not \in \)&nbsp;\(\{p_n\}\)<br></li><ol><li>\(p_n\)&nbsp;\(\to\)&nbsp;\(p\) implies&nbsp;\(f\)(\(p_n\))\(\to\)&nbsp;\(q\)<br></li></ol></ol>Proof backward:<br><ol><li>We want to show: If&nbsp;\(\lim_{x \to p}\)&nbsp;\(f(x)\)&nbsp;\(\neq\) \(q\) then&nbsp;\(\exists\)&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(0\) such that&nbsp;&nbsp;</li><ol><li>{{c3::\(\forall\)}}&nbsp;\(\delta\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(0\) there {{c2::exists}}&nbsp;\(x\)&nbsp;\(\in\)&nbsp;\(E\) such that</li><li>\(d(x,p)\)&nbsp;\(&lt;\)&nbsp;\(\delta\) but&nbsp;\(d\)\((f(x),q)\)&nbsp;{{c1::\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)}}</li></ol><li>We propose a bad sequence&nbsp;\(\delta_n\) ={{c4::&nbsp;\(\frac{1}{n}\)}} and choose&nbsp;\(x_n\)&nbsp; such that {{c5::the condition above holds}}</li><li>\(x_n\)&nbsp;\(\to\)&nbsp;\(p\) but \(d\)\((f(x_n),q)\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)<br></li><li>Thus&nbsp;\(f(x_n)\)&nbsp;\(\not \to\)&nbsp;\(q\)</li></ol>

    <ol><li>For&nbsp;\(X,Y\) abstract metric spaces and&nbsp;\(E\)&nbsp;\(\subset\)&nbsp;\(X\) wtih&nbsp;\(p\)&nbsp;\(\in\)&nbsp;\(E\)</li><li>A function&nbsp;\(f\)&nbsp;\(:\)&nbsp;\(E\)&nbsp;\(\to\)&nbsp;\(Y\) is {{c5::continous}} at&nbsp;\(p\) iff:</li><ol><li>\(\forall\)&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(0\), \(\exists\)&nbsp;\(\delta\)&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(0\) such that</li><li>\(\forall\)&nbsp;\(x\)&nbsp;\(\in\)&nbsp;\(E\),&nbsp;{{c3::\(d\)\((x,p)\)}}&nbsp;{{c1::\(&lt;\)&nbsp;\(\delta\)}} implies{{c4::&nbsp;\(d\)\((f(x),f(p))\)}}&nbsp;{{c2::\(&lt;\)&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)}}</li></ol><li>Crucially,&nbsp;\(p\) is an element of&nbsp;\(E\) and&nbsp;\(x\) is allowed to equal&nbsp;\(p\)</li></ol>

============================================================

  

    <ol><li>For&nbsp;\(X,Y\) abstract metric spaces and&nbsp;\(E\)&nbsp;{{c1::\(\subset\)&nbsp;\(X\)}} wtih&nbsp;\(p\)&nbsp;{{c2::\(\in\)&nbsp;\(E\)}}</li><li>A function&nbsp;\(f\)&nbsp;\(:\)&nbsp;\(E\)&nbsp;\(\to\)&nbsp;\(Y\) is continous at&nbsp;\(p\) iff:</li><ol><li>{{c5::\(\forall\)&nbsp;}}\(\epsilon\)&nbsp;{{c1::\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(0\)}}, {{c4::\(\exists\)}}&nbsp;\(\delta\)&nbsp;{{c1::\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(0\)}} such that</li><li>\(\forall\)&nbsp;\(x\)&nbsp;\(\in\)&nbsp;\(E\),&nbsp;\(d\)\((x,p)\)&nbsp;\(&lt;\)&nbsp;\(\delta\) implies&nbsp;\(d\)\((f(x),f(p))\)&nbsp;\(&lt;\)&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)</li></ol><li>Crucially, \(x\) is allowed to {{c3::equal&nbsp;\(p\)}}</li></ol>

============================================================

  

    For \(X,Y\) metric spaces, If&nbsp;\(p\) is a {{c5::limit point}} of&nbsp;\(E\)&nbsp;\(\subset\)&nbsp;\(X\), \(f\)\(:\)&nbsp;\(E\)&nbsp;\(\to\)&nbsp;\(Y\)&nbsp; being {{c1::continous}} at&nbsp;\(p\) is equivalent to&nbsp;{{c4::\(\lim_{x \to p}\)}}{{c3::&nbsp;\(f\)\((x)\)}} =&nbsp;{{c2::\(f\)\((p)\)}}

============================================================

  

    If&nbsp;\(\{x_n\}\) is a {{c1::convergent}} sequence then&nbsp;\(f\) being {{c5::continous}} is equivalent to saying&nbsp;{{c3::\(\lim_{n \to \infty}\)}}{{c2::\(f\)\((x_n)\)}} =&nbsp;{{c4::\(f\)}}{{c1::(\(\lim_{n \to \infty} x_n\))}}

    Any function from a {{c1::discrete}} metric space to another metric space is {{c3::automatically}} {{c5::{{c2::continous}}}}, as you vaccously {{c5::{{c4::cannot approach points}}}}&nbsp;

============================================================

  

    {{c3::Sums}} and {{c4::products}} of {{c1::continous}} functions are {{c2::continous}} when the {{c5::domain}} is {{c3::R}}

============================================================

  

    {{c1::Quotients}} of {{c3::continous}} functions with domain R {{c2::\(\frac{f}{g}\)}} are also {{c4::continous}}&nbsp; when&nbsp;{{c1::\(g\)&nbsp;}}{{c5::\(\neq\)&nbsp;\(0\)}}

============================================================

  

    For&nbsp;\(f,g\)&nbsp;\(\in\)&nbsp;\(X\)&nbsp;\(\to\)&nbsp;\(R^k\) , which can be represented as&nbsp;\(f\) =&nbsp;\((f_1,\ldots,f_n)\) we can check if&nbsp;\(f\) is {{c5::{{c3::continous}}}} by checking if {{c4::{{c1::each indepdendent&nbsp;\(f_j\)}}}} {{c4::{{c2::is continous}}}}

============================================================

  

    For&nbsp;\(f,g\)&nbsp;\(\in\)&nbsp;\(X\)&nbsp;\(\to\)&nbsp;\(R^k\), if&nbsp;\(f,g\) are continous<br><ol><li>{{c5::{{c1::\(f+g\)}}}} is continous<br></li><li>{{c4::{{c2::\(\lambda f\)}}}} is continous<br></li><li>{{c4::{{c3::\(\langle f,g \rangle\)}}}} is continous</li></ol>

============================================================

  

    For&nbsp;\(f,g\)&nbsp;\(\in\)&nbsp;\(X\)&nbsp;\(\to\)&nbsp;\(R^k\), if&nbsp;\(f,g\) are continous<br><ol><li>\(f+g\) is continous</li><ol><li>Proof:</li><li>\(| f_i(x) - f_i(p)|\)&nbsp;\(\leq\)\(||f(x) - f(y)||\)<br></li><ol><li>If the RHS is&nbsp;\(&lt;\)\(\epsilon\) than LHS {{c1::also is}}, so the {{c2::same&nbsp;\(\delta\)}} will work</li><li>Alternatively, if the LHS is&nbsp;\(&lt; \epsilon\) we can {{c5::find an epsilon for the RHS}}, e.g choose {{c3::\(\sqrt{\frac{\epsilon}{n}}\)}} for the LHs and RHS will be&nbsp;{{c4::\(&lt;\)&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)}}</li></ol></ol></ol>

============================================================

  

    \(f\)&nbsp;\(:\)&nbsp;\(X\)&nbsp;\(\to\)&nbsp;\(Y\) is {{c4::continous}} iff&nbsp;{{c5::\(\forall\)}} {{c3::open}} sets&nbsp;\(U\) in&nbsp;{{c1::\(Y\)}},&nbsp;{{c1::\(f^{-1}\)}}\((U)\) is {{c2::open in&nbsp;\(X\)}}

============================================================

  

    \(f\)&nbsp;\(:\)&nbsp;\(X\)&nbsp;\(\to\)&nbsp;\(Y\) ic continous iff&nbsp;\(\forall\) open sets&nbsp;\(U\) in&nbsp;\(Y\),{{c5::&nbsp;\(f^{-1}\)\}}((U)\) is {{c4::open in&nbsp;\(X\)}}<br>Pictographically:<br>{{c2::<img src="paste-e030b148e58ec11732ec4504b2a4895a6edb4348.jpg">}}{{c3::<img src="paste-ff5d72b2873f2d3f9c6ddc3e936239e7395b26ff.jpg">}}{{c1::<img src="paste-f64d23143cada4355c40722d5f1ffc9b9152ead6.jpg">}}

============================================================

  

    What are the three definitions of function continuity?<br><ol><li>{{c5::\(\epsilon\) -&nbsp;\(\delta\)::name}}:&nbsp;{{c1::\(d(x,p)&lt;\delta\)&nbsp;}}{{c4::\(\implies\)}}&nbsp;{{c1::\(d(f(x),q)\)&lt; \(\epsilon\)}}<br></li><li>{{c5::Sequence::name}}:&nbsp;{{c2::\(\forall\)&nbsp;\(\{x_n\}\)}},&nbsp;{{c2::\(\lim_{n \to \infty} f(x_n)\)}} {{c4::=}}&nbsp;{{c2::\(f\)\((\lim_{n \to \infty} x_n)\)}}</li><li>{{c5::Topological::name}}: {{c4::For}} {{c3::all open sets&nbsp;\(U\) in the codomain}},&nbsp;{{c3::\(f^{-1}(U)\)}} {{c4::is}} {{c3::open in the domain}}</li></ol>

============================================================

  

    How you can think of the topological definition of continuity:<br><ol><li><img src="paste-34e823775f0be1f5c1b6dcbf7c5ddab1c8468d54.jpg"><br></li><ol><li>The pre-image {{c1::does not}} {{c2::contain its endpoints}} because the {{c3::original set&nbsp;\(U_1\)}} in the {{c3::domain}} {{c1::does not}}</li></ol><li><img src="paste-8ccdf58d58d16c68f144b9660d39dcbe051741c0.jpg"><br></li><ol><li>In this case the inverse image must be closed since we have to pick the {{c4::one point within the interval and nothin else}}</li></ol><li><img src="paste-1e90a6eef36ebb4ea75c8c792d00820186a73caa.jpg"><br></li><ol><li>Here the one point is open in E since there is a ball around it in E containing {{c5::just the point}}</li></ol></ol>

============================================================

  

    &nbsp;\(f\)&nbsp;\(:\)&nbsp;\(X\)&nbsp;\(\to\)&nbsp;\(Y\) is continous iff&nbsp;\(\forall\) open sets&nbsp;\(U\) in&nbsp;\(Y\),&nbsp;\(f^{-1}\)\((U)\) is open in&nbsp;\(X\)<br>Proof<br><ol><li><img src="paste-c034d751b0d8adaa299738bc65c8aa07760b1800.jpg"><br></li><li>For any \(U\)&nbsp;\(\in Y\) open, pick any point&nbsp;\(x\)&nbsp;\(\in\)&nbsp;\(f^{-1}(U)\), we want to show&nbsp;\(x\) is interior to \(f^{-1}(U)\)</li><li>Since U is open,&nbsp;\(f(x)\) is interior so there exists a neighbourhood \(N_{\epsilon}(f(x))\) =&nbsp;\(\{y \in Y, d(f(x),y) &lt; \epsilon\}\) with \(N_{\epsilon}(f(x))\)&nbsp;&nbsp;\(\subset\)&nbsp;\(U\)</li><li>By continuity of&nbsp;\(f\),&nbsp;\(\exists\) a {{c1::\(\delta\)-ball&nbsp;\(N_{\delta}(x)\)}}&nbsp; such that {{c1::\(N_{\delta}(x)\)}} is {{c2::mapped into}} {{c5::\(N_{\epsilon}(f(x))\)}}</li><li>Because {{c5::\(N_{\epsilon}(f(x))\)}}&nbsp;{{c3::\(\subset\)&nbsp;\(U\)}}, this means {{c1::\(N_{\delta}(x)\)}}&nbsp;{{c4::\(\subset\)&nbsp;\(f^{-1}(U)\)}}</li><li>Which means&nbsp;\(x\) is an interior point of \(f^{-1}(U)\)&nbsp;</li></ol>

============================================================

  

    &nbsp;\(f\)&nbsp;\(:\)&nbsp;\(X\)&nbsp;\(\to\)&nbsp;\(Y\) is continous iff&nbsp;\(\forall\) open sets&nbsp;\(U\) in&nbsp;\(Y\),&nbsp;\(f^{-1}\)\((U)\) is open in&nbsp;\(X\)<br>Proof<br><ol><li><img src="paste-b57c500122d976a7a3880d42b553035060f33902.jpg"><br></li><li>Assuming openness holds show&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)-\(\delta\) holds</li><li>Fix&nbsp;\(p\)&nbsp;\(\in\)&nbsp;\(X\)</li><li>Let&nbsp;\(B\) =&nbsp;\(\epsilon\) -ball around&nbsp;\(f(p)\) for any&nbsp;\(\epsilon\)</li><li>Then&nbsp;\(p\)\(\in\)&nbsp;\(f^{-1}(B)\), which is {{c5::open}} by assumption</li><li>Since&nbsp;\(p\) is an {{c5::interior}} point of \(f^{-1}(B)\),&nbsp;\(\exists\) {{c1::some ball&nbsp;\(N_{\delta}(p)\)}}&nbsp;\(\in\)&nbsp;{{c2::\(f^{-1}(B)\)}}</li><li>This {{c1::\(\delta\)}} has the required property since {{c1::\(N_{\delta}(p)\)&nbsp;}}\(\in\)&nbsp;{{c2::\(f^{-1}(B)\)}} means that&nbsp;{{c3::\(f\) (}} {{c1::\(N_{\delta}(p) \)}} {{c3::)}} lies {{c4::completely within&nbsp;\(B\)}}</li></ol>

============================================================

  

    For&nbsp;\(f,g\) {{c3::continous}},&nbsp;\(f:\) {{c4::\(X \to&nbsp; Y\)}} and&nbsp;\(g:\) {{c5::\(Y \to Z\)}},&nbsp;{{c2::\(g \circ f\)}} is {{c1::continous}}

============================================================

  

    Proof that the composition of continous functions \(f: X \to Y\) and \(g: Y \to Z\)is continous:<br><ol><li>Given&nbsp;\(U\) open in&nbsp;\(Z\),&nbsp;{{c3::\(g^{-1}(U)\)}} is {{c4::open in&nbsp;\(Y\)}} by&nbsp;{{c4::\(g\) continous}}</li><li>{{c5::\(f^{-1}(g^{-1}(U))\)}} is {{c4::open in&nbsp;\(X\)}} by&nbsp;{{c5::\(f\) continous}}<br></li><li>{{c1::\((g \circ f)^{-1}\)}} = {{c2::\(f^{-1}\) \(\circ\) \(g^{-1}\)}}, thus&nbsp;\(g \circ f \) is continous<br></li></ol>

============================================================

  

    <ol><li>\(f:\)\(X\)&nbsp;\(\to\)\(Y\) is {{c4::continous}} iff&nbsp;\(\forall\)&nbsp;\(K\) {{c1::closed}} in&nbsp;\(Y\),&nbsp;{{c5::{{c3::\(f^{-1}\)\((K)\)}}}} is {{c5::{{c2::closed}}}} in&nbsp;\(X\)</li></ol>

============================================================

  

    <ol><li>\(f:\)\(X\)&nbsp;\(\to\)\(Y\) is continous iff&nbsp;\(\forall\)&nbsp;\(K\) closed in&nbsp;\(Y\),&nbsp;\(f^{-1}\)\((K)\) is closed in&nbsp;\(X\)</li></ol><div>Proof Idea:</div><div><ol><li>\(f^{-1}\)&nbsp;\((K)\) =&nbsp;{{c1::\( (\)}} {{c5::\(f^{-1}\)}} {{c2::\((K^c)\)}} {{c1::\()^c\)}}<br></li><li>{{c2::\(K^c\)}} is {{c3::open}}</li><li>If&nbsp;\(f\) is continous, then {{c5::\(f^{-1}\)}} {{c2::\((K^c)\)}}, the {{c5::inverse image}} of an {{c3::open}} set, is {{c3::open}}</li><li>Then {{c1::\( (\)}} {{c5::\(f^{-1}\)}} {{c2::\((K^c)\)}} {{c1::\()^c\)}}&nbsp;is {{c4::closed}}</li></ol></div>

============================================================

  

    <div>{{c5::{{c3::Continous}}}} functions preserve {{c4::{{c2::limits}}}} and they also preserve the {{c4::{{c1::compactness}}}} of of a set</div>

============================================================

  

    If \(f\)\(:\)&nbsp;\(X \to Y\) is {{c4::continous}} and&nbsp;\(X\) is {{c1::compact}}, then {{c5::{{c3::\(f\)\((X)\)}}}} is {{c5::{{c2::compact}}}}

============================================================

  

    If \(f\)\(:\)&nbsp;\(X \to Y\)&nbsp; is continous and&nbsp;\(X\) is compact, then \(f\)\((X)\) is compact<br>Proof:<br><ol><li><img src="paste-32b3afdf6a4484ef3682b844aaccc464a0bd7d00.jpg"><br></li><li><img src="paste-2af4e81a8e880a093b3953d22c1f49253b941f45.jpg"><br></li><li><img src="paste-d2d37bad37972d5ce806f2c51a5c94f523e8beb0.jpg"><br></li><li>Take an arbitrary cover of&nbsp;\(f\)\((X)\),&nbsp;\(\{V_{\alpha}\}\)</li><li>Since&nbsp;\(f\) is continous,&nbsp;\(\{U_{\alpha}\}\) =&nbsp;{{c1::\(\{f^{-1}(V_{\alpha})\}\)}} are {{c2::open}} sets</li><li>By {{c4::compactness}} of \(X\), \(\{U_{\alpha}\}\) has a {{c3::finite subcover&nbsp;\(U_{1},\ldots,U_{n}\)}}</li><li>The {{c4::corresponding&nbsp;\(V_{1},\ldots,V_n\)}} cover&nbsp;{{c5::\(f(X)\)}}, meaning&nbsp;{{c5::\(f(X)\)}} is compact</li><li>Which holds because anything in the inverse image must map to something in&nbsp;\(f(X)\), there is no additional point in&nbsp;\(f(X)\) not originating from the inverse image</li></ol>

============================================================

  

    For a {{c1::continous}} function it is possible to map {{c2::open}} intervals to {{c5::all of&nbsp;\(R\)}} but not {{c3::closed}} intervals, since {{c4::the endpoints must go somewhere}}

============================================================

  

    For a {{c5::continous}} function from a {{c3::compact}} set to&nbsp;\(R^k\) &nbsp;\(f\)\(:\)\(X_{\mathrm{cpt}}\)&nbsp;\(\to\)&nbsp;\(R^k\), the {{c5::image&nbsp;\(f(X_{\mathrm{cpt} })\)}} must be {{c1::closed}} and {{c2::bounded}} because it must be {{c3::compact}}. In&nbsp;\(R^k\) {{c3::compact}} sets are {{c1::closed}} and {{c2::bounded}} by {{c4::Heine Borel}} theorem

============================================================

  

    If a {{c4::continous}} function \(f\)\(:\)&nbsp;\(X\)&nbsp;\(\to\)&nbsp;{{c5::\(R\)}} is defined on \(X\) {{c3::compact}}, then&nbsp;\(f\) achieves its {{c1::maximum}} and {{c2::minimum}}

============================================================

  

    If a continous function \(f\)\(:\)&nbsp;\(X\)&nbsp;\(\to\)&nbsp;\(R\) is defined \(X\) compact, then&nbsp;\(f\) achieves its maximum and minimum.&nbsp;<br><ol><li>This is because&nbsp;{{c5::\(f(X)\)}} must be {{c2::closed}} and {{c3::bounded}}</li><li>The image being {{c3::bounded}} means an&nbsp;{{c4::\(\inf,\sup\)}} exist</li><li>Since&nbsp;{{c5::\(f(X)\)}} is {{c2::closed}} it must {{c1::contain}} its&nbsp;{{c4::\(\inf,\sup\)&nbsp;}}</li></ol>

============================================================

  

    If a {{c3::continous}} {{c4::bijection}}&nbsp;\(f\)\(:\)&nbsp;\(X\)\(\to\)\(Y\) exists over \(X\) {{c2::compact}}, then&nbsp;{{c1::\(f^{-1}\)}} is {{c5::also continous}}, which does not hold in general over non-{{c2::compact}} sets

============================================================

  

    <ol><li>Proof inverses for continous bijections are continous when the domain \(X\) is compact<br></li><li>Suppose a set&nbsp;\(U\) is open, then&nbsp;\(U^c\) is closed</li><li>Since&nbsp;\(U^c\) is closed in the compact set&nbsp;\(X\), then&nbsp;\(U^c\) is compact</li><li>Which then means&nbsp;{{c1::\(f\)\((U^c)\)}} is {{c2::compact}}</li><li>Which means&nbsp;&nbsp;{{c1::\(f\)\((U^c)\)}} is {{c3::closed}}</li><li>Which means&nbsp;&nbsp;{{c4::\(f\)\((U)\)}} is {{c5::open}}</li><li>Showing that&nbsp;\(f^{-1}\) is continous since&nbsp;{{c4::\(f\)}} is the {{c4::pre-image}} of&nbsp;\(f^{-1}\)</li></ol>

============================================================

  

    <div>A {{c3::bijection}} is sometimes called a {{c2::homeomorphism}}, meaning that its {{c5::{{c4::domain}}}} and {{c5::{{c4::co-domain}}}} are {{c5::{{c1::topologically}}}} equivalent</div>

============================================================

  

    A function&nbsp;\(f\)\(:\)\(X\)\(\to\)\(Y\), where&nbsp;\(X,Y\) are metric spaces, is {{c1::uniformly}} continous on&nbsp;\(X\):<br><ol><li>&nbsp;iff&nbsp;\(\forall\)\(\epsilon\)\(&gt;\)\(0\),&nbsp;\(\exists\)\(\delta\)\(&gt;\)\(0\) such that</li><li>{{c1::\(\forall\)}}{{c5::&nbsp;\(x\)\(\in\)\(X\)}} and&nbsp;{{c1::\(\forall\)}}{{c5::\(p\)\(\in\)\(X\)}}</li><ol><li>&nbsp;if{{c4::&nbsp;\(d\)}}{{c3::\((x,p)\)}}&nbsp;{{c2::\(&lt;\)&nbsp;\(\delta\)}}&nbsp;\(\implies\){{c4::&nbsp;\(d\)}}{{c2::\((f(x),f(p))\)}}{{c3::\(&lt;\)\(\epsilon\)&nbsp;}}</li></ol></ol>

============================================================

  

    What is the difference between:<br><ol><li>{{c4::Continous everywhere}}: {{c3::continous at every point}} with&nbsp; {{c1::a potentially custom&nbsp;\(\delta\) per point}}</li><li>{{c5::Uniformly continous}}: {{c3::continous at every point}} with {{c2::the same delta&nbsp;}}</li></ol>

============================================================

  

    A function \(f\)&nbsp;\(:\)\(X\)\(\to\)\(Y\) defined over \(X\) {{c5::{{c3::compact}}}}, is {{c4::{{c1::uniformly}}}} {{c4::{{c2::continous}}}}

============================================================

  

    What is the lebesque covering lemma?<br><ol><li>If&nbsp;\(\{U_\alpha\}\) is an open cover of a {{c5::compact}} metric space&nbsp;\(X\)</li><li>Then \(\exists\)\(\delta\)\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(0\) such that&nbsp;{{c1::\(\forall\)}}\(x\)\(\in\)\(X\) the {{c2::ball&nbsp;\(B_\delta(x)\)}} is {{c3::contained in some&nbsp;\(U_\alpha\)}}</li><li>This&nbsp;\(\delta\) is known as the {{c4::lebesque number}}</li><li>Pictographically:</li><ol><li><img src="paste-7d08c303bff2dd4cc0f0e6314e440164428fe3bc.jpg"></li></ol></ol>

============================================================

  

    <ol><li>If&nbsp;\(\{U_\alpha\}\) is an open cover of a compact metric space&nbsp;\(X\)</li><li>Then \(\exists\)\(\delta\)\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(0\) such that&nbsp;\(\forall\)\(x\)\(\in\)\(X\) the ball&nbsp;\(B_\delta(x)\) is contained in some&nbsp;\(U_\alpha\)</li><li>This&nbsp;\(\delta\) is known as the lebesque number</li><li>Pictographically:</li><ol><li><img src="paste-7d08c303bff2dd4cc0f0e6314e440164428fe3bc.jpg"></li></ol></ol><div>Proof:</div><div><ol><li>The open cover has a finite subcover&nbsp;\(\{U_{\alpha_{i}}\}\) by compactnes</li><li>The complements are all closed</li><li>For a closed set&nbsp;\(K\) we can define a distance to a point by \(d_c\)\((x,K)\) ={{c1::&nbsp;\(\inf\{ d(x,y): y \in K\}\)}}</li><li>This&nbsp;\(d_c\) is a {{c5::continous}} function of&nbsp;\(x\) for fixed&nbsp;\(K\)</li><li>Define</li><ol><li>\(f\)\((x)\) =&nbsp;{{c2::\(\frac{1}{n}\)}} {{c3::\(\sum_{i=1}^n\)\(d_c\)\((x,U_{\alpha_i}^c)\)&nbsp;}}</li><li>Which is a {{c5::continous}} function on a compact set</li><li>Which means it attains its maximum and minimum</li></ol><li>Let&nbsp;\(\delta\) =&nbsp;{{c4::\(\min \)\(f(x)\)}}</li><li>Since&nbsp;\(f\)\((x)\)&nbsp;{{c4::\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(\delta\)}}, at least one of {{c2::\(d_c\)\((x,U_{\alpha_i}^c)\)}}&nbsp;&nbsp;\(&gt;\)&nbsp;\(\delta\)</li><li>Which means&nbsp;\(B_{\delta}(x)\) is in the respective \(U_{\alpha_i}\) as it is sufficiently {{c5::far from the complement}}</li></ol></div>

============================================================

  

    <div>A function \(f\) \(:\)\(X\)\(\to\)\(Y\) defined over \(X\) compact,&nbsp; is uniformly continous&nbsp;</div><div>Proof:&nbsp;</div><div><ol><li>Given \(\epsilon\)\(&gt;0\) lets find a \(\delta\) which allows uniformity \(\forall\)\(p\)\(\in\)\(X\)&nbsp;</li><li>Every point \(p \in X\) has an \(\{N_{\delta_p}\}\) ball around it such that \(d(p,x)\)\(&lt;\)\(\delta_p\) implies \(d(f(p),f(x))\)\(&lt;\){{c5::\(\frac{\epsilon}{2}\)}}, these form a cover by definition&nbsp;</li><li>Since \(X\) is compact, this has a finite subcover \(N_{\delta_1},\ldots,N_{\delta_n}\)&nbsp;</li><li>By {{c1::lebesque covering}} lemma,&nbsp;\(\exists\)\(\delta\), the {{c1::lebeqsue}} number, such that if {{c4::\(d\) \((p,q)\)}}{{c3::\(&lt;\)\(\delta\)}} then&nbsp;\(p,q\) are {{c2::in the same element}} of {{c2::the finite subcover}}</li><li>Then&nbsp;{{c4::\(d\)\((p,q)\)}}&nbsp;\(\leq\){{c5::&nbsp;\(d\)\((p,x)\) +&nbsp;\(d\)\((x,q)\)&nbsp;}}\(\leq\){{c5::&nbsp;\(\frac{2\epsilon}{2}\)}} where&nbsp;{{c5::\(x\)}} is {{c5::the center of the neighbourhood}}</li><li>This {{c1::lebesque}} number is universal meaning the function is uniformly continous</li></ol></div>

============================================================

  

    For&nbsp;\(f\)\(:\)\(X\)\(\to\)\(Y\) {{c4::continous}}, if&nbsp;\(E\) is a {{c3::connected}} subset of&nbsp;\(X\), then&nbsp;{{c5::{{c2::\(f\)\((E)\)}}}} is {{c5::{{c1::connected}}}}

============================================================

  

    For&nbsp;\(f\)\(:\)\(X\)\(\to\)\(Y\) continous, if&nbsp;\(E\) is a connected subset of&nbsp;\(X\), then&nbsp;\(f\)\((E)\) is connected<br>Proof:<br><ol><li><img src="paste-27a6583491ec519801ab3467b3bce055c551a99a.jpg"><br></li><li>Suppose&nbsp;\(f\)\((E)\) is not connected, which means&nbsp;\(f\)\((E)\) =&nbsp;\(A\)\(\cup\)\(B\) where&nbsp;\(A,B\) are nonempty separated sets and&nbsp;\(\overline{A}\)\(\cap\)\(B\) = \(A\)\(\cap\)\(\overline{B}\) =&nbsp;\(\emptyset\)</li><li>Consider the closures&nbsp;\(\overline{A}\),&nbsp;\(\overline{B}\), which we know are closed</li><li>Take&nbsp;\(K_A\)&nbsp; =&nbsp;{{c1::\(f^{-1}\)}}{{c2::\((\overline{A})\)}} and&nbsp;\(K_B\) = {{c1::\(f^{-1}\)\}}{{c2::((\overline{B})\)}}, both of which are {{c5::{{c3::closed}}}} because&nbsp;\(f\) is {{c5::{{c4::continous}}}}</li><li>Then take \(E_1\)&nbsp; =&nbsp;\(f^{-1}\)\((A)\)&nbsp;\(\cap\)&nbsp;\(E\) and \(E_2\)&nbsp; =&nbsp;\(f^{-1}\)\((B)\)&nbsp;\(\cap\)&nbsp;\(E\), which are disjoint because&nbsp;\(A\) and&nbsp;\(B\) are disjoint and nonempty because&nbsp;\(A,B\) are nonempty</li><li>Notice&nbsp;\(E_1\)&nbsp;\(\subset\)&nbsp;\(K_a\) which is closed, because&nbsp;\(A\) is a subset of its closure</li><li>Same for&nbsp;\(E_2\)&nbsp;\(\subset\)&nbsp;\(K_b\)</li><li>Since the closure is the smallest closed set containing a given set</li><ol><li>\(\overline{E_1}\)\(\subset\)\(K_a\)<br></li><li>\(\overline{E_2}\)\(\subset\)\(K_b\)</li><li>Because&nbsp;\(K_a,K_b\) are closed and contain&nbsp;\(E_1,E_2\)</li></ol><li>Because&nbsp;\(\overline{A}\) is disjoint from&nbsp;\(B\) and&nbsp;\(E_2\)&nbsp;\(\subset\)\(f^{-1}(B)\) and&nbsp;\(K_2\) =&nbsp;\(f^{-1}(\overline{A})\)</li><ol><li>&nbsp;\(K_a\)\(\cap\)\(E_2\) =&nbsp;\(\emptyset\)</li></ol><li>Similary \(K_b\)\(\cap\)\(E_1\) =&nbsp;\(\emptyset\)</li><li>Which means&nbsp; \(\overline{E_1}\)\(\cap\)\(E_2\) =&nbsp;\(\emptyset\) and vice-versa meaning&nbsp;\(E\) is separatable giving a contradiction</li></ol>

============================================================

  

    For&nbsp;\(f\)\(:\)\(X\)\(\to\)\(Y\) continous, if&nbsp;\(E\) is a connected subset of&nbsp;\(X\), then&nbsp;\(f\)\((E)\) is connected<br>Proof:<br><ol><li><img src="paste-27a6583491ec519801ab3467b3bce055c551a99a.jpg"><br></li><li>Suppose&nbsp;\(f\)\((E)\) is not connected, which means&nbsp;\(f\)\((E)\) =&nbsp;\(A\)\(\cup\)\(B\) where&nbsp;\(A,B\) are nonempty separated sets and&nbsp;\(\overline{A}\)\(\cap\)\(B\) = \(A\)\(\cap\)\(\overline{B}\) =&nbsp;\(\emptyset\)</li><li>Consider the closures&nbsp;\(\overline{A}\),&nbsp;\(\overline{B}\), which we know are closed</li><li>Take&nbsp;\(K_A\)&nbsp; =&nbsp;\(f^{-1}\)\((\overline{A})\) and&nbsp;\(K_B\) = \(f^{-1}\)\((\overline{B})\), both of which are closed because&nbsp;\(f\) is continous</li><li>Then take \(E_1\)&nbsp; =&nbsp;{{c1::\(f^{-1}\)}}{{c2::\((A)\)}}&nbsp;{{c3::\(\cap\)&nbsp;\(E\)}} and \(E_2\)&nbsp; =&nbsp;{{c1::\(f^{-1}\)}}{{c2::\((B)\)}}&nbsp;{{c3::\(\cap\)&nbsp;\(E\)}}, which are {{c4::disjoint}} because&nbsp;\(A\) and&nbsp;\(B\) are {{c4::disjoint}} and {{c5::nonempty}} because&nbsp;\(A,B\) are {{c5::nonempty}}</li><li>Notice&nbsp;\(E_1\)&nbsp;\(\subset\)&nbsp;\(K_a\) which is closed, because&nbsp;\(A\) is a subset of its closure</li><li>Same for&nbsp;\(E_2\)&nbsp;\(\subset\)&nbsp;\(K_b\)</li><li>Since the closure is the smallest closed set containing a given set</li><ol><li>\(\overline{E_1}\)\(\subset\)\(K_a\)<br></li><li>\(\overline{E_2}\)\(\subset\)\(K_b\)</li><li>Because&nbsp;\(K_a,K_b\) are closed and contain&nbsp;\(E_1,E_2\)</li></ol><li>Because&nbsp;\(\overline{A}\) is disjoint from&nbsp;\(B\) and&nbsp;\(E_2\)&nbsp;\(\subset\)\(f^{-1}(B)\) and&nbsp;\(K_2\) =&nbsp;\(f^{-1}(\overline{A})\)</li><ol><li>&nbsp;\(K_a\)\(\cap\)\(E_2\) =&nbsp;\(\emptyset\)</li></ol><li>Similary \(K_b\)\(\cap\)\(E_1\) =&nbsp;\(\emptyset\)</li><li>Which means&nbsp; \(\overline{E_1}\)\(\cap\)\(E_2\) =&nbsp;\(\emptyset\) and vice-versa meaning&nbsp;\(E\) is separatable giving a contradiction</li></ol>

============================================================

  

    For&nbsp;\(f\)\(:\)\(X\)\(\to\)\(Y\) continous, if&nbsp;\(E\) is a connected subset of&nbsp;\(X\), then&nbsp;\(f\)\((E)\) is connected<br>Proof:<br><ol><li><img src="paste-27a6583491ec519801ab3467b3bce055c551a99a.jpg"><br></li><li>Suppose&nbsp;\(f\)\((E)\) is not connected, which means&nbsp;\(f\)\((E)\) =&nbsp;\(A\)\(\cup\)\(B\) where&nbsp;\(A,B\) are nonempty separated sets and&nbsp;\(\overline{A}\)\(\cap\)\(B\) = \(A\)\(\cap\)\(\overline{B}\) =&nbsp;\(\emptyset\)</li><li>Consider the closures&nbsp;\(\overline{A}\),&nbsp;\(\overline{B}\), which we know are closed</li><li>Take&nbsp;\(K_A\)&nbsp; =&nbsp;\(f^{-1}\)\((\overline{A})\) and&nbsp;\(K_B\) = \(f^{-1}\)\((\overline{B})\), both of which are closed because&nbsp;\(f\) is continous</li><li>Then take \(E_1\)&nbsp; =&nbsp;\(f^{-1}\)\((A)\)&nbsp;\(\cap\)&nbsp;\(E\) and \(E_2\)&nbsp; =&nbsp;\(f^{-1}\)\((B)\)&nbsp;\(\cap\)&nbsp;\(E\), which are disjoint because&nbsp;\(A\) and&nbsp;\(B\) are disjoint and nonempty because&nbsp;\(A,B\) are nonempty</li><li>Notice&nbsp;\(E_1\)&nbsp;\(\subset\)&nbsp;\(K_a\) which is closed, because&nbsp;\(A\) is a subset of its closure</li><li>Same for&nbsp;\(E_2\)&nbsp;\(\subset\)&nbsp;\(K_b\)</li><li>Since the closure is the {{c1::smallest closed set containing a given set}}</li><ol><li>\(\overline{E_1}\)\(\subset\)\(K_a\)<br></li><li>\(\overline{E_2}\)\(\subset\)\(K_b\)</li><li>Because&nbsp;\(K_a,K_b\) are closed and contain&nbsp;\(E_1,E_2\)</li></ol><li>Because&nbsp;\(\overline{A}\) is {{c2::disjoint}} from&nbsp;\(B\) and&nbsp;\(E_2\)&nbsp;\(\subset\)\(f^{-1}(B)\) and&nbsp;\(K_2\) =&nbsp;\(f^{-1}(\overline{A})\)</li><ol><li>{{c3::&nbsp;\(K_a\)}}{{c2::\(\cap\)}}{{c4::\(E_2\)}} =&nbsp;{{c5::\(\emptyset\)}}</li></ol><li>Similary {{c3::\(K_b\)}}{{c2::\(\cap\)}}{{c4::\(E_1\)}} =&nbsp;{{c5::\(\emptyset\)}}</li><li>Which means&nbsp; \(\overline{E_1}\)\(\cap\)\(E_2\) =&nbsp;\(\emptyset\) and vice-versa meaning&nbsp;\(E\) is separatable giving a contradiction</li></ol>

============================================================

  

    <ol><li>If&nbsp;\(f:\)\([a,b]\)\(\to\)\(R\) is {{c5::continous}} and&nbsp;{{c4::\(f(a)\)\(&lt;\)}}{{c2::\(c\)}}{{c3::\(&lt;\)\(f(b)\)}}</li><li>Then&nbsp;{{c3::\(\exists\)}}{{c4::\(x\)&nbsp;\(\in\)\(a,b\)}} such that&nbsp;{{c1::\(f(x)\)}} = {{c2::c}}</li></ol>

============================================================

  

    <ol><li>If&nbsp;\(f:\)\([a,b]\)\(\to\)\(R\) is continous and&nbsp;\(f(a)\)\(&lt;\)\(c\)\(&lt;\)\(f(b)\)</li><li>Then&nbsp;\(\exists\)\(x\)&nbsp;\(\in\)\(a,b\) such that&nbsp;\(f(x)\) = c</li></ol><div>Proof:</div><div><ol><li>\([a,b]\) is connected implies&nbsp;\(f([a,b])\) is connected</li><li>However, if&nbsp;\(c\)&nbsp;\(\neq\)&nbsp;\(f(x_c)\) for some&nbsp;\(x_c\) then we can use&nbsp;\(c\) to create {{c4::two separated sets}}</li><li>Specifically take&nbsp;\(E_1\) = {{c1::\((-\infty,c)\)}}{{c3::\(\cap\)\([f([a,b])\)}} and&nbsp;\(E_2\) = {{c2::\((c,\infty)\)}}{{c3::\(\cap\)\(f([a,b])\)}}, then&nbsp;\(E_1\) and&nbsp;\(E_2\) form {{c4::a separation}} by&nbsp;{{c4::\(f(x_c)\) only being able to be in one of the closures at a time}}</li><li>Since a {{c5::continous}} function applied to a {{c5::connected}} set must result in a {{c5::connected}} image, we have a contradiction</li></ol></div>

============================================================

    For a function&nbsp;\(f:\)\((a,b)\)&nbsp;\(\to\)&nbsp;\(R\)<br><ol><li>If&nbsp;\(\forall\) sequences \(\{t_n\}\) \(\in\) {{c1::\((x,b)\)}} with&nbsp;\(t_n\)&nbsp;\(\to\)&nbsp;\(x\),&nbsp;\(f\)\((t_n)\)&nbsp;\(\to\)&nbsp;\(q\), then&nbsp;{{c1::\(f(x^+)\)&nbsp;}}\(=\)&nbsp;\(q\) =&nbsp;{{c2::\(\lim_{t \to x^+}\)}} \(f(t) \) is the right-hand limit of&nbsp;\(f\)</li><li>Mutatis mulandis for left-hand limit&nbsp;&nbsp;{{c3::\(f(x^-)\)}}&nbsp;\(=\)&nbsp;\(p\) ={{c4::&nbsp;\(\lim_{t \to x^-}\)}} \(f(t) \)</li><li>If&nbsp;{{c5::\(\lim_{t \to x}\)}} exists then \(\lim_{t \to x}\) = {{c2::\(\lim_{t \to x^-}\)}} \(f(t) \) = {{c4::\(\lim_{t \to x^+}\)}} \(f(t) \)&nbsp;</li></ol>

============================================================

  

    When does a function over intervals have a discontinuity of the {{c1::first}} kind?<br><br><ol><li>{{c2::LHS}} and {{c3::RHS}} limits of a point {{c5::exist}} but they are {{c4::not equal}}</li></ol>

============================================================

  

    When does a function have a discontinuity of the second kind?<br><ol><li>When it is {{c1::not of the first kind}}, i.e it is {{c2::discontinous}} but it is {{c1::not the case}} that {{c3::LHS}} and {{34::RHS}} {{c4::limits of a point}} {{c5::exist but they are not equal}}</li></ol>

============================================================

  

    An example of a discontinuity of the {{c2::second kind}} for functions defined on intervals of the real line is when a {{c4::left}} or {{c4::right handed}} {{c5::limit of a point}} {{c3::does not exit}}<br><br>E.g topologists sine curve on the right and 0 on the other&nbsp;<br>{{c1::<img src="paste-7258708ebd14183e9676b46322748bd43b927757.jpg">}}

    For \(f\)\(:\)\((a,b)\)\(\to\)\(R\):<br><ol><li>\(f\) is {{c4::monotonically}} {{c5::increasing}} if&nbsp;{{c2::\(x\)}}&nbsp;{{c1::\(\leq\)}}{{c3::\(y\)}}&nbsp;\(\implies\)&nbsp;{{c3::\(f(x)\)}}{{c1::\(\leq\)}}{{c2::\(f(y)\)}}<br></li><li>Flip {{c2::ordering}} for {{c4::monotonically}} {{c5::decreasing}}</li></ol>
